**Summary.** The research presents an experimental framework to correct distortions in Time-Frequency (TF) quantum states of entangled photons caused by noisy channels. By using a stable TF grid state as a reference and applying Gaussian Process Regression, the authors learn a nonlinear correction map. This method successfully restores high fidelity to independent test states, establishing TF grid states as vital metrological resources for resilient quantum communication.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a robust, model-agnostic tool for quantum metrology and communication, directly addressing decoherence/channel noise in photonic systems by treating the distortion as a measurable coordinate transformation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is reliably characterizing Time-Frequency (TF) quantum states when their joint spectral intensities are distorted by imperfect transmission or measurement channels.

**Main result.** A data-driven correction framework using TF grid states successfully recovers the undistorted state, improving Gaussian fidelity from 76.2% to 90.0%.

**Method.** The authors use a Time-Frequency (TF) grid state as an intrinsic reference resource and employ Gaussian Process Regression (GPR) to learn a nonlinear mapping that corrects for channel-induced coordinate deformations.

**Model / system.** The experiment uses entangled photon pairs generated via SPDC, measured using a polarization-based Franson interferometer setup and Time-of-Flight Spectrometers (ToFS).

**Key observables.** Joint Spectral Intensities (JSI), residual coordinate deviation of the TF grid state, and Gaussian-shape fidelity ($F_G$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The correction reduces the residual coordinate deviation by a factor of 11; the test state fidelity improves from 76.2% to 90.0%.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method is powerful because it does not assume any prior physical model for the distortion, relying only on analyzing the displacement pattern of the reference grid points.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the end-to-end process: generating and distorting a TF grid state (Fig. 1), showing the Franson interferometer setup (Fig. 2), visualizing the JSI modulation into a lattice (Fig. 3), and demonstrating the reconstruction/correction mapping applied to test states (Figs. 8, 10).

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a structure of problem definition (distortion in TF characterization) -> methodology development (using grid state + GPR) -> experimental demonstration on an independent test state -> quantitative validation showing significant fidelity improvement.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Characterization of time-frequency (TF) quantum states requires reliable reconstruction of their TF distributions. However, imperfect transmission or measurement channels can distort reconstructed joint spectral intensities (JSIs), especially when the underlying perturbation mechanism is unknown. Here, we experimentally demonstrate a reconstruction and correction framework that uses a TF grid state as an intrinsic frequency-domain reference. By analyzing the displacement of the grid points, a Gaussian process regression model is employed to reconstruct a correction mapping for the nonlinear coordinate deformation without assuming a prior physical model of the distortion. The learned mapping reduces the residual coordinate deviation of the TF grid state by approximately a factor of 11 and, when applied to an independent frequency-entangled test state, improves the Gaussian-shape fidelity from 76.2\% to 90.0\%. These results establish TF grid states as practical metrological resources for diagnosing and correcting distortions in TF quantum systems, providing a pathway toward distortion-resilient quantum communication and high-dimensional quantum information processing.

</details>

### [Compressed minimum-purity time evolution for late-time quantum dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11392v1)

**Summary.** The paper introduces CoMPuTE, an advanced algorithm designed to simulate the long-time quantum dynamics of many-body systems by efficiently managing entanglement growth. It achieves this by using a minimum-purity principle based on Rényi-2 entropy to systematically truncate and reconstruct information within local density matrices. This allows for accurate study of transport phenomena in models like the Ising and XXZ chains, significantly improving computational feasibility over previous methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, scalable numerical tool for studying non-equilibrium quantum dynamics in 1D systems. The focus on systematic information management via purity diagnostics is highly relevant to understanding how complexity builds up and where approximations break down in strongly correlated systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is simulating unitary time evolution for quantum many-body systems at late times, which generates excessive entanglement, by systematically discarding irrelevant information while maintaining accuracy for physical observables.

**Main result.** The proposed CoMPuTE method significantly reduces computational complexity compared to prior methods (like LITE) by using Rényi-2 entropy, allowing accurate simulation of energy diffusion and out-of-equilibrium dynamics over extended time scales.

**Method.** CoMPuTE tracks reduced local density matrices ($ho_{\ell}^n$) by closing the hierarchical equations of motion using a minimum-purity principle based on Rényi-2 entropy. It employs an information-lattice approach with defined recovery and truncation steps to manage computational resources.

**Model / system.** The method is benchmarked on various quantum many-body systems, including the one-dimensional mixed-field Ising model for energy diffusion, driven Floquet spin chains, and the XXZ chain for transport studies. The dynamics are governed by standard unitary time evolution equations.

**Key observables.** Energy diffusion coefficient ($D_E(t)$), local reduced density matrices ($ho_{\ell}^n$), purity gain ($\gamma_\ell^n$), and the crossover time ($t_{	ext{cross}}$) for transport breakdown.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Truncation parameters ($\ell_{\min}, \ell_{\max}$), positive information thresholds ($q_{\ell^*}, q_{\max}$), and system-specific coupling constants (e.g., $\Delta$ in the XXZ chain).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that physical observables at late times can be accurately captured by tracking a consistent set of reduced local density matrices, even when discarding information via minimum-purity projection. A key limitation noted is that superdiffusion relies on increasingly non-local operators, eventually exceeding the method's truncation scale.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the time evolution of diffusion coefficients ($D_E(t)$) for the Ising model and XXZ chain, showing systematic convergence with increasing simulation depth ($\ell_{\min}$), alongside plots detailing purity gain diagnostics.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces CoMPuTE as an improvement over LITE by replacing von Neumann entropy with Rényi-2 entropy to achieve $O(d^{2\ell})$ scaling. It details the hierarchical update, information removal/recovery steps using minimum-purity projection, and validates this framework across multiple physical benchmarks (Ising, Floquet, XXZ).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Unitary time evolution of initially simple quantum many-body states rapidly generates entanglement and complex correlations, which limits direct numerical simulations. The late-time dynamics of physical observables, however, typically exhibits an effective simplicity in the form of hydrodynamics or kinetic theory. This leads to the question whether microscopic equations of motion can remain accurate and tractable up to long time scales by discarding irrelevant information in a controlled manner. Here, we introduce compressed minimum-purity time evolution (CoMPuTE) as an approach to keep track of a consistent set of reduced local density matrices, closing the hierarchical equations of motion using a minimum-purity principle. In benchmark applications we demonstrate (i) accurate description of energy diffusion in the one-dimensional mixed-field Ising model, (ii) the applicability to genuinely out-of-equilibrium Floquet dynamics starting from a pure state, and (iii) the limitations of the local reduced density matrix approximation when describing transport in the XXZ chain at $Δ=1$ that is governed by increasingly non-local integrals of motion. The CoMPuTE method enhances computational efficiency in comparison to the closely related local-information time evolution algorithm, opening a possible route towards an extension to systems in higher spatial dimensions.

</details>

### [Fast Adiabatic Quantum Gates via Hyperfine Intermediate States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11655v1)

**Summary.** This paper proposes an advanced protocol for realizing fast, high-fidelity CNOT gates in Rydberg-atom systems by ingeniously incorporating hyperfine intermediate states. By optimizing pulse sequences across two distinct dynamical pathways (STAY and TRANSFER), the authors significantly accelerate the gate operation time while maintaining fidelity above 0.9991. This represents a practical advancement toward scalable quantum computation using neutral atoms.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the practical limitations of quantum simulation by providing a concrete mechanism (HIS utilization) to reconcile the conflict between gate speed and adiabatic robustness in atomic platforms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is accelerating adiabatic quantum gate operations while maintaining high fidelity, as standard protocols are inherently too slow.

**Main result.** The proposed protocol achieves fast and robust CNOT gates with fidelities exceeding 0.9991 in a short time frame ($\sim 0.39 \mu s$).

**Method.** The method involves utilizing hyperfine intermediate states (HISs) within an EIT-based scheme to simultaneously enhance the energy gap (STAY pathway) and accelerate population transfer (TRANSFER pathway).

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using Rydberg-atom platforms, involving control and target qubits coupled via electromagnetically induced transparency (EIT) pathways that incorporate multiple HISs. The dynamics are governed by non-Hermitian Hamiltonians to account for decay.

**Key observables.** Gate fidelity ($F_0$, $F_s$, $F_t$), gate time ($	au$), and the energy gap between dark and bright states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The efficiency depends critically on satisfying a 'k-factor' condition ($k_r = k_{0,1}$), and performance is tested against varying intermediate decay rates ($\gamma_p$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Adiabatic elimination of highly detuned HISs is assumed, and the protocol is optimized using pulse shaping techniques (e.g., Genetic Algorithm).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate energy level diagrams showing STAY and TRANSFER pathways, and compare gate performance under ideal vs. non-ideal conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of slow adiabatic gates, details the Hamiltonian structure involving HISs for both STAY and TRANSFER dynamics, presents the optimization procedure using pulse shaping, and concludes with quantitative results demonstrating speedup and fidelity enhancement.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The appeal of adiabatic quantum computing lies in its intrinsic robustness against various technical imperfections, making it attractive for many quantum information applications. However, it faces a fundamental challenge: accelerating the adiabatic operations while preserving adiabaticity within the qubit coherence time. In this article, we propose an electromagnetically induced transparency-based adiabatic CNOT gate protocol which harnesses atomic hyperfine intermediate states (HISs) to speed up the adiabatic evolution. The HISs, naturally-existed in two-photon transitions, often need to be suppressed due to their significant decay errors. In contrast, this paper introduces a novel method that utilizes appropriately chosen HISs not only to enhance the adiabaticity in STAY pathway but also to accelerate the population transfer in TRANSFER pathway. Through pulse optimization, we achieve adiabatic gate fidelities exceeding 0.9991 within 0.3903 μs in realistic Cs atomic setups. To demonstrate the generality of protocol we further assess the impact of decays from multiple HIS and extend our model to arbitrary number of states, providing a practical route toward fast and robust adiabatic quantum gates in Rydberg-atom platforms.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11475"></a>
### [Linear Combination of Hamiltonian Simulation with Commutator Scaling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11475v1)

**Authors:** Junaid Aftab, Dong An, Konstantina Trivisa

**Summary.** The paper presents an advanced quantum algorithm combining Linear Combination of Hamiltonian Simulation (LCHS) with Multi-Product Formulas (MPFs) to simulate non-unitary dynamics. It rigorously analyzes the total simulation error and complexity, demonstrating that incorporating commutator scaling provides tighter and more accurate resource estimates than previous norm-based methods. This is crucial for designing efficient quantum hardware implementations for open quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a sophisticated theoretical framework for quantum simulation that moves beyond simple norm-based complexity estimates. By explicitly incorporating commutator scaling, it offers potentially superior algorithmic guarantees for simulating dissipative or open quantum systems relevant to AMO physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient quantum algorithm for simulating general non-unitary dynamics while achieving near-optimal scaling in both time and precision, specifically by incorporating commutator sensitivity.

**Main result.** The authors develop the LCHS-MPF framework, showing that complexity bounds explicitly reflect both quadrature structure and commutator scaling, leading to improved convergence rates over norm-based methods.

**Method.** The simulation uses Linear Combination of Hamiltonian Simulation (LCHS) combined with Multi-Product Formulas (MPFs) to approximate time evolution integrals. The analysis rigorously quantifies the error based on quadrature rules and commutator structures.

**Model / system.** The focus is on simulating linear differential equations, $\frac{d}{dt}u(t) = -A(t)u(t) + b(t)$, which models open quantum systems dynamics. The underlying mathematical structure involves approximating integrals over unitary operators using quadrature and MPFs.

**Key observables.** Overall simulation error ($\|u(T) - u_{	ext{MPF}Q,P}(T)\|$), block-encoding complexity, and the amplitude amplification factor $\chi_{Q,P}$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quadrature radius ($R_Q$), LCU normalization factor ($\alpha_Q$), commutator scale ($\mu_Q$), total simulation time ($T$), and target accuracy ($\epsilon$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is highly dependent on the choice of quadrature rule, the existence of favorable commutator scaling in the Hamiltonian family, and the ability to implement controlled second-order product-formula queries.

**Figures summary.** Not specified; the notes refer to tables comparing LCHS variants and equations detailing error bounds (e.g., Eq. 3.5, D.39).

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from general simulation frameworks (LCU/MPF) to specific techniques for non-unitary dynamics (LCHS). It systematically analyzes the total error by decomposing it into quadrature errors ($E^{	ext{quad}}$), time discretization errors ($E_{	ext{time}}$), and inner MPF approximation errors, culminating in complexity bounds.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Linear Combination of Hamiltonian Simulation (LCHS) framework simulates dissipative linear dynamics by representing time evolution as an integral over unitary operators, which is discretized by quadrature and implemented via Hamiltonian simulation. While existing analyses achieve near-optimal scaling in time and precision using norm-based quantities of the dissipative generator, we show that implementing the Hamiltonian simulation steps with Multi-Product Formulas (MPFs) yields commutator-sensitive error and complexity bounds. We demonstrate that the quadrature rule affects not only discretization error but also commutator structure and query complexity. This dependence is quantified through post-quadrature analysis for abstract MPF error profiles and for general time-independent and local Hamiltonians using known commutator-sensitive MPF error estimates. We compare uniform trapezoidal and free-scale sinh--sinh quadrature, showing improved quadrature-cardinality scaling for the latter, and illustrate the framework with applications to fractional diffusion, advection--diffusion, and open quantum systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.11731"></a>
### [Quantum Correlation Hierarchy and Teleportation in Dephased Hydrogen Hyperfine System](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11731v1)

**Authors:** Geerthana Thiyagarajan, R. Muthuganesan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11731v1>

**Summary.** The paper analyzes how various measures of quantum correlation degrade in a dephasing environment using the hydrogen hyperfine spin system. It establishes that while entanglement decays rapidly (sudden death), other discord-like measures persist longer due to population correlations. Crucially, it shows that for standard teleportation protocols, only the initial presence of entanglement dictates whether an advantage over classical communication is possible.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed theoretical roadmap for experimentally verifying the persistence of quantum correlations beyond entanglement decay, which is crucial for understanding resource theories in noisy environments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how different measures of quantum correlation (entanglement, discord-like measures, steering coherence) decay relative to each other when a two-qubit system undergoes local dephasing noise.

**Main result.** A strict hierarchy $C(t) \le N_1(t) \le 	ext{ASC}(t)$ is established, showing that entanglement ($C$) decays fastest (sudden death), while discord-like measures ($N_1$ and ASC) persist longer due to population correlations.

**Method.** The authors derived the exact time-dependent density matrix for an X-state family under a Lindblad master equation describing local dephasing, allowing for closed-form computation of correlation functions.

**Model / system.** The system is the hydrogen hyperfine spin pair (electron and proton), modeled as an open two-qubit system governed by an isotropic hyperfine Hamiltonian and subject to Markovian phase noise (local dephasing).

**Key observables.** Concurrence ($C$), Trace MIN–N1 ($	ext{Trace MIN-}N_1$), Average Steering Coherence (ASC), and Quantum Teleportation Fidelity ($F_A$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dephasing strength ($\kappa$), time ($t$), initial state parameters ($b_1, b_2, b_3$, or purity $\epsilon$)

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the X-state family of two-qubit states and assumes local dephasing noise (Markovian process).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the time evolution confirming the $C \le N_1 \le 	ext{ASC}$ ordering, showing entanglement sudden death ($t^*$) followed by the freezing/saturation of $N_1$ and ASC at non-zero values.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically derives correlation measures for the X-state family under dephasing, establishes their strict hierarchy, analyzes the operational equivalence between teleportation advantage and entanglement survival ($F_A > 2/3 \iff C > 0$), and concludes by proposing a measurement scheme based on Pauli correlators.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the dynamics of quantum correlations in the hydrogen hyperfine spin system subject to Markovian phase noise. Treating the electron and proton spin degrees of freedom as an open two-qubit system governed by an isotropic hyperfine Hamiltonian and local dephasing, we obtain the exact time-dependent density matrix and derive analytical expressions for the full X-state family. We compute concurrence($C$), trace-distance measurement-induced nonlocality (Trace MIN--$\mathcal{N}_1$), and average steering coherence (ASC) in closed form and establish their strict ordering $ C(t)\leq \mathcal{N}_1(t)\leq \mathrm{ASC}(t) $ at all times. Entanglement is identified as the most fragile resource, undergoing sudden death at a finite time. Trace MIN exhibits dephasing-immune freezing for states with nonzero population imbalance, while ASC is the most robust quantity, persisting longest in every scenario studied.We additionally demonstrate that the dephased thermal hyperfine state serves as a resource for quantum teleportation, deriving a closed-form expression for the average fidelity and establishing that the teleportation advantage window coincides exactly with the entanglement survival interval, $\mathcal{F}_A > 2/3 \Longleftrightarrow \mathcal{C} > 0$, for the full X-state family with maximally mixed marginals. We identify four distinct dynamical regimes and map all three correlation measures onto directly measurable Pauli spin correlators, enabling experimental reconstruction of the full hierarchy without full state tomography.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12338"></a>
### [Entanglement generation between field modes mediated by a fluctuating conducting wall](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12338v1)

**Authors:** Luca Giovanni Cammarata, Tommaso Fazio, Roberto Passante, Lucia Rizzuto  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12338v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper models two field cavities separated by a movable, quantum mechanically fluctuating wall. By treating the system's dynamics using perturbation theory and calculating entanglement via negativity, the authors demonstrate that vacuum fluctuations of the wall generate significant entanglement between the fields in the two separate cavities. This highlights how mechanical degrees of freedom can mediate non-local quantum correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses quantum light-matter interaction in a cavity setting (cavity QED analogue), showing how mechanical fluctuations can act as an effective mediator for generating non-classical correlations between distant quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how quantum entanglement can be generated between field modes confined in two separate sub-cavities when these cavities are separated by a movable, fluctuating conducting wall.

**Main result.** Quantum position fluctuations of the movable wall generate measurable entanglement between the field modes in opposite sub-cavities, an effect absent if the wall were fixed.

**Method.** The analysis employs second-order perturbation theory to find the interacting ground state and calculates entanglement using the negativity measure on the reduced density operator by tracing out the wall's degrees of freedom.

**Model / system.** The system consists of two massless 1D scalar fields confined in sub-cavities separated by a movable conducting plate. The wall's mechanical motion is quantized via a harmonic potential, leading to an effective field-wall interaction Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Negativity (N), which quantifies the entanglement between the two field modes; total multi-mode negativity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass ($M$) and oscillation frequency ($\omega_0$) of the movable wall, sub-cavity lengths ($l_1, l_2$), and the frequencies of the considered field modes ($\omega_k, \omega_j$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is performed using perturbation theory up to second order in the interaction Hamiltonian; the use of negativity is necessary because the reduced state is generally mixed.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of entanglement (negativity) on the wall's equilibrium position, mode frequencies, and the number of modes considered, showing maximization under specific resonance conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the Hamiltonian describing the coupled system, derives the reduced density operator by tracing out the wall, calculates the negativity analytically for two modes, and then extends this analysis numerically to include multiple field modes while discussing parameter dependencies.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider a movable conducting plate of finite mass, between two fixed ones, whose mechanical degrees of freedom are treated quantum-mechanically and bound to its equilibrium position by a harmonic potential. The movable wall is thus subjected to quantum fluctuations of its position. This creates a system of two sub-cavities separated by the movable fluctuating plate, and two massless one-dimensional scalar fields, one in each sub-cavity. This system is described by an appropriate generalization of the Law Hamiltonian. The presence of the movable wall yields an effective plate-fields interaction, as well as an effective interaction between the field modes. We obtain, at the second order in perturbation theory, the ground state of the interacting system and the reduced density operator of the fields in each sub-cavity by tracing out the wall's degrees of freedom. We calculate the entanglement between two field modes, one in each cavity, by evaluating analytically the negativity; we then evaluate numerically also the total multimode negativity. Our results show that in both cases the fields in the two sub-cavities are entangled, in contrast to the case in which the wall is fixed in space. We discuss the amount of the field entanglement present as a function of relevant physical parameters of the system such as the mass and oscillation frequency of the movable wall, its distance from the fixed walls and the frequencies of the field modes considered.

</details>

**Summary.** This work theoretically analyzes how nonlinear interfaces roughen when subjected to colored active noise that breaks symmetry. It successfully derives a crossover diagram showing the transition between three distinct physical regimes: Larkin, anharmonic Larkin, and Edwards–Wilkinson scaling. The findings provide a unified framework for understanding finite-size effects in such non-equilibrium systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical interface dynamics, the concept of universality classes, non-equilibrium steady states, and crossovers driven by noise correlations are fundamental concepts applicable to many open quantum systems and non-equilibrium statistical mechanics problems in AMO physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study focuses on characterizing the roughening dynamics of active nonlinear interfaces driven by temporally correlated noise that breaks statistical tilt symmetry.

**Main result.** The system exhibits a unified description across three distinct self-affine scaling regimes: Larkin (LM), anharmonic Larkin (ALM), and Edwards–Wilkinson (EW).

**Method.** The analysis employs scaling arguments, the self-consistent Hartree approximation, and comparison with known asymptotic limits.

**Model / system.** The model describes an overdamped interface height h(x, t) governed by a nonlinear elastic energy functional and driven by an Ornstein–Uhlenbeck colored noise process. The dynamics are captured by a nonlinear partial differential equation.

**Key observables.** Structure factor $\langle|h_q|^2angle = S(q)$, roughness exponent ($\zeta$), and dynamical exponent ($z$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The crossover diagram depends on the normalized length scale $	ilde{\ell}$ and a dimensionless coupling constant $\Gamma_{2n}$, which controls the transition between regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations like scaling arguments and the Hartree approximation to derive analytical boundaries for transitions between universality classes.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the crossover diagram mapping the EW, LM, and ALM regimes using dimensionless parameters, with data collapsing onto universal master curves in numerical simulations.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the governing equation, analyzes the linear limit (LM to EW crossover), extends the analysis to include nonlinearity via the Hartree approximation, and finally presents a unified crossover diagram showing the interplay between all three regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the roughening of an interface with nonlinear elasticity driven by temporally correlated noise, which breaks statistical tilt symmetry. Using scaling arguments and a self-consistent Hartree approximation, we derive the crossover diagram and the steady-state structure factor. We identify three scaling regimes associated with the Larkin, anharmonic Larkin, and Edwards--Wilkinson universality classes, and obtain the crossover lengths separating them. Numerical simulations of large systems confirm the analytical predictions over the full parameter range. Our results provide a unified description of finite-size and crossover effects in a minimal nonlinear-elastic Ornstein--Uhlenbeck active interface.

</details>

### [Quantum repeater segment with free-space coupled co-trapped ions using telecom photon interference](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12313v1)

**Summary.** The research demonstrates the feasibility of building a quantum repeater segment using co-trapped $^{40}	ext{Ca}^+$ ions. By converting emitted photons to the telecom band and performing interference over long optical fibers, they successfully generated entangled Bell states with high fidelity. This work establishes trapped ions as a promising hardware platform for scalable quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses a core challenge in quantum networking—the distribution of entanglement over lossy channels—by demonstrating a robust hardware platform (trapped ions) compatible with existing telecom infrastructure.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to implement a quantum repeater segment capable of generating buffered entanglement between distant quantum memories, thereby enabling long-distance quantum communication.

**Main result.** The authors successfully generated an entangled Bell state with fidelity $\ge 68(8)\%$ using co-trapped $^{40}	ext{Ca}^+$ ions and demonstrated the feasibility of this platform for quantum networking.

**Method.** Entanglement is established by generating photons from two trapped ions, converting these photons to the telecom C band, transmitting them over optical fiber, and performing a photonic Bell State Measurement (BSM).

**Model / system.** The physical system utilizes co-trapped $^{40}	ext{Ca}^+$ ions in a Paul trap. The process involves Raman photon generation, quantum frequency conversion (QFC) to 1550 nm, and subsequent entanglement swapping via photonic interference.

**Key observables.** Entanglement fidelity ($\ge 68(8)\%$), Hong–Ou–Mandel (HOM) visibility ($>0.7$ for $T < 120 	ext{ ns}$), CHSH parameters ($2.42-2.49$), and parity oscillation amplitude ($A = 1.36(15)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fiber length of $440 	ext{ m}$, coincidence time windows $< 120 	ext{ ns}$, and the telecom C band ($\sim 1550 	ext{ nm}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The high fidelity relies on maintaining short coincidence windows for maximum HOM visibility, and future work requires integrating additional trapping nodes or cavities.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the setup schematic (Paul trap, QFCs, BSM), show HOM visibility vs. time window size $T$, and plot parity oscillations versus phase to verify entanglement.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the experimental realization of the quantum repeater segment protocol, covering photon generation, frequency conversion, long-distance transmission, Bell state measurement, and subsequent characterization using multiple diagnostic tools like HOM interference and parity measurements.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A quantum repeater segment is a basic building block of a quantum repeater, generating buffered entanglement of quantum memories to connect quantum repeater cells. It also enables the connection between quantum computers. In the implementation we present here, photons emitted from two co-trapped free-space coupled $^{40}$Ca$^+$ ions are converted to the telecom-C band and interfered after transmission over 440$\,$m of optical fiber (220$\,$m per arm), where a photonic Bell measurement is performed to create entanglement between the memories. With this scheme we generate an entangled $\left|Ψ^+\right\rangle$ Bell state with $\ge 68(8)\,$% fidelity, highlighting trapped $^{40}$Ca$^+$ ions as a promising quantum repeater hardware platform.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12307"></a>
### [Super-Link Fragility in Asymmetric W-Class States under Quantum Noise](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12307v1)

**Authors:** Sougata Bhattacharyya, Fatih Ozaydin, Sovik Roy  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12307v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.12307_figures/2606.12307_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Borromean Rings. Three mutually inter- locked rings in which no two rings are directly linked as a pair, yet the three together cannot be separated. This topology serves as an analogy for the Borromean mode of three-qubit entanglement: when any single qubit is measured and found in |0⟩, the residual two-qubit state of the remaining pair collapses to a fully separable prod- uct state with zero coherence (Cl1 = 0), mirroring how severing one Borromean ring causes the other two to fall entirely apart.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2: 3-Hopf Link. Three rings arranged so that removing any single ring still leaves the remaining two interlinked. This topology serves as an analogy for the Hopf mode of three-qubit entanglement: when any single qubit is measured and found in |1⟩, the residual two-qubit state retains non-zero coherence (Cl1 &gt; 0) and bipartite entanglement, mirroring the persistent linkage of the re- maining two Hopf rings.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

For the symmetric |W⟩state (red...</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.12307_figures/2606.12307_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Isosceles entanglement-network geom- etry of the asymmetric |W L 3 ⟩state. The vertex qubit A (top) acts as a balanced switch: it triggers the Hopf mode with probability 1</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.12307_figures/2606.12307_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Equilateral entanglement-network ge- ometry of the symmetric |W⟩state. Each vertex represents a qubit (A, B, C) and each edge represents a bipartite entanglement link. Because the single excita- tion is distributed uniformly, all three qubits play struc- turally identical roles. Every qubit acts as an unbiased probabilistic switch, triggering the Hopf mode (residual coherence Cl1 = 1.0) with probability 2 3 and the Bor- romean mode (residual separability, Cl1 = 0) with prob- ability 1 3. Consequently, tracing out any single qubit yields the same reduced density matrix, and all three bipartite concurrences are equal: CW = 2</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes the robustness of entanglement in asymmetric three-qubit W-states compared to symmetric ones when exposed to different types of quantum noise. It demonstrates that initial structural advantages, such as a strong 'super-link,' can be fragile and even reverse under specific decoherence channels like amplitude damping. The findings emphasize that entanglement resilience depends on the interplay between network geometry, excitation sector, and noise symmetry.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides crucial insights into how structural properties derived from ideal quantum states break down under realistic, non-Markovian noise environments, which is vital for designing robust quantum communication protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how the initial structural advantages of asymmetric W-class quantum states persist or degrade when subjected to various forms of quantum noise.

**Main result.** The initial entanglement hierarchy ($C_{VB} > C_W > C_{BB}$) is not guaranteed under decoherence; for instance, amplitude damping causes a reversal termed the Super-Link Fragility Effect.

**Method.** The authors analytically compare bipartite concurrence dynamics between symmetric and asymmetric W states using established noise models (phase, amplitude, depolarization).

**Model / system.** The system involves three-qubit entangled W-class states ($|Wangle$ vs. $|\overline{W_3^L}angle$), whose entanglement geometry is analyzed via bipartite concurrence links (Vertex-Base and Base-Base).

**Key observables.** Bipartite Concurrence ($C_{VB}, C_W, C_{BB}$), Entanglement Sudden Death (ESD) thresholds.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Noise parameters ($\gamma$ for amplitude damping, $p$ for depolarization, $\alpha$ for GADC).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on the assumption that reduced density matrices remain X-states to simplify concurrence calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate entanglement decay under different noise channels (e.g., Fig 7 shows hierarchy reversal under amplitude damping; Fig 8 shows ESD thresholds under depolarization).

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the initial geometric advantage, then systematically analyzes the robustness of this structure across four distinct noise models: phase damping (preserves hierarchy), amplitude damping (reverses hierarchy/Super-Link Fragility), depolarization (erases asymmetry), and GADC (interpolates between limits).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The asymmetric three-qubit W-class state $|\overline{W_3^L}\rangle$ defines an isosceles entanglement-network geometry, (a) two vertex-base (VB) links form stronger bipartite connections, (b) while the base-base (BB) link is weaker. This suggests that concentrating entanglement into a super-link may be advantageous for quantum-network tasks. Here, we show that this intuition is incomplete. We analytically compare the bipartite concurrence dynamics of the symmetric |W> state and the asymmetric $|\overline{W_3^L}\rangle$ state, which differ both in entanglement-network geometry and excitation sector under standard noise models. In the absence of noise, the concurrence hierarchy is C_{VB} > C_W > C_{BB}$. Under phase damping, this hierarchy is preserved for all noise strengths and no entanglement sudden death occurs. Under amplitude damping, however, the hierarchy is reordered. The symmetric |W> state becomes the most robust, while the base-base concurrence of $|\overline{W_3^L}\rangle$ vanishes at the finite threshold of parameter $γ$. We term this reordering as the \textit{Super-Link Fragility Effect}. The same structural asymmetry that produces a stronger vertex-base link also makes it more vulnerable to energy dissipation when coupled with multi-excitation amplitudes. Under depolarization, the asymmetry advantage is erased, with $C_W$ and $C_{VB}$ sharing the same sudden-death threshold for some value of the parameter p, while $C_{BB}$ disappears earlier at some other value of the parameter p. The generalized amplitude damping channel continuously connects the damping-dominated regime to the pure-excitation limit, where the initial hierarchy is restored. These results show that entanglement robustness in $W$-class resources is controlled not by initial concurrence alone, but by the joint structure of entanglement-network geometry, excitation sector, and noise symmetry.

</details>

### [Large Fluctuations in Open Quantum Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11822v1)

**Summary.** This paper investigates rare fluctuations in driven open quantum systems using advanced semiclassical methods. It demonstrates that unlike equilibrium states, these non-equilibrium steady states exhibit large-deviation functions with sharp, non-analytic features. These features arise fundamentally from the competition between multiple distinct instanton trajectories governing the system's dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides deep insights into non-equilibrium statistical mechanics by showing that quantum dissipation can induce complex, non-analytic structures in fluctuation statistics, extending concepts from classical instanton theory to the quantum domain.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study focuses on characterizing atypical measurement outcomes (rare fluctuations) in the steady states of driven, open quantum systems.

**Main result.** The large-deviation function for these systems is generically non-analytic due to the competition between multiple competing semiclassical instanton trajectories. This contrasts with equilibrium systems where analyticity holds.

**Method.** The analysis employs semiclassical methods derived from Keldysh path integrals and WKB approximations, relating rare events to instanton solutions of the effective action.

**Model / system.** The model is an open quantum system, exemplified by a parametrically driven Kerr oscillator coupled to a dissipative bath, governed by a Lindblad master equation.

**Key observables.** Large-deviation function $S(x, p)$ and the phase-slip rate $\Gamma$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Drive strength ($G$), detuning ($\Delta$), dissipation rates ($\kappa, \eta, \kappa_\phi$), and the semiclassical parameter $\hbar$ (or $\delta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the WKB approximation for the path integral and identifying an invariant subspace parameterized by $(\alpha(t), \chi(t))$ in the Keldysh formalism.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate real-time instanton trajectories spanning a fluctuation Riemann surface, showing multiple paths to endpoints. They also map out Stokes lines and anti-Stokes lines on phase space plots, visualizing where non-analyticities occur.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the general problem of large deviations in open systems to applying it to a specific driven oscillator model. It develops the semiclassical formalism using Keldysh theory, identifies the multi-valued nature of solutions (Riemann surface), and finally analyzes how Stokes phenomena dictate non-analytic features in the resulting large-deviation function.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study statistics of atypical measurement outcomes in the steady states of driven open quantum systems. In equilibrium, the probability distribution over the phase space, as encoded in, e.g., the Wigner function, is analytic in the phase-space coordinates. We show that this property is generically lost in driven dissipative systems: their {\it large-deviation function} develops lines and surfaces across which its derivatives are discontinuous. As an illustrative example, we consider a parametrically driven Kerr oscillator coupled linearly and/or nonlinearly to a dissipative bath. Rare fluctuations in the amplitude and phase of the induced oscillations are governed by semiclassical instanton trajectories of the corresponding Keldysh-Lindblad action. We demonstrate that a given fluctuation can be realized through multiple distinct instanton trajectories. The competition between these trajectories leads to abrupt switching of the dominant instanton and, consequently, to non-analytic features in the large-deviation function.

</details>

**Authors:** Tanay Pathak  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11311v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops an exact analytical method to calculate entanglement dynamics in Floquet spin models with finite-range interactions, moving beyond the limitations of nearest-neighbor dual-unitary systems. By constructing a staggered sublattice structure that preserves duality, the authors derive explicit formulas for $n$-Rényi entropies. This work provides deep theoretical insight into how interaction range controls entanglement growth rates in quantum many-body physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a controlled theoretical framework to study how increasing interaction range affects quantum information measures like entanglement growth rates in periodically driven many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to extend the study of exactly solvable entanglement dynamics beyond nearest-neighbor dual-unitary Floquet systems to incorporate finite-range interactions.

**Main result.** They derive exact closed-form expressions for $n$-Rényi entanglement entropies, showing that the interaction range acts as a tunable multiplier on the entanglement growth rate.

**Method.** The analysis utilizes space-time duality and the replica trick to evaluate moments of the reduced density matrix ($	ext{tr}[(ho_X(t))^n]$) for structured spin chains under Floquet evolution.

**Model / system.** The system is a spin-1/2 chain governed by a Floquet unitary operator incorporating range-$r$ interactions, which are analytically tractable by structuring the lattice into coupled sublattices that maintain dual-unitarity.

**Key observables.** $n$-Rényi entanglement entropies ($S_\alpha^X(t)$), spectral properties (level spacing distribution, spectral form factor).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction range $r$, time $t$, and the replica parameter $\alpha$ (or $n$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The construction relies on grouping sites into enlarged unit cells to preserve dual-unitarity despite inter-sublattice couplings, and specific solvable initial states are analyzed.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematic constructions of the $r=2$ model (showing coupled sublattices) and numerical plots showing the time evolution of Rényi entropy for various initial conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the formalism by defining the Hamiltonian structure, applying space-time duality to find exact formulas for density matrix moments using the replica trick, and finally deriving explicit expressions for entanglement entropy in terms of these calculated traces.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Exact results using dual-unitarity largely rely on nearest-neighbor structures, while finite-range interactions typically lead to complications. Going beyond the usual nearest-neighbor setting, we introduce an analytically tractable family of finite-range kicked Ising models that admit exact closed-form entanglement dynamics. The construction is based on a staggered structure in which dual-unitarity is present on sublattices that are then coupled to each other. The central observation is that these inter-sublattice couplings do not obstruct the dual-unitarity of the resulting model. For the minimal interaction range of $r= 2$, we derive exact expressions for all the $n-$Rényi entanglement entropies at all times and show that the result is the sum of the two coupled sublattice contributions. Our framework extends naturally to larger finite interaction ranges and to systems with heterogeneous local Hilbert spaces, without additional assumptions. It thus provides a controlled setting for studying exact entanglement growth beyond strictly nearest-neighbor dual-unitary models.

</details>

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper rigorously compares different theoretical approaches—mean-field, Quantum Kinetic Equation, and full many-body Hamiltonian—for modeling neutrino flavor oscillations in dense astrophysical matter. It demonstrates that non-forward scattering effects are crucial, leading to significant deviations from simpler models. Furthermore, it quantifies the quantum computational resources required for these complex simulations by analyzing entanglement growth and Trotter error scaling.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly bridges high-energy astrophysics (neutrino oscillations) with cutting-edge quantum computation theory by quantifying the necessary resources to simulate complex interacting quantum systems beyond mean-field approximations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study compares neutrino evolution using mean-field approximations against full many-body treatments, focusing on non-forward effects and quantifying the resulting quantum non-classicality.

**Main result.** Full many-body calculations show significantly higher entanglement entropy compared to truncated or mean-field results, indicating that multi-body correlations are crucial for accurate dynamics. The resource estimation suggests these simulations require substantial computational resources related to gate complexity.

**Method.** The authors employ both solving differential equations (QKE/Mean-Field) and estimating quantum circuit requirements (Trotter error scaling, gate costs) for the full Hamiltonian evolution.

**Model / system.** The system is a neutrino gas undergoing collective flavor oscillations in dense astrophysical environments. The dynamics are governed by a complex many-body Hamiltonian ($H = H_{	ext{vac}} + H_{
u
u}$), which includes non-forward scattering terms.

**Key observables.** Entanglement entropy ($S_{
u_e}(t)$), Rényi entropies ($ar{S}_
u$, $M_2(t)$), occupation numbers of momentum states, and the ratio of general to forward interaction modes ($N_{PS}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The comparison hinges on the relative importance of non-forward scattering terms versus those captured by mean-field approximations; resource costs are compared against quantum chemistry problems.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key assumptions include using a 'plane waves in a box' discretization for momentum space and employing first-order Trotter error bounds to estimate computational cost.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare time evolution of occupation numbers across Full, Truncated, and Mean-field Hamiltonians; entanglement entropy plots show deviations from mean-field predictions; and resource estimation figures detail scaling with system size N.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the mathematical framework for neutrino self-interactions in various dimensions (1D, 2D, 3D), compares QKE/Mean-Field results against full many-body simulations, and concludes by analyzing quantum computational resource requirements via Trotter error bounds.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Neutrino evolution in dense astrophysical environments is typically described either within a quantum kinetic framework, which neglects the build-up of multi-body correlations, or through simplified many-body calculations that allow significant entanglement to develop. In this work, we compare these two approaches in a simple neutrino-gas configuration, with particular emphasis on the role of non-forward scattering processes. These effects are incorporated either through a collision term in the kinetic description, or by considering the full neutrino-neutrino many-body Hamiltonian. We highlight differences between the two descriptions in both their characteristic timescales and asymptotic behavior. Motivated by the natural suitability of quantum computing for many-body calculations, we further investigate the non-classicality of neutrino evolution, discussing Trotter error scaling, along with the associated costs of constructing quantum circuits in terms of entangling gates and non-Clifford gates. We find that the resources needed for neutrino many-body evolution are on the low end of typical high-energy physics problems and on the mid to high end with respect to quantum chemistry problems. For the full Hamiltonian, resource requirements increase relative to the truncated version. We emphasize the importance of efficient fermion-to-qubit encodings, which are essential for reducing the substantial computational resources required for such simulations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11340"></a>
### [Q-DICE: Quantum Distributed Interconnect Compiler and Emulator](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11340v1)

**Authors:** Michael Silver, Zachary Vernec, Hans-Arno Jacobsen  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11340v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11340_figures/2606.11340_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

Graph nodes indicate qubits, with respective colors referring to single gate error rates (in this case for the Hadamard gate). Graph edges represent qubit-qubit connections, with respective colors referring to the error rate of a native two-qubit gate on these qubits. Side bars indicate single-qubit readout error rates.</sub>

We refer to these sparse, modular structures as “butterfly backends,” reflecting their uniquely shaped and low- connectivity modular topologies similar to near-term DQC architectures.</sub>

<img src="2606.11340_figures/2606.11340_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5. Gate teleportation circuit. The dashed box labeled “Nonlocal Link” encloses the entanglement generation and distribution subcircuit consumed by the teleportation. **Summary.** This paper introduces Q-DICE, a comprehensive emulator designed to benchmark distributed quantum computing algorithms by accurately simulating the physical limitations and noise sources inherent in connecting multiple QPUs. It achieves this through novel compiler techniques like QPU slicing/stitching and detailed modeling of nonlocal link errors. The work is crucial for accelerating hardware co-design by providing a reliable tool to test complex, large-scale quantum network protocols.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous incorporation of physical link noise models (Kraus operators) into a compiler framework is highly relevant for theorists designing fault-tolerant quantum networks, as it moves beyond idealized gate sets.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Benchmarking distributed quantum algorithms (DQC) is difficult because current tools cannot realistically simulate or emulate the physical constraints of connecting multiple Quantum Processing Units (QPUs).

**Main result.** The proposed Q-DICE emulator accurately models complex noise sources and topological constraints, achieving a low fidelity deviation when simulating established protocols like Grover's search on trapped-ion hardware.

**Method.** Q-DICE provides a compiler/emulator framework using programmatic techniques (slicing/stitching) to map circuits onto distributed backends while incorporating physically motivated noise models (Kraus operators).

**Model / system.** The system models Distributed Quantum Computing, connecting multiple QPUs via virtual nonlocal links. It supports modeling both Hamiltonian-mediated (superconducting) and entanglement-mediated (photonic/trapped-ion) link noise.

**Key observables.** Fidelity deviation between simulation and experiment; Graph state fidelity; Success rate of benchmark algorithms (e.g., Grover's search, Deutsch-Jozsa).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Link error probabilities ($p$), coherence times ($T_1, T_2$), link interaction times ($t_{	ext{link}}$), and the degree of topological constraint violation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Standard transpilers fail to respect virtual QPU boundaries; the algorithm uses a constrained random search which does not guarantee global optimality for layout selection.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate QPU topologies, circuit partitioning (slicing/stitching), and benchmark results comparing simulated fidelity across different noise models (e.g., Gate Teleportation fidelity vs. link error rate).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem gap in DQC benchmarking, details the three core components of Q-DICE (mapping scheme, nonlocal noise modeling, boundary-aware transpilation), validates it across multiple benchmark circuits (Grover's, GHZ, Deutsch-Jozsa), and concludes with advanced topology analysis (ring-of-rings).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

As distributed quantum computing (DQC) offers a leading path towards scalable quantum computation, the ability to benchmark distributed algorithms under realistic conditions becomes critical for system co-design. However, without access to physical systems, researchers lack tools to evaluate distribution protocols. We introduce Q-DICE (Quantum Distributed Interconnect Compiler and Emulator), a hardware-aware emulation environment for benchmarking distributed quantum circuits on classical simulators and on NISQ-era monolithic hardware. This work provides three core contributions: (1) a programmatic scheme to construct distributed QPU backends, utilizing two novel techniques - QPU slicing and stitching - to facilitate distributed circuit mapping, (2) a methodology for modeling nonlocal link noise using physically motivated Kraus operators and stochastic error channels, and (3) a boundary-aware circuit mapping algorithm enforcing distributed QPU topology constraints during transpilation. Together, these components constitute a distribution-aware compiler and noise-modeling engine that faithfully enforces the physical limitations of distributed quantum hardware within existing execution environments. We validate Q-DICE against a multitude of experimentally demonstrated quantum circuits, including a distributed Grover's search on optically linked trapped-ion hardware, achieving a worst-case fidelity deviation of 4% between simulated and experimental results. These findings demonstrate Q-DICE's capacity to accurately reproduce real distributed quantum system behavior across platforms, streamlining experimentation with distributed quantum algorithms and architectures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11579"></a>
### [Tensor-Network-Based Distributed Quantum Dynamics on Independent Quantum Computers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11579v1)

**Authors:** Anurag Dwivedi, Melissa C. Revelle, Daniel S. Lobser, Brian K. McFarland, Edward C. Tortorici, Christopher G. Yale, Susan M. Clark, Philip Richerme, Srinivasan S. Iyengar  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11579v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11579_figures/2606.11579_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: The essence of the distributed algorithm presented here which appears from a tensor net- work formalism. There is one set of parallel streams of computational tasks for each value of the pair of variables (α, β). Thus, there are [α × β × number of nuclear dimensions] parallel computing tasks, each executed here as a separate ion-trap com- putation, possible due to elevation of the entanglement variables. This yields an enormous computational ad- vantage as the number of dimensions increase. Future developments will involve a heterogeneous set of classi- cal and hardware systems all runing these parallel set of tasks.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.11579_figures/2606.11579_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Time evolution of a wavepacket in a tensor- network representation as given by Eq. (3). The ini- tial state is parameterized by entanglement variables ¯α, while the time-evolution propagator is characterized by ¯β.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.11579_figures/2606.11579_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: The circuit in Fig. 3 can be distributed, in principle, across four quantum computers, where each constituent sub-circuit can be executed simultaneously. In this paper, this is all done sequentially on one quan- tum computer using three qubits. The aggregation (the double vertical arrow at the bottom of the figure) is also done classically here through measurement, and the final state inherits the entanglement from the uni- tary above. As described in Section II C, such entangle- ment arises from the potential energy surface.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.11579_figures/2606.11579_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 6: (a) Uniformly controlled gate (UCG) representation of the block-diagonal operator shown in Fig. 5.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.11579_figures/2606.11579_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 7: The circuit in Fig. 6 can, in principle, be distributed across several quantum computers, and this figure shows the full-scale parallel generalization of our algorithm. The ion-traps are indexed here using the dimension (first index), and the two entanglement indices following the dimension. So for example, “QC-(2,1,1)” implies the specific quantum hardware system (trapped ions in our case) that computes the second dimension for entanglement variables β1 = β2 = 1. As is clear, the number of parallel streams (dotted boxes) is equal to P</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes using tensor networks to decompose the challenging task of simulating quantum wavepacket dynamics into many independent, smaller computational tasks. This decomposition allows for asynchronous execution across multiple, heterogeneous quantum computers, which was experimentally tested on a trapped-ion system to calculate vibrational spectra with high accuracy.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the scalability bottleneck in simulating complex molecular dynamics by providing a concrete algorithmic blueprint that maps entanglement structure onto parallel hardware resources, making it highly relevant to AMO/quantum optics simulation efforts.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Simulating complex, high-dimensional quantum dynamics (like molecular wavepacket evolution) efficiently on near-term, heterogeneous quantum hardware.

**Main result.** The authors developed a tensor-network based formalism that decomposes global unitary evolution into independent, parallelizable sub-tasks executable across distributed quantum processors.

**Method.** The core method involves mapping the time-evolution operator onto a block-diagonal structure within an enlarged Hilbert space, allowing for decomposition into multiple, smaller, and independently controlled quantum circuits (UCGs).

**Model / system.** The simulation is demonstrated on protonated water clusters, modeling vibrational spectra. The implementation utilizes trapped-ion quantum computers with native partial-entangling XX(theta) gates.

**Key observables.** Vibrational spectral properties and energy differences ($\omega$), calculated via the Fourier power spectrum of time-correlation functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The fidelity improvement is quantified by reducing expected two-qubit gate infidelity by over 30% compared to conventional decompositions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach assumes that for practical problems, the entanglement structure allows for a decomposition into efficiently manageable, parallelizable sub-circuits suitable for hybrid quantum/classical execution.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the distributed algorithm derived from tensor networks (Fig. 1), the mapping to block-diagonal UCG circuits (Figs. 3, 6), and the overall phase estimation structure for spectroscopy (Fig. 8).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the theoretical link between tensor network decomposition of the time-evolution operator ($\hat{U}$) and its representation as a block-diagonal unitary in an elevated space. This leads to a circuit implementation using UCGs, which is then generalized into a fully asynchronous, distributed quantum computing architecture suitable for calculating spectral functions via QPE.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present an approach based on tensor networks for distributed quantum computing simulation of chemical wavepacket dynamics in a continuous variable representation. The central idea is that the tensor-network representation of the multidimensional time-evolution operator naturally induces an elevated Hilbert space where the dynamics decomposes into a set of independent lower-dimensional propagations. This transformation converts an entangled quantum evolution into a set of parallel computational tasks that can be executed asynchronously across heterogeneous quantum and classical computing architectures. The resulting formalism establishes a direct connection between tensor-network decompositions, uniformly controlled quantum circuits, and asynchronous distributed quantum computing. The approach is developed with a goal towards hybrid quantum/classical implementation, and is appropriate for a general heterogeneous mixture of quantum hardware systems. The experimental realization of the asynchronously distributed quantum processes that arise from the tensor-network decomposition are carried out on the Sandia National Laboratories' trapped-ion quantum computer, where the circuits are compiled using native partial-entangling $XX(θ)$ gates, reducing the expected two-qubit gate infidelity by more than 30\% relative to conventional fully entangling decompositions. We demonstrate the methodology by quantum computing the vibrational spectra of a small protonated water cluster that shows critical quantum nuclear behavior. Such water cluster systems have been found to be challenging for experimental action spectroscopy and for theory, and here, for the first time, we provide results for vibrational spectroscopy that are in agreement with the respective classical results to within 4cm$^{-1}$, thus allowing for the potential for spectroscopic accuracy from quantum computations.

</details>

### [Grand-canonical phase diagram and chiral-current suppression at $π$ flux in a bosonic two-leg ladder](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12159v1)

**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper maps the phase diagram of interacting bosons on a two-leg ladder under an artificial magnetic flux using the Cluster Gutzwiller Mean-Field method. It characterizes various superfluid and Mott insulating states based on current measurements. The key finding is that at $\phi=\pi$, symmetry forces the system into a nonchiral Mott insulator, providing crucial theoretical guidance for experiments with synthetic gauge fields.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a theoretically rigorous and computationally efficient framework for mapping complex quantum phases (like those involving chirality) in synthetic gauge fields, directly relevant to ultracold atom experiments using optical lattices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the ground-state phase diagram of repulsively interacting bosons on a two-leg ladder threaded by an artificial magnetic flux, with a specific focus on the physics at the $\pi$-flux point.

**Main result.** At $\phi=\pi$, symmetry constraints enforce the vanishing of net chiral currents, leading to a nonchiral Mott-insulating state, which contrasts sharply with the expected chiral superfluidity away from this flux value.

**Method.** The authors employ the Cluster Gutzwiller Mean-Field (CGMF) method on a $2	imes4$ cluster to calculate ground-state properties and construct grand-canonical phase diagrams.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled as repulsively interacting bosons on a two-leg ladder subjected to a uniform artificial magnetic flux ($\phi$). The Hamiltonian includes tunneling terms (intra-leg $t$, inter-leg $K$) and interaction terms ($U$), alongside a chemical potential $\mu$.

**Key observables.** Superfluid order parameter, leg-resolved currents, chiral current, ratio of currents on adjacent legs, and density imbalance between the two legs.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Artificial magnetic flux ($\phi$), filling fraction ($ho$), interaction strength ($U/t$), and chemical potential ($\mu$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of the CGMF method is an approximation balancing computational efficiency with physical accuracy, and previous studies were limited to fixed-density calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase boundaries (e.g., M-SF, V-SF, BLP-SF, MI) across different flux values, showing how the ground state evolves as a function of flux and density.

**Paper structure.** The paper first analyzes the system's phases using CGMF diagnostics to distinguish between various superfluid/Mott regimes. It then constructs the grand-canonical $t-\mu$ phase diagrams, culminating in a detailed analysis of the singular $\phi=\pi$ point where chiral symmetry dictates the ground state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the ground-state phase diagram of repulsively interacting bosons on a two-leg ladder threaded by a uniform artificial magnetic flux, using the cluster Gutzwiller mean-field method. In the strong-rung-coupling regime, self-consistent calculations are performed on a $2\times4$ cluster. By analyzing the superfluid order parameter, leg-resolved currents, chiral current, the current ratio on adjacent legs, and the density imbalance between the two legs, we distinguish Mott-insulating from superfluid regimes and characterize the observed states as Meissner-like, vortex-like (superfluid or Mott insulating), or biased-ladder. In regions overlapping with previous DMRG studies, our results qualitatively agree with the established phase structure, demonstrating that the cluster Gutzwiller approach balances computational efficiency and physical accuracy. We then construct the first grand-canonical $t$--$μ$ phase diagrams for this system, revealing how the magnetic flux modifies the shape, tilt, and extent of the Mott lobes. We further explore previously inaccessible regimes, including higher fillings $ρ\gtrsim1$ and the intermediate interaction window $U/t\in[7.69,9.09]$. Special attention is paid to $\varphi=π$, where the effective triangular-ladder mapping becomes singular. Owing to the equivalence of $\varphi=π$ and $-π$ modulo $2π$, a combined symmetry forbids net chiral currents, leading to a nonchiral Mott-insulating state, in contrast to the chiral-superfluid tendency expected away from $\varphi=π$. Our results offer a computationally efficient route for mapping the global phase structure of bosonic flux ladders and provide guidance for future ultracold-atom experiments in artificial gauge fields.

</details>

### [On-Chip Quantum Randomness Amplification](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12173v1)

**Summary.** The paper presents a breakthrough demonstration of on-chip Quantum Randomness Amplification using Semi-Device-Independent protocols. By integrating advanced variational entropy certification techniques onto a silicon photonic chip, the authors achieved a high, practical throughput rate of 20 Mbps. This work paves the way for deploying quantum cryptographic hardware in portable telecom devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly bridges fundamental quantum information theory (entropy bounding) with cutting-edge solid-state photonics engineering, providing a concrete blueprint for next-generation quantum cryptographic hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is achieving high-rate, secure randomness amplification using Semi-Device-Independent (SDI) protocols.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated the first on-chip implementation of SDI randomness amplification on a silicon photonic chip, achieving a practical throughput rate of 20 Mbps.

**Method.** They combine advanced theoretical techniques—specifically an upgraded variational Gauss-Radau method for entropy certification—with state-of-the-art integrated photonics hardware.

**Model / system.** The system is an integrated silicon photonic chip utilizing an electro-optical (EO) and opto-electrical (OE) platform operating at 1550 nm. It implements a protocol that extracts uniform bits from biased quantum seeds generated via an $\epsilon$-biased Santha-Vazirani source.

**Key observables.** Certified von Neumann entropy, MDL Score, throughput rate (20 Mbps), and frequency responses ($\approx 47 	ext{ GHz}$ EO, $52 	ext{ GHz}$ OE).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bias parameter $\epsilon$ (e.g., 0.12); energy bound $\omega$; raw data length ($n=10^{11}$ bits); output security level ($\epsilon_{	ext{sec}} \le 10^{-12}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol relies on low-energy constraints ($\omega$) and the assumption that variational methods provide tighter entropy bounds than standard Gauss-Radau techniques.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate experimental setups, frequency responses (EO/OE), and comparative plots showing certified entropy lower bounds derived from different theoretical methods versus the source bias $\epsilon$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the need for SDI protocols, then introducing the variational Gauss-Radau method to tighten entropy bounds, followed by detailed characterization of the integrated photonic hardware (EO/OE responses), and culminating in the demonstration of high-rate randomness generation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Randomness amplification, the task of extracting uniform private bits from biased seeds that may be partly known by a malicious third party, is of central importance in cryptography. The highest security in this task is provided by a class of quantum protocols known as device-independent, which however are challenging to integrate into scalable devices. Semi-device-independent (SDI) protocols are a promising alternative that guarantees security under few natural assumptions, such as bounds on the amount of energy used by the devices. Here, we provide the first demonstration of SDI randomness amplification on an integrated silicon photonic chip, achieving a throughput rate of 20 Mbps suitable for practical applications. This rate is achieved through a novel technique for SDI entropy certification, which delivers strictly tighter von Neumann entropy bounds compared to existing methods and remains valid even if the preparation and measurement devices share quantum correlations. Overall, the methods developed in this work enable the integration of SDI technology into portable telecom devices, opening up a new generation of quantum cryptographic hardware.

</details>
