**Summary.** The paper presents a novel tensor-network algorithm to overcome the computational bottleneck of evaluating trace norms for many-body operators. By combining Zolotarev's rational approximation with a DMRG-like variational solver, it achieves controlled and systematically improvable estimates for key quantum information measures like entanglement negativity. This opens a practical pathway for studying mixed states in large quantum systems using tensor networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, scalable tool for calculating non-local correlation measures (like entanglement negativity) in large quantum systems that are otherwise intractable due to the exponential scaling of required matrix operations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is efficiently calculating trace norms of Matrix Product Operators (MPOs) for large many-body quantum systems, as exact diagonalization is computationally infeasible.

**Main result.** The authors introduce a controlled tensor-network algorithm that estimates these trace norms and related quantities with systematically improvable accuracy, surpassing the limitations of polynomial-based Lanczos methods.

**Method.** The technique combines Zolotarev's rational approximation for the sign function with a variational formulation solved using a Density-Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG)-like algorithm to estimate $	ext{Tr}[|A|]$.

**Model / system.** The method is applied to general many-body quantum systems described by MPOs, benchmarking its utility on observables like entanglement negativity and quantum fidelity for states such as decohered cluster states.

**Key observables.** Trace norm ($\|A\|_1$), Entanglement Negativity ($N(ho)$), Quantum Fidelity ($F(ho, \sigma)$), and Quantum Fisher Information (QFI).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The approximation accuracy is controlled by the spectral cutoff $\ell$ and the rational approximation degree $r$. The system dynamics are governed by noise parameters like $p$ in decoherence models.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that the required trace norm can be accurately approximated via the variational minimization problem, and it compares favorably against Lanczos methods which rely on polynomial approximations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Zolotarev approximation of the sign function; they also show convergence studies comparing the relative error of the estimated trace norm using this method versus a standard Lanczos approach for various physical states.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the computational bottleneck and proposes the core variational/rational approximation technique. It then details its application to calculate specific quantum information measures (negativity, fidelity) on benchmark systems, concluding with convergence studies comparing it against existing numerical techniques.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Trace norms are fundamental to quantum information theory, yet in many-body systems their evaluation remains a major computational bottleneck, as it generally requires diagonalizing exponentially large operators. Here, we overcome this bottleneck by introducing a controlled tensor-network algorithm for estimating the trace norm of matrix product operators without full diagonalization. The key idea is to combine Zolotarev's rational approximation to the sign function with a variational formulation solved using a density-matrix-renormalization-group-like algorithm. The resulting approximation is systematically improvable, with its accuracy controlled by the rational approximation parameters and the spectral weight near zero. Beyond the reach of exact diagonalization, we demonstrate controlled trace-norm calculations for entanglement negativity, quantum fidelity and quantum Fisher information, achieving substantially improved accuracy over polynomial-based Lanczos approaches. Our results establish trace-norm-based quantities as practical tensor-network observables, opening a route toward tensor-network studies of quantum information in mixed states.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.11919"></a>
### [Quantum tidal locking in orbiting Bose-Einstein condensates](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11919v1)

**Summary.** This paper explores quantum tidal locking in Bose-Einstein condensates by analyzing the coupling between the condensate's internal rotation and its overall orbital motion. Through theoretical modeling and numerical simulations, the authors show that anharmonic potentials induce a self-organization process where intrinsic angular momentum locks to the orbital frequency. This mechanism is significant because it provides a clean platform for studying fundamental synchronization phenomena in quantum matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a quantum mechanical analog to classical celestial mechanics (tidal locking) using ultracold atoms. It demonstrates how intrinsic non-linear interactions can drive a system into a highly ordered, synchronized state without external driving fields, which is crucial for understanding emergent collective dynamics in many-body quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates quantum tidal locking, which is the synchronization between the intrinsic rotation and the orbital motion in mesoscopic quantum fluids.

**Main result.** Quantum tidal locking establishes a robust, self-organized mechanism for generating and stabilizing circulating states, leading to the formation of ring-shaped vortex arrays in Bose-Einstein condensates.

**Method.** The work combines theoretical Hamiltonian analysis (canonical transformations) with numerical simulations solving the Gross-Pitaevskii equation to model the dynamics.

**Model / system.** The system is a Bose-Einstein condensate (BEC) confined by an anharmonic potential, undergoing central force motion and exhibiting orbital angular momentum. The physics is governed by coupling terms derived from the trap's anharmonicity.

**Key observables.** Intrinsic angular momentum accumulation ($\hat{L}_{z}''$), convergence of intrinsic to orbital angular velocity ($\omega_{i,r} 	o \omega_c$), and the observable $\alpha$ used as a proxy for synchronization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The anharmonicity parameter ($eta$) and interaction strength ($g$) significantly influence the rate and success of the locking process.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Approximations include treating the center-of-mass motion classically and discarding higher-order terms in the potential energy expansion.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison across scales (microscopic to macroscopic), show the convergence of angular velocities over time, and detail four stages of vortex array formation from coherent circulation to a lattice structure.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the theoretical framework via Hamiltonian analysis, demonstrating the squeezing process leading to locking, quantifying the synchronization dynamics through observables like $\alpha$, and finally predicting the macroscopic consequence: stable ring-shaped vortex arrays.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Angular momentum coupling manifests widely in diverse physical systems, underpinning the emergent properties and collective dynamics across different scales. The tidal locking, which originates from the synchronization of rotational and orbital motions, has far-reaching impacts in celestial mechanics, reflecting fundamental processes of angular momentum transfer, energy dissipation, and evolution toward dynamical equilibrium. However, its counterpart in mesoscopic quantum fluids has remained largely unexplored. Here we demonstrate the emergence of quantum tidal locking in Bose-Einstein condensates undergoing central force motion in an anharmonic potential. The condensate follows a well-defined orbital trajectory in a static trap and experiences an effective rotating potential induced by the trap anharmonicity. The sustained geometric squeezing continuously deforms the condensate and drives a self-organized synchronization process, in which the intrinsic rotation gradually locks to the orbital motion. Numerical simulations further reveal the formation of a ring-shaped vortex array over longer timescales, arising from the coherent evolution of the rotating matter wave during the locking dynamics. Our findings establish quantum tidal locking in mesoscopic systems as a robust self-organized mechanism for generating and stabilizing circulating states.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a perturbative quantum field theory approach to calculate the thermodynamic and statistical properties of weakly interacting Bose gases within the canonical ensemble. By deriving resummed recursion formulas for the partition function, it accurately predicts observables like condensate occupancy cumulants and critical temperature shifts, particularly useful for understanding finite-size effects in trapped atomic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous theoretical framework for calculating finite-size effects and interaction corrections (beyond mean-field) in Bose gases using the canonical ensemble, which is crucial for interpreting modern trapped-atom experiments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To study how thermodynamic and statistical properties of weakly interacting Bose gases change perturbatively with respect to weak two-particle interactions in the canonical ensemble.

**Main result.** The authors derive resummed recursion formulas for the canonical partition function and calculate the cumulants of ground-state occupancy, providing quantitative predictions for finite systems.

**Method.** The calculation employs path integrals and perturbation theory, developing a recursive approach to handle the interacting partition function in the canonical ensemble.

**Model / system.** The system is a weakly interacting Bose gas confined in a box trap geometry, analyzed using Dirichlet boundary conditions relevant for current atomic gas experiments. Interactions are treated perturbatively via contact potentials.

**Key observables.** Canonical partition function ($Z_N(eta)$), one-particle density matrix ($ho^{(0)}_N$), ground-state occupancy probability distribution $p^{(0)}_q(k|N, eta)$, and cumulants ($\kappa_1$ to $\kappa_4$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Weak interaction strength (perturbation order), temperature ($T$) relative to critical temperatures ($T_c$), and particle number ($N$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to first-order perturbation theory in the weak interaction potential, which leads to known limitations compared to non-perturbative results like Bogoliubov theory at zero temperature.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate Feynman diagrams for Hartree and Fock energies, show plots of the first four cumulants ($\kappa_1$ to $\kappa_4$) versus $T/T_c^{(0)}$ comparing ideal vs. interacting cases, and plot the critical temperature shift ($\Delta T_c/T_c$) derived from heat capacity and fluctuation measurements.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from deriving non-interacting results via cycle decomposition to establishing recursive relations for $Z_N(eta)$. It then calculates first-order corrections involving Hartree and Fock terms, culminating in the resummation of these formulas to determine cumulants and thermodynamic properties like heat capacity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Based on the canonical description of a non-interacting Bose gas, we work out how both thermodynamic and statistical properties change perturbatively with respect to weak two-particle interactions. Up to first order, we obtain a recursion formula for the canonical partition function, which consists of the same Feynman diagrams as the grand-canonical description but with different Feynman rules. Resumming this recursion formula for the canonical partition function allows one to characterize the statistics of the ground-state occupancy by its respective cumulants. We demonstrate the applicability of this approach by analyzing a dilute Bose gas with contact interaction in a box trap. To this end, we used Dirichlet boundary conditions in view of their relevance for current experiments with atomic gases, where the box trap is implemented, for instance, with digital mirror devices.

</details>

### [Single Photon Cross-Phase Shifts Can Be Enhanced by Localization in both Frequency and Time](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11516v1)

**Summary.** The research experimentally shows that single-photon optical nonlinearity can overcome the spectral/temporal trade-off by employing post-selection techniques on photons interacting with an atomic cloud. By conditioning measurements on photon detection, they achieve significant enhancements in the Cross-Phase Shift (XPS), confirming predictions from weak value theory and raising foundational questions about quantum measurement.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses fundamental limitations in quantum nonlinear optics by leveraging measurement back-action (post-selection) to achieve effects that are classically forbidden or limited by uncertainty relations. It bridges advanced concepts from weak measurement theory into a tangible experimental platform.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The fundamental trade-off limiting single-photon optical nonlinearities—requiring both spectral resonance (narrow bandwidth) and high peak intensity (short duration)—is circumvented using post-selection.

**Main result.** The experiment demonstrates a significant enhancement ($\ge 6\pm1$) in the measured Cross-Phase Shift (XPS) when utilizing post-selection, confirming theoretical predictions related to weak value theory.

**Method.** The study combines experimental measurement of XPS using single photons passing through an atomic cloud with theoretical derivations involving time-dependent weak values for atomic excitation.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a cold atomic cloud (e.g., Rb atoms) used as the nonlinear medium, where the interaction between a resonant signal photon and the probe beam induces a measurable phase shift (XPS).

**Key observables.** Peak Cross-Phase Shift ($	ext{XPS}$), which measures the induced phase shift on the probe beam.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Optical Depth ($	ext{OD}$); the enhancement factor is measured at $	ext{OD} = 2.4\pm0.1$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies heavily on post-selection, which allows for simultaneous knowledge of frequency (preparation) and time (post-selection), violating standard uncertainty constraints.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the measured peak XPS dependence on OD, comparing experimental data against theoretical predictions derived from weak value theory. Schematics detail the setup involving resonant signal beams and phase measurement techniques.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the nonlinearity trade-off, presents the experimental methodology using post-selection in cold atoms to measure enhanced XPS, compares these results quantitatively with established weak value theories, and discusses the foundational implications of non-commuting preparation/post-selection measurements.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Single-photon optical nonlinearities face a fundamental trade-off: maximum nonlinearity requires both spectral resonance (narrow bandwidth) and high peak intensity (short duration), constraints that are incompatible due to the time-energy uncertainty relation. We demonstrate experimentally that this limitation does not need to exist in cases involving post-selection. We measure a cross-phase shift (XPS) produced by a resonant photon from a narrow-band source that is first transmitted through a cold atomic cloud and then localized in time through detection. The peak size of this XPS is greatly enhanced compared to that of Gaussian single-photon-level pulses without post-selection, benefiting from the narrow bandwidth of the resonant prepared state and the high intensity of the post-selected state simultaneously. We measure enhancements in the peak XPS of 6$\pm$1 at an optical depth (OD) of 2.4$\pm$0.1, and our results are in qualitative agreement across a range of optical depths with the recently developed weak value theory of atomic excitation [Thompson et al., APL Quantum 2, 036108 (2025)] for such post-selected photons. This work uncovers new consequences of having simultaneous knowledge of frequency and time, raising new foundational questions about how a particle behaves, and interacts with other systems, when its preparation and post-selection are non-commuting.

</details>

### [Shadow Engineering of Quantum Processes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12035v1)

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.12035_figures/2606.12035_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Shadow engineering: framework, key cases, and applications. (b) Sparse transfer-matrix encoding: First, evolve random product states under under E and perform randomized Pauli measurements on the outputs [7, 12]. Then, map input-outcome index pairs to matrix entries, building a row-normalized 6n×6n sparse matrix (stored in compressed sparse...</sub>

**Summary.** The paper introduces 'shadow engineering,' a novel technique to characterize complex quantum processes by encoding individual channel information into sparse transfer matrices. This allows researchers to predict properties of composite operations (like sequential gates or time evolution) with polynomial resource scaling, significantly outperforming traditional methods like QPT on real superconducting hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, resource-efficient tool that bypasses the exponential scaling bottleneck of full process tomography, making complex quantum characterization feasible for near-term noisy hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is efficiently characterizing complex, composite quantum processes $f(\mathcal{E}_1, \dots, \mathcal{E}_k)$ for hardware benchmarking, avoiding the exponential resource scaling of Quantum Process Tomography (QPT).

**Main result.** Shadow engineering provides a scalable framework to predict properties of these composite processes using only classical shadows from individual channels, achieving polynomial sample complexity.

**Method.** The authors encode single-channel process information into sparse transfer matrices ($\hat{T}_E$) and then use algebraic manipulation (e.g., matrix multiplication for concatenation) to construct the transfer matrix for the composite process $\hat{T}_f$.

**Model / system.** The framework is tested on a superconducting quantum processor, characterizing noise channels ($\mathcal{E}_M$) and simulating Hamiltonian dynamics (TFIM model). The theory applies to general CPTP maps acting on $n$-qubit states.

**Key observables.** Expected values of observables $	ext{Tr}[O \cdot f(\mathcal{E}_1, \dots, \mathcal{E}_k)(ho)]$, error mitigation ratios ($r$), and magnetization/correlation functions in dynamics simulations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Qubit count ($n$), truncation parameter ($\kappa$), sample sizes ($N_1, N_2$), and physical parameters like gate errors and coherence times.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that the composite process structure $f$ allows for an algebraic relationship between the constituent transfer matrices. It also relies on truncating observables to low-weight Pauli operators ($\kappa$-truncation) to manage complexity.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the three-step framework (shadow $	o$ matrix $	o$ prediction), benchmarking against prior art, and applying the method to error mitigation and Hamiltonian dynamics simulations on superconducting hardware.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from theory by defining the shadow engineering framework using transfer matrices for single processes. It then extends this algebraically to predict composite operations (adjoints, concatenations) and finally validates its use in practical applications like error mitigation and time-evolution simulation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Characterizing quantum processes is essential for hardware benchmarking, error diagnosis, and algorithm verification. While recent work [PRX QUANTUM \textbf{4}, 040337 (2023)] extended classical shadows from quantum state to quantum process, enabling efficient single-channel $\mathcal{E}$ property prediction, its applicability to composite processes $f(\mathcal{E}_1, \mathcal{E}_2,\cdots, \mathcal{E}_k)$ remains unexplored. We introduce shadow engineering, a framework encoding the classical shadows of processes into sparse transfer matrices to predict $f(\mathcal{E}_1, \mathcal{E}_2,\cdots, \mathcal{E}_k)$ properties with proven polynomial sample complexity, matching single-channel efficiency while exponentially lower than quantum process tomography. Crucially, this approach repurposes existing $\mathcal{E}_m$-shadow data without physical execution of $f(\mathcal{E}_1, \mathcal{E}_2,\cdots, \mathcal{E}_k)$, enabling flexible quantum process characterization with minimal hardware overhead. We demonstrate the framework's effectiveness and practicality on a superconducting quantum processor for typical applications such as error mitigation and Hamiltonian dynamical simulation. This framework unlocks new capabilities for predicting complex quantum behaviors without physical re-execution, with immediate applications in near-term device calibration and quantum simulation.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12056"></a>
### [Clifford disentanglers for entanglement reduction in molecular electronic structure simulations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12056v1)

**Authors:** Longfei Chang, Zibo Wu, Yunzhi Li, Haiqi Liu, Jiajun Ren, Mingpu Qin, Zhendong Li, Wei-Hai Fang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12056v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.12056_figures/2606.12056_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Schematic illustration of the determination of Clifford disentanglers from an MPS and the applications in DMRG and VQE. (a) Local 2Q or 4Q Clifford gates are applied sequentially to an MPS to reduce entanglement. For each bond, representatives of the relevant equivalent classes are tested to minimize the bipartite entropy Sm,m+1. The sweeping process is repeated until the entropy converges, yielding the optimized disentangler C∗ D within this local search protocol. The transformed Hamiltonian can be used in subsequent DMRG and VQE. (b) In CAMPS, DMRG first produces an MPS |ΨMPS⟩; then C∗ D is determined from the obtained MPS and the disentan- gled MPS C∗ D|ΨMPS⟩is truncated to the...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.12056_figures/2606.12056_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Schematics of 2Q and 4Q Clifford op- erators in the same equivalence class. (a) Two 2Q Clifford operators C2 and C′ 2 belong to the same class if they differ only by arbitrary 1Q Clifford operators C1 and C′ 1 acting on the two qubits. (b) Two 4Q Clifford operators C4 and C′ 4 belong to the same class if they differ only by arbitrary 2Q Clifford operators C2 and C′ 2 acting on qubits (1, 2) and (3, 4), respectively.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Figure 3: Bond-length dependence of the relative energy error ratio ηE (solid lines) between DMRG and CAMPS and the ratio of the maximum entanglement entropies ηS (dashed lines) for the MPS obtained from conventional DMRG and after Clifford disentangling step. Upper: JW mapping, Lower: parity mapping.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

</details>

(a,b) Prob- ability distributions of the exact ground-state wavefunction for the H12 system in the STO- 3G basis (OAO, Jordan–Wigner mapping, bond length 1 ˚A). </details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces Clifford disentanglers as a structured method to reduce entanglement in simulating molecular electronic structures using tensor networks. By applying these transformations, the authors significantly improve energy accuracy and robustness of methods like DMRG and VQE at fixed computational resources. This establishes them as a valuable 'entanglement-engineering' tool for quantum chemistry simulations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides concrete algorithmic tools to manage entanglement structure within tensor network simulations of quantum chemistry, directly impacting how efficiently we can simulate complex molecular interactions on classical or near-term quantum hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Entanglement is a major bottleneck limiting the efficiency of tensor-network and quantum simulations for molecular electronic structures.

**Main result.** Clifford disentanglers are established as a useful, structure-preserving tool that reduces entanglement and improves energy accuracy in both DMRG (CAMPS) and VQE calculations at fixed bond dimensions.

**Method.** The authors systematically assess Clifford disentanglers by classifying them based on their action on the Schmidt spectrum to reduce search space. These transformations are applied within an iterative Matrix Product State framework to preprocess Hamiltonians.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on molecular electronic structure problems, using fermionic Hamiltonians mapped onto qubit representations (e.g., Jordan-Wigner mapping). Simulations involve systems like H12 and N2.

**Key observables.** Bipartite entanglement entropy ($S_{1/2}(ho_A)$), relative energy error ratio ($\eta_E$), Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR), and sensitivity to orbital ordering ($\sigma_X$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bond dimension ($M$), basis set size, and the specific choice of Clifford gates (2Q vs 4Q).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach assumes that entanglement reduction via structured Clifford transformations can significantly improve simulation accuracy without fundamentally altering the physics captured by the fixed bond dimension.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the schematic process of disentangler application in DMRG/VQE workflows, show the linear correlation between energy error improvement and entropy reduction, and compare energy errors across different molecular systems and mappings.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem (entanglement bottleneck), proposes Clifford disentanglers as a solution, details the systematic search methodology using equivalence classes, validates the method by showing improved results in DMRG/VQE for various molecules, and discusses limitations regarding basis set size dependence.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement is a key bottleneck limiting the efficiency of tensor-network and quantum simulations of molecular electronic structures. Here, we systematically assess and extend Clifford disentanglers as a structure-preserving approach to entanglement reduction: they can modify the entanglement structure of qubit wavefunctions while retaining the Pauli-string form of qubit Hamiltonians. To enable a practical search over Clifford transformations, we classify Clifford operators by their action on the Schmidt spectrum across a bipartition, reducing the two- and four-qubit search spaces to 20 and 91392 representatives, respectively. Embedded in an iterative Clifford-augmented matrix product state framework, these transformations reduce the energy errors at fixed bond dimension for the molecular test cases studied and mitigate the dependence on orbital orderings and fermion-to-qubit mappings. We further show that Clifford disentanglers can also benefit quantum simulations such as the shallow-circuit variational quantum eigensolver calculations. Together, these results establish Clifford disentanglers as a useful structure-preserving entanglement-engineering tool for tensor-network and quantum simulations of molecular electronic structure, while also clarifying their correlation dependence and motivating future developments.

</details>

### [Logical error estimation from syndrome data of surface-code experiments](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11496v1)

**Summary.** The paper introduces a novel technique to estimate the Detector Error Model (DEM) probabilities directly from raw syndrome measurement data of surface code experiments. By using these empirically derived DEMs as decoder priors, the authors demonstrate significant and reliable reductions in the estimated logical error rate compared to standard noise models on state-of-the-art quantum hardware. This method offers a powerful, model-agnostic tool for improving QEC performance analysis.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a practical, data-driven methodology for characterizing hardware noise in QEC that bypasses complex Hamiltonian reconstruction, which is highly relevant to experimental condensed matter/quantum physics groups working on superconducting or trapped-ion qubits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is estimating the Detector Error Model (DEM) event probabilities directly from experimental syndrome data to improve quantum error correction (QEC) decoders, thereby reducing estimated logical error probabilities.

**Main result.** Syndrome-estimated DEMs consistently reduce the decoded logical error probability relative to baseline device-informed models on both Google and IBM hardware, often achieving improvements of $5\%-10\%$ or more.

**Method.** The authors estimate event probabilities by analyzing detector moments (e.g., $\langle v_i angle$, $\langle v_i v_j angle$) from raw syndrome data, avoiding the need for independent device benchmarking or supervised fitting to final logical outcomes.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on surface-code memory experiments performed on superconducting quantum processors (Google Willow and IBM miami). The formalism uses a Detector Error Model (DEM) that maps physical detector events to syndrome measurements.

**Key observables.** Logical Error Probability ($	ext{LEP}$ or $P_L$), fractional percent change in $	ext{LEP}$ ($\Delta P_L$), detector-pair covariances ($C_{ij}$), and entanglement infidelity ($	ext{EF}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code distance ($d=3, 5, 7$), number of syndrome extraction cycles ($r$), and the type of dynamical decoupling (DD) applied.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that detector moments are sufficient to infer reliable event probabilities. Limitations include handling negative correlators by setting probabilities to zero and benchmarking only on stochastic circuit-level noise initially.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare $\Delta P_L$ for various code distances ($d=3, 5, 7$) across different DEM priors (estimated vs. reference) on both Google and IBM hardware; other figures show detector covariances, time evolution of rates, and entanglement infidelity comparisons.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the formalism for syndrome-based DEM estimation using moment calculations, applies this method to analyze memory experiments on two different quantum processors, compares the resulting logical performance against established noise models, and discusses scaling laws and diagnostics like crosstalk.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Decoders for quantum error correction (QEC) experiments rely on detector error models (DEMs), which encode, for each error, its probability and the detectors and logical observables it flips. Here we show that estimating DEM event probabilities from experimental syndromes is feasible, avoids independent device benchmarking, and produces useful decoder priors for estimating and reducing decoded logical error probabilities. We evaluate our methods using open-source data from surface-code memory experiments performed on Google's Willow chip, and we carry out analogous surface-code experiments on IBM's \texttt{ibm\_miami} processor. Despite the different physical error scales of the Google and IBM devices, in both cases our estimated DEMs improve logical error probabilities relative to baseline device-informed DEMs, typically at the $5\%-10\%$ level and with larger gains in some IBM cases, without additional calibration circuits, decoder fine-tuning, or supervised fitting to logical outcomes.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11644"></a>
### [Raw-Curve Quantum Fingerprints: A Mahalanobis Authentication Framework with Drift Early Warning and Adversarial Detection](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11644v1)

**Authors:** Geyuyan Ma, Xiangdong Meng, Yangyang Fei, Zhiqiang Fan, Hanshi Zhao, Chenhui Wang, Haoran Yang, Weilong Wang, Zheng Shan

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

The processor is authenticated by actively probing with four quantum experiments. (c) PCA compresses the high dimensional fingerprint into a low dimensional subspace; a Mahalanobis nearest-neighbor classifier identifies the claimed device and computes...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.11644_figures/2606.11644_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Data acquisition and workflow. (a) Four interleaved quantum experiments are executed on a fixed 5-qubit chain. (b) Raw measurement statistics are directly concatenated into a 1468-dimensional feature vector. (c) The dataset is split chronologically (70% train / 30% test).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.11644_figures/2606.11644_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

**Summary.** This work introduces a novel authentication framework that uses raw measurement data from multiple interleaved quantum experiments to create high-dimensional 'quantum fingerprints' of superconducting processors. By employing a Mahalanobis nearest-neighbor classifier, it not only verifies the device identity but also provides a confidence score that predicts performance degradation due to environmental noise or malicious attacks. This offers a practical path toward building trustworthy and reliable quantum cloud computing services.

**Why it may be interesting.** The methodology directly addresses hardware trustworthiness in quantum computing, providing quantitative metrics ($C_{	ext{claimed}}$) to monitor device health proactively, which is crucial for deploying complex quantum algorithms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of transparency in quantum cloud platforms enables hardware substitution attacks, compromising computational integrity.

**Main result.** The proposed framework achieves 100% benign authentication accuracy and provides a quantifiable confidence score ($C_{	ext{claimed}}$) that predicts device degradation due to noise or adversarial manipulation.

**Method.** It constructs high-dimensional quantum fingerprints from raw measurement statistics using a Mahalanobis nearest-neighbor classifier (MNN).

**Model / system.** The system involves superconducting quantum processors (e.g., TianYan, LQMS) tested on fixed chains of five qubits across four complementary interleaved experiments.

**Key observables.** Authentication Confidence ($C_{	ext{claimed}}$), Mahalanobis distance, raw measurement statistics (means and standard deviations).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Noise strength ($\sigma$), perturbation strength ($\epsilon$), feature dimensionality (up to 1468-d).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes a closed-set environment for authentication; theoretical analysis relies on additive isotropic Gaussian noise.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the workflow from raw data acquisition to high-dimensional embedding, showing confidence decay curves under increasing noise ($\sigma$) and detection rates against various adversarial attacks.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the fingerprinting method (raw statistics $ightarrow$ high-D vector), then applying MNN for authentication, followed by rigorous testing of drift monitoring using $C_{	ext{claimed}}$ under Gaussian noise, and finally evaluating robustness against white-box adversarial attacks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum cloud platforms are poised to deliver powerful computing capabilities, but users have no direct means to verify which physical device executes their workload. This lack of transparency enables hardware substitution attacks, where a malicious adversary could redirect a job to a substituted or inferior processor. We present a general authentication framework that addresses this problem by constructing multi-dimensional quantum fingerprints from raw measurement data. Without any curve fitting, we directly concatenate the raw statistics of complementary experiments into a high-dimensional feature vector that preserves subtle device-specific information. A Mahalanobis nearest-neighbor classifier achieves 100\% benign authentication accuracy on three superconducting processors over a three-week chronological split. The classifier naturally yields an authentication confidence $C_{\mathrm{claimed}}$ which reveals device-specific safety margins and motivates per-device alert thresholds. We assess the framework's robustness under two distinct scenarios. Under additive isotropic Gaussian noise, $C_{\mathrm{claimed}}$ decays predictably at a rate explained by inverse covariance traces, enabling an early warning mechanism. Against white-box adversarial perturbations, the same confidence threshold detects $L_2$ targeted attacks with near-perfect success and reveals device-dependent empirical thresholds for $L_\infty$ attacks, while untargeted and sparse attacks are ineffective. The proposed framework thus unifies fingerprint extraction, drift-resilient authentication, proactive health monitoring, and adversarial defense, offering a practical step toward trustworthy quantum cloud computing.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11978"></a>
### [The Kondo effect in ferromagnetic quantum critical CeRh$_6$Ge$_4$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11978v1)

**Authors:** Martin Sundermann, Joe D. Thompson, Eric D. Bauer, Chun Fu Chang, Sheng-Huai Chen, Chang-Yang Kuo, Liu Hao Tjeng, Getrud Zwicknagl, Andrea Severing  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11978v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This paper investigates the electronic structure of the heavy fermion ferromagnet CeRh6Ge4 near a quantum critical point. By analyzing X-ray absorption spectroscopy and photoelectron data, the authors demonstrate that simple models fail to explain temperature dependence. They successfully model the results by incorporating the Kondo effect into a multiorbital ground state description, highlighting orbital-dependent correlations as key to understanding its magnetic criticality.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed spectroscopic pathway to map out the complex many-body physics governing quantum criticality in heavy fermion systems, directly linking measurable optical properties (dichroism) to fundamental correlated parameters like $T_K$.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the mechanism behind a pressure-induced quantum critical point (QCP) in the heavy fermion ferromagnet CeRh6Ge4, focusing on the interplay between localized and itinerant 4f electron character.

**Main result.** The experimental data are best explained by incorporating the Kondo effect into a multiorbital ground state model, suggesting an orbital-dependent hybridization mechanism is crucial for describing the low-temperature magnetic behavior.

**Method.** The research combines advanced spectroscopic measurements (PES and XAS) with theoretical modeling using full-multiplet configuration interaction (fm-CI) and simplified Anderson Impurity Models (SIAM/NCA).

**Model / system.** The system is the heavy fermion ferromagnet CeRh6Ge4, which exhibits complex magnetic ordering influenced by strong electron correlations. The model treats the hybridization of Ce 4f states with conduction electrons within a multi-orbital framework.

**Key observables.** Temperature dependence of linear dichroism (LD), spectral weights in XAS/PES satellites, and the derived Kondo temperature (T_K).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The critical pressure ($p_c = 0.8 	ext{ GPa}$), the effective $4f$ occupancy ($n_f \sim 0.9$), and the Kondo coupling strength ($T_K \sim 60 	ext{ K}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations such as the Non-Crossing Approximation (NCA) for the SIAM, and assumes specific symmetries or constraints when fitting spectroscopic data.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the temperature dependence of experimental LD versus theoretical predictions derived from both simple thermal occupation models and more complex Kondo-affected multiorbital calculations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing evidence for $4f$ hybridization via PES/XAS, then using linear dichroism measurements to constrain a CEF model, and finally employing the SIAM/NCA framework to incorporate the temperature-dependent Kondo screening effect into the ground state description.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The mechanism of a pressure-induced quantum critical point in the heavy fermion ferromagnet CeRh$_6$Ge$_4$ has attracted interest, as ferromagnetic quantum criticality in a clean itinerant Ce compound is typically avoided. The localized versus itinerant character of the 4\textit{f} electrons is a key aspect for understanding this behavior. We investigated the electronic structure of the 4\textit{f} shell in CeRh$_6$Ge$_4$ using core-level photoelectron and x-ray absorption spectroscopy, demonstrating the hybridization of Ce 4\textit{f} with the conduction electrons. Linearly polarized x-ray absorption reveals a temperature-dependent linear dichroism consistent with the crystal-electric-field (CEF) sequence as inferred from the static susceptibility. This dichroism cannot be described by an ionic full-multiplet model alone, but is reproduced by including the Kondo effect within a single-impurity Anderson model in the non crossing approximation (SIAM/NCA). The Kondo effect mixes higher lying crystal-field states into a resulting multiorbital ground state with 4\textit{f} occupancy \textit{n}$_f$\,$\sim$\,0.9. Deviations at low temperatures between the measured linear dichroism and calculated dichroism suggest an orbital-dependent Kondo effect. A scenario in which there is a multiorbital ground state and orbital-dependent Kondo hybridization should be a starting point for a model of pressure-induced criticality in CeRh$_6$Ge$_4$.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11485"></a>
### [A Cryogenic Uniaxial Strain Cell for Quantum Devices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11485v1)

**Authors:** Bradley Lloyd, Davis Rash, Chandler Wilburn, Paul Kliewer, Meenakshi Singh

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.11485_figures/2606.11485_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Finite element analysis of axial strain (εxx) (a) Three-dimensional strain map of the dorsal silicon die under 200 V differential piezo bias at 50 mK, showing the distribution of axial strain across the chip surface for the asymmetrically loaded (top) and symmetrically loaded (bottom) cases. (b) Axial strain at the center of the 1×1 mm² region of interest as a function of substrate thickness for three mounting configurations: symmetric dual-chip loading (circles), asymmetric single-chip loading (squares), and chip epoxied directly to the actuator face (triangles). </details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a novel cryogenic uniaxial strain cell specifically engineered to apply uniform mechanical strain to thick, square semiconductor chips. This design overcomes limitations of prior cells by integrating high-density electrical wiring and using advanced shielding techniques. It provides a critical tool for experimentally tuning quantum device properties via controlled strain at low temperatures.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides essential hardware infrastructure for realizing quantum devices whose properties are tunable by mechanical strain, which is crucial for many solid-state qubit platforms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing piezoelectric strain cells are unsuitable for thick, square-profile semiconductor quantum devices, and integrating dense wiring while maintaining electrical isolation is challenging.

**Main result.** The authors designed a novel cryogenic uniaxial strain cell that homogeneously strains thick silicon dies while successfully incorporating high-density RF/DC interconnects and using Faraday cages for electrical isolation.

**Method.** The design utilizes a highly symmetric dual-chip loading configuration with a three-stack actuation scheme, validated by finite element simulations and experimental measurement on a 200 $\mu$m thick silicon die.

**Model / system.** The platform is a cryogenic uniaxial strain cell operating in a dilution refrigerator, designed to apply controlled mechanical strain to semiconductor quantum devices (e.g., Si qubits). The system incorporates complex electrical shielding and mounting structures.

**Key observables.** Applied uniaxial strain ($	ext{215 } \mu\epsilon$ for $200 	ext{V}$ bias), strain homogeneity ($\Delta$), and the ability to support dense RF/DC wiring under strain.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cryogenic temperatures (down to 50 mK), applied voltage ($\pm 200 	ext{V}$), substrate thickness ($200 \mu	ext{m}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The symmetric mounting configuration effectively suppresses flexural deformation and shear stress, and the Faraday cage successfully prevents unwanted Stark shifts.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides an exploded overview of the strain cell design components. Figure 2 details measurements validating the setup's performance at cryogenic temperatures.

**Paper structure.** The paper first identifies limitations in current strain cells, then introduces the novel symmetric dual-chip loading design and electrical integration strategy. It presents simulation results confirming homogeneity improvements before concluding with experimental validation on a silicon die.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Mechanical strain is a powerful resource for tuning quantum systems, but existing piezoelectric strain cells are generally optimized for fragile, high-aspect-ratio single crystals rather than the thick, square-profile chips typical of semiconductor quantum devices. Furthermore, adapting these cells for qubits requires accommodating dense RF and DC wiring while maintaining strict electrical isolation from high-voltage piezo actuators. Here, we present a piezoelectric uniaxial strain cell designed to homogeneously strain thick, square-profile substrates. We introduce a highly symmetric dual-chip loading configuration that effectively suppresses flexural deformation and shear stress. The cell integrates a high-density RF/DC interposer to support standard wire bonding and encloses the actuators in a grounded Faraday cage to prevent unwanted Stark shifts in the device layer. Finite element simulations confirm that combining stiff actuators with this symmetric mounting drastically improves strain homogeneity. Finally, we validate the apparatus experimentally by applying uniaxial strain to a 200 $μ$m thick silicon die. Surface strain measurements demonstrate an applied strain of 215 $με$ for 200 V applied piezo bias.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces 'superspace concentration' as a novel resource measure quantifying how well quantum information can be concentrated in a preferred subspace. It rigorously proves its properties and demonstrates that this focus measure offers superior resilience against adversarial attacks compared to standard fidelity metrics. The work provides a powerful, theoretically grounded tool for assessing the security and capacity of quantum protocols.

**Why it may be interesting.** For AMO physicists studying open quantum systems, this work provides a new, quantifiable metric ($F(ho)$) that detects structural information loss beyond simple entanglement or coherence measures when the system is subjected to structured noise or targeted attacks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops a resource-theoretic framework around 'superspace concentration' ($F(ho)$) to quantify quantum resources, particularly for understanding adversarial robustness and algorithmic complexity.

**Main result.** Focus ($F(ho)$) was shown to be operationally distinct from other measures like U(dS)-asymmetry, providing superior resilience against coherent unitary attacks, and its capacity gap scales as $\log_2(d_S)$.

**Method.** The authors use resource theory axioms (monotonicity, etc.) combined with GPU-accelerated numerical simulations to test the behavior of $F(ho)$ under various noise channels and adversarial perturbations.

**Model / system.** The system involves a composite Hilbert space $H = H_{	ext{phys}} \otimes H_{	ext{super}}$, where the focus measure is derived from the largest eigenvalue ($\lambda_{\max}$) of the reduced density matrix $ho_{	ext{super}}$ over an extended superspace.

**Key observables.** Focus Measure ($F(ho) = \lambda_{\max}(ho_{	ext{super}})$), Focus Capacity Gap ($\Delta F$), and comparison against standard fidelity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Superspace dimension $d_S$, attack strength $\epsilon$, noise parameters $p$ (for depolarizing channels).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework relies on identifying 'Focus-Non-Generating' (FNG) operations, and capacity gap analysis is currently limited to product and correlated noise channels.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the superior robustness of Focus over fidelity under adversarial attacks, confirm the $\log_2(d_S)$ scaling law for the capacity gap, and map out monotonicity violations across various system configurations.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the mathematical definition of $F(ho)$, proves its resource-theoretic properties (monotonicity), demonstrates its operational superiority over other measures in adversarial settings, connects it to algorithmic complexity (Grover's algorithm), and numerically characterizes the capacity gap scaling law.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study superspace concentration as a quantum resource, formalized through the focus measure F(\r{ho}) = λ_max(\r{ho}_super) - the largest eigenvalue of the reduced superspace state - which quantifies the capacity of a quantum system to concentrate informational weight into a preferred subspace of an extended degree-of-freedom space. We develop a complete resource-theoretic framework around this measure and validate its properties through GPU-accelerated numerical simulation. Analytic decoherence predictions are confirmed to machine precision (1.11 x 10^{-16}) for superspace dimensions dS in {2,4,8,16,32}. Focus monotonicity holds across 10,000 random states with zero violations under four focus-non-generating channels across six system configurations. Focused quantum states resist coherent unitary attacks with significantly greater resilience than standard fidelity predicts, with focus remaining above 0.9 at attack strength ε = 0.302 versus ε = 0.174 for fidelity. We further demonstrate that the focus measure and the U(dS)-asymmetry measure are operationally distinct: asymmetry remains near zero and provides no robustness signal under coherent and targeted attacks while focus tracks spectral concentration and remains robust until ε > 0.3. The connection between Grover's algorithm and superspace concentration is made explicit via the identity F(|ψ_k><ψ_k|) = P(marked), providing a resource-theoretic interpretation of oracle query complexity. Finally, we provide the first numerical characterization of the focus capacity gap ΔF, identifying a log_2(dS) scaling law confirmed for both product and correlated noise channels.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12367"></a>
### [Nonadditivity in Quantum Field Theory: Replica Energies, Scaling Filters, and the Renormalization Group](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12367v1)

**Authors:** Giacomo Santoni, Francesco Scardino  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12367v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.12367_figures/2606.12367_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: (a) Thermodynamic F-function FE(x) for a free massive scalar on S3 (x = mR). FE starts at FUV ≈0.064 (dashed line), overshoots below FIR = 0, reaching a minimum at x∗≈1.58 (dot), then returns to 0−from below. (b) Flow derivative dFE/d log R. Negative for x &lt; x∗, positive for x &gt; x∗(shaded regions). The dashed curve on the top right corner is the analytic asymptotic π/(96 x) in Eq. (107), which controls the large-x tail. The sign change demonstrates that FE is not a monotone F-function.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work develops the concept of 'replica energy' as a universal thermodynamic diagnostic tool to quantify deviations from ideal additivity in Quantum Field Theory. By applying specific differential filtering operators tailored to different dimensions, the authors successfully extract fundamental physical constants like central charges and anomaly coefficients from finite-size effects. It provides a rigorous mathematical language for understanding nonextensive behavior across various quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The systematic application of scaling filters provides a powerful, unified diagnostic tool for theoretical physicists studying critical phenomena or topological phases where simple extensivity assumptions break down.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates nonadditivity and deviations from simple scaling laws (extensivity) in Quantum Field Theory, aiming to bridge concepts between statistical mechanics and QFT.

**Main result.** The replica energy provides a unified thermodynamic language for diagnosing additivity violations, defect-free energies, and renormalization group irreversibility across different dimensions.

**Method.** The core method involves applying specialized differential filters (e.g., $\left(1 - rac{1}{L}\partial_Light)$) to the logarithm of the partition function ($\log Z$) to systematically remove leading bulk contributions and isolate universal, nonadditive corrections.

**Model / system.** The analysis applies to general thermodynamic systems, finite-volume QFTs (e.g., on spheres $S^3$ or $S^4$), topologically ordered gapped phases (Chern–Simons theory), and fracton models in various dimensions ($d=2, 3, 4$).

**Key observables.** Replica Energy ($eta E$), Sphere Free Energy ($F_E(R)$), Euler Anomaly Coefficient ($a$), Central Charge ($c$), and the dimension of the topological Hilbert space ($\dim H(\Sigma)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size $L$, temperature $eta^{-1}$, spatial dimensions $d=2, 3, 4$, and correlation lengths $\xi$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on asymptotic factorization of the partition function and specific definitions of filtering operators designed to project out different types of scaling behavior.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how the replica energy functional changes its dependence on size $R$ or $L$ across dimensions, showing distinct behaviors for finite-size corrections versus topological contributions.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops a general framework using replica energies to diagnose nonadditivity. It then applies this framework dimension by dimension ($d=2, 3, 4$) to extract universal constants like $c$ and $a$, comparing the mathematical structure of the filters required in each case.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Extensive systems have a simple thermodynamic signature: the logarithm of the partition function scales homogeneously with the size of the system. We show that the failure of this scaling, measured by the replica energy ${\cal E}$, provides a useful bridge between statistical mechanics and quantum field theory. The associated differential operator $(1-\frac1d L\partial_L)$ removes the leading bulk contribution to $W=\log Z$ and isolates the part that is sensitive to boundaries, topology, defects, long-range forces, or other sources of nonadditivity. In quantum field theory this thermodynamic idea has two closely related uses. For ordinary finite-volume or spherical partition functions, suitable higher-order versions of the same filter remove local counterterms and extract universal fixed-point data such as the central charge, the sphere free energy $F$, and the Euler anomaly coefficient $a$. For replica geometries with entangling defects, the same filtering principle gives the renormalized defect free energy. In $2+1$ dimensions, its $n\to1$ limit is precisely the entropic $F$-function. We use this perspective to distinguish ordinary finite-size corrections, topology-dependent constants in gapped phases, subextensive fracton degeneracies, and genuinely nonextensive systems with long-range interactions such as self-gravitating thermal matter. Replica energy therefore offers a common thermodynamic language for additivity, defect free energies, and renormalization-group irreversibility.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper tackles the challenge of simulating continuum Quantum Field Theories using lattice methods by implementing Hamiltonian Renormalization Group (RG) flow on quantum computers. It introduces VAPOR, a novel variational algorithm that translates the complex fixed-point search of RG theory into a solvable quantum optimization problem. This provides a crucial pathway for extracting physically meaningful observables from highly correlated gauge theories on NISQ devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses how complex, continuum physics problems from high-energy theory can be mapped onto near-term quantum hardware by reformulating difficult spectral/flow problems into tractable variational optimization routines.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is implementing Hamiltonian Renormalization Group (RG) flow, necessary to find discretization-error-free operators for continuum Quantum Field Theory (QFT), on quantum computers.

**Main result.** The authors introduce VAPOR, a variational algorithm that successfully maps the fixed-point identification of RG flow onto a solvable quantum optimization problem, demonstrating its applicability to Lattice Gauge Theories.

**Method.** They combine theoretical RG techniques—which involve defining operator families and identifying fixed points via spectral decomposition—with variational quantum algorithms (VAPOR/VQE) for numerical execution on near-term hardware.

**Model / system.** The primary model is a Lattice Gauge Theory, specifically using a toy model of an SU(2) Yang-Mills theory. The Hamiltonian and operators are constructed within a spin-network basis to enforce gauge invariance.

**Key observables.** Fixed-point 4-flux operator ($O^*$), ground state energy expectation values ($\langle O^* angle$), and the cost function $C(	heta)$ used for optimization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling constants (e.g., $\lambda$), lattice size/cutoff ($j_{	ext{max}}$), and variational parameters ($	heta$) defining the quantum circuit ansatz depth.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The VAPOR approach assumes that the RG flow can be restricted to finite-dimensional, RG-invariant Pauli orbits, allowing the fixed-point search to be framed as a constrained optimization problem.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the convergence of operator families under RG flow towards fixed points; they also compare simulated expectation values of the 4-flux operator using VAPOR/VQE against known analytical results for various coupling regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically builds from continuum QFT concepts to lattice discretization (using spin-network bases), then develops the theoretical framework for RG flow on Pauli operators, and finally proposes and tests the variational quantum algorithm (VAPOR) to solve the resulting fixed-point optimization problem.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

While Hamiltonian Lattice Gauge Theory is gaining traction, today's limited numerical capacity leaves simulations affected by discretization errors. This motivates the implementation of renormalization group (RG) techniques to find discretization-error-free operators. To this end, we introduce VAPOR, a variational quantum algorithm that decomposes operators into Pauli strings, identifies RG flow orbits, and determines fixed points of a naively discretized operator. We illustrate this using a toy model of a kinematic operator in a symmetry-restricted SU(2) Yang-Mills theory.

</details>

### [Kinetic kagome magnetism: from self-trapping RVB polarons to semiclassical correlations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12204v1)

**Authors:** Yufei Pei, Shuai A. Chen, Claudio Castelnovo, Roderich Moessner

<sub>FIG. 5. Rescaled peak intensity at the 𝐾point for our unpolarized system on lattices of size 𝐿𝑥× 3, with 6 ≤𝐿𝑥≤24, obtained using DMRG (circles joined by solid lines). **Summary.** This paper investigates kinetic magnetism on the kagome lattice by studying how a single hole interacts with localized spins, forming RVB polarons. The authors show that as the system moves toward an unpolarized state, these short-range quantum correlations evolve into robust, long-range $\sqrt{3} \times \sqrt{3}$ antiferromagnetic order. By combining ED and DMRG, they bridge the gap between localized valence bond physics and conventional magnetic ordering.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed theoretical mechanism linking microscopic quantum entanglement (RVB singlets) to macroscopic magnetic ordering ($\sqrt{3} \times \sqrt{3}$), which is crucial for understanding high-temperature superconductivity and frustrated magnetism in correlated materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how hole kinetics dictate magnetism in highly frustrated doped Mott insulators, specifically tracking the transition from short-range RVB polaron physics to long-range magnetic correlations.

**Main result.** The research demonstrates a conceptual bridge where localized RVB polarons at low spin polarization evolve into conventional $\sqrt{3} \times \sqrt{3}$ antiferromagnetic order in the unpolarized limit.

**Method.** The authors employ Exact Diagonalization (ED) and Density Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG) techniques, supplemented by variational calculations using Husimi cactus clusters to model the hole-spin interactions.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled as a single-hole counter-Nagaoka problem on the kagome lattice, governed by an infinite-$U$ Hubbard Hamiltonian. The physics is analyzed by tuning the magnetization parameter (doping level $n$).

**Key observables.** Polaron bandwidth, spin and hole density profiles, spin structure factor $S(\mathbf{k})$, and ground state energy scaling.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetization/spin polarization ($n$), lattice geometry (kagome), and the transition between small-$n$ (localized) and large-$n$ (semiclassical) regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on modeling polarons using Husimi cactus clusters, and extrapolations to infinite systems from finite-size DMRG/ED calculations are necessary but carry potential self-interaction errors.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the formation of RVB polarons (Fig. 3), show band structures and spin correlations for small $n$ (Fig. 2, Fig. S1), and compare the resulting $\sqrt{3} \times \sqrt{3}$ magnetic order to classical Potts model benchmarks (Fig. S5, Fig. S12).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the polaron physics via ED for small doping ($n=1$ to $5$), showing self-trapping; it then uses DMRG for larger systems ($n\geq 5$) to observe the recovery of delocalized behavior and $\sqrt{3} \times \sqrt{3}$ order in the unpolarized limit, benchmarking this against classical models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

To gain deeper insight into the role of hole kinetics in determining magnetism in highly frustrated doped Mott insulators, we consider the single-hole counter-Nagaoka problem on the kagome lattice, using magnetization as a tuning parameter. Near full polarization, a doped hole delocalizes upon binding reversed spins in a pattern of singlet bonds which we term resonating-valence-bond (RVB) polaron. These RVB polarons can have extremely small effective bandwidths, and hence exhibit self-trapping. By tuning the spin polarization, we track the evolution of these states toward the unpolarized sector, where we observe the emergence of $\sqrt{3}\times\sqrt{3}$ antiferromagnetic correlation reminiscent of the classical Potts and Heisenberg models on the kagome lattice. These results provide a framework to understand how RVB physics at short scales evolves into conventional magnetic correlations at long scales.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12038"></a>
### [Phonons and magnetism of kagome FeGe probed by nuclear resonant scattering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12038v1)

**Authors:** Yu Tang, Saizheng Cao, Sijie Xu, Xiaokun Teng, Sven Velten, Ilya Sergeev, Pengcheng Dai, Yilin Wang, Yu Song  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12038v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5**

**Summary.** The research investigates the complex interplay between charge density waves and magnetism in kagome FeGe using advanced spectroscopic techniques. By measuring phonon spectra and magnetic hyperfine fields across phase transitions, the authors demonstrate that the formation of a CDW significantly alters the lattice structure while probing subtle differences in coupling strength at subsequent magnetic transitions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work exemplifies how lattice dynamics measurements (phonons) can be used as highly sensitive probes for subtle electronic instabilities like CDWs, which is relevant in understanding correlated electron behavior beyond simple band theory descriptions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To investigate the interplay between charge density wave (CDW) order, antiferromagnetic (AFM) order, and lattice dynamics in kagome FeGe.

**Main result.** The CDW state significantly modifies the lattice dynamics by causing phonon hardening, while the AFM order is enhanced below $T_{CDW}$. The coupling between CDW and magnetism appears distinct from the behavior observed at the incommensurate magnetic transition ($T_{IC}$).

**Method.** The study employs $^{57}	ext{Fe}$ Nuclear Inelastic Scattering (NIS) to probe phonon density of states, time-domain Mössbauer spectroscopy to measure hyperfine interactions, and is complemented by specific heat and susceptibility measurements.

**Model / system.** The physical system is kagome FeGe. The analysis probes the material's behavior across multiple electronic/structural transitions ($T_{CDW}$ and $T_{IC}$) which involve coupled spin, charge, and lattice degrees of freedom.

**Key observables.** Phonon density of states (PDOS) hardening at specific energies ($\sim 22	ext{ meV}, 27	ext{ meV}, 31	ext{ meV}$); magnetic hyperfine field ($\vec{B}_{hf}$) enhancement; changes in lattice dynamics parameters like the Debye level.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transition temperatures ($T_{CDW} \approx 115	ext{ K}$, $T_{IC} \approx 65	ext{ K}$); phonon energies (22, 27, 31 meV).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the observed changes in lattice dynamics and magnetism are directly coupled to the formation of the CDW state. DFT calculations provide a theoretical baseline for comparison.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the temperature dependence of the Fe partial PDOS showing hardening across $T_{CDW}$, and show time-domain Mössbauer spectra detailing magnetic hyperfine field changes around both transition temperatures.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes experimental data (NIS, Mössbauer) across different temperature regimes relative to known phase transitions ($T_{CDW}, T_{IC}$). It compares these findings against theoretical DFT calculations to build a comprehensive picture of the coupled order parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Kagome FeGe hosts a $2\times2\times2$ charge-density wave (CDW) that strongly interplays with antiferromagnetic order. Here, we report $^{57}$Fe nuclear resonant scattering measurement to study FeGe across its long-range CDW and incommensurate magnetic transitions. Upon entering the CDW state, hardening of acoustic phonons and optical phonons around 22~meV, 27~meV, and 31~meV are observed in the Fe partial phonon density of states, which can be qualitatively captured by first-principle calculations. Upon entering the incommensurate magnetic phase, neither the phonon density of states nor the hyperfine interaction parameters change significantly, although a subtle feature associated with the incommensurate magnetic order or slow fluctuations is detected in the time-domain Mössbauer spectra. These findings show that the CDW in kagome FeGe significantly modifies its lattice dynamics and magnetism, evidencing an intertwined nature of the spin, charge, and lattice degrees of freedom.

</details>

### [Chiral Magnons and Cycloidal Phonons in Altermagnetic CuF$_{2}$ Monolayer](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11584v1)

**Authors:** Andrea M. León, Matías F. Torreblanca, Carmine Autieri, Jhon W. González  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11584v1>

**Summary.** The research investigates how the underlying crystal symmetries of altermagnetic CuF2 simultaneously govern both its magnetic (magnon) and lattice (phonon) collective excitations. The key finding is that magnon chirality and phonon angular momentum are directionally complementary, confirming a unified symmetry framework controls these distinct physical responses in 2D materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a direct theoretical link between electronic topology (altermagnetism/Chern numbers) and emergent collective excitations (magnons/phonons), which is highly relevant for designing novel spintronic or phononics devices based on symmetry control.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether symmetry principles governing electronic structure (altermagnetism) also govern collective spin and lattice excitations, and if the chiral responses of these two sectors are related.

**Main result.** The study shows that monolayer CuF2 hosts both chirality-split magnons and cycloidal phonons controlled by the same non-symmorphic symmetry operations. Crucially, phonon and magnon chiral responses exhibit a directionally complementary relationship in momentum space.

**Method.** First-principles calculations (VASP) combined with linear spin-wave theory for magnons and Density Functional Perturbation Theory (DFPT) for phonons were employed to analyze the coupled spin and lattice dynamics.

**Model / system.** The system studied is monolayer CuF2, an altermagnetic material whose behavior is dictated by its non-symmorphic crystal symmetry operations ($P2_1/c$). The analysis models both magnetic interactions (exchange, DMI) and lattice vibrations.

**Key observables.** Magnon Chern numbers ($C^M = \pm 2$), phonon angular momentum ($J_{q
u}$), magnon chirality ($\chi_{AB}(\mathbf{k})$), and electronic spin splitting patterns.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The magnetic interactions include isotropic exchange, symmetric anisotropic exchange, and Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interaction. The system is analyzed in the context of $d$-wave altermagnetism.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Phonon calculations were performed without spin-orbit coupling, and the initial magnetic structure (collinear AFM Néel order) breaks pure inversion symmetry despite the crystal structure possessing it.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically summarize the interplay between altermagnetism, cycloidal phonons, and chiral magnons. Other figures illustrate electronic band structures with momentum-dependent spin splittings and phonon dispersion showing angular momentum.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical framework by analyzing how non-symmorphic symmetries govern both magnetic (magnon) and lattice (phonon) excitations in CuF2. It then presents results demonstrating the shared symmetry control, highlighting the complementary nature of their chiral responses and confirming topological properties via Chern numbers.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnetism establishes momentum-dependent spin splitting through non-symmorphic crystal symmetries, yet whether these same symmetries simultaneously govern spin and lattice collective excitations remains open. Here we show, using first-principles calculations and linear spin-wave theory, that monolayer CuF$_2$ hosts both chirality-split magnons and cycloidal phonons controlled by the same $P2_1/c$ symmetry operations. The altermagnetic order drives strongly anisotropic magnon chirality via symmetric anisotropic exchange, with Dzyaloshinskii--Moriya interactions acting as a weak secondary modulation. Crucially, the phonon and magnon chiral responses are directionally complementary: cycloidal phonon angular momentum emerges precisely where magnon chirality is symmetry-suppressed, and vice versa. The magnon bands further carry quantized Chern numbers $C^M = \pm 2$, confirming non-trivial altermagnetic topology. These results establish monolayer CuF$_2$ as a platform where a single symmetry framework engineers magnonic, phononic, and topological responses, providing a direct connection between altermagnetism and spin-lattice chirality in two-dimensional materials.

</details>

### [A semi-definite programming formulation of the device-dependent guessing probability](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12079v1)

**Authors:** Raffaele D'Avino, Aurora Mugnai, Miguel Navascués, Antonio Acín, Gabriel Senno  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12079v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

Eve holds a purification |ψ⟩SME of the joint state ρSM of the system S and the ancillary system ρM inside M.</sub>
