</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces an SDP formulation to calculate the device-dependent guessing probability in prepare-and-measure quantum setups. By reformulating the problem using moment matrices, the authors provide a computationally efficient method to quantify intrinsic randomness. The results demonstrate that assuming ideal or separable conditions can significantly overestimate the true amount of extractable randomness.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, general tool for quantifying fundamental limits on randomness extraction in realistic quantum devices, which is crucial for assessing the security of quantum communication protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work aims to develop a computationally tractable method, specifically using Semidefinite Programming (SDP), to estimate the maximum probability with which an adversary can guess the outcomes in device-dependent prepare-and-measure quantum setups.

**Main result.** The authors successfully formulate this guessing probability as an SDP, providing a complete characterization of the intrinsic randomness extractable from such physical processes. They also show that entanglement between preparation and measurement devices strictly increases Eve's predictive power.

**Method.** The core method involves reformulating a non-convex optimization problem into an equivalent SDP by utilizing generalized moment matrices derived from the quantum state and measurement operators.

**Model / system.** The model is a general prepare-and-measure (PM) setup where Alice prepares a system $S$ in $ho_S$, followed by a POVM $\{M_a^S\}$ on an auxiliary system $M$. Eve possesses side information $E$, and the analysis quantifies her ability to predict measurement outcomes.

**Key observables.** The device-dependent guessing probability ($P_{	ext{guess}}$) and the associated conditional min-entropy ($H_{\min} = -\log_2 P_{	ext{guess}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Noise parameters ($\delta$, $\epsilon$), number of measurement outcomes ($k$), and the degree of entanglement between preparation and measurement devices.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The SDP formulation relies on mapping the problem into moment matrices, which provides sufficient conditions for the existence of the underlying quantum structure (projective measurements).

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the calculated conditional min-entropy ($H_{\min}(A|E)$) from the general SDP against known analytical bounds in various noisy and constrained scenarios. They also illustrate how assuming trusted measurements or separable correlations leads to overestimations of randomness.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the mathematical framework by first defining the problem, then reformulating it using moment matrices ($\Gamma_e$), establishing equivalence via Theorem 1, and finally applying this SDP formulation to benchmark various physical scenarios (depolarizing noise, measurement constraints).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In quantum mechanics, a measurement applied to a state in general produces some amount of intrinsic randomness. This is not only a fundamental feature of the theory, but is also at the basis of any quantum process to generate random numbers. The simplest of such processes consists of a single, fully charaterized, measurement acting on a single, fully characterized, state. Unfortunately, no general method to estimate the intrinsic randomness produced in such setups is known. In this work, we address this issue by presenting a semidefinite programming formulation of the maximum probability with which an adversary, Eve, can guess the outcomes of characterized but untrusted prepare-and-measure setups. We then present several applications of this construction. First, we apply our method to a variety of specific setups, allowing us both to benchmark the approach and, more importantly, to determine the exact amount of certifiable randomness in scenarios where only upper bounds were previously available. Then, we show that the presence of entanglement between the device preparing the state and the measurement strictly increases Eve's predictive power, already in the most elementary setup of a binary measurement acting on a qubit state.

</details>

(15) for zero external potential V (x) = 0, which measures the longitudinal wave-vector component kx. The resulting spectrum consists of eight parabolic bands, with the largest band gap occurring for the K↑and K′↓states. The inclusion of a Zeeman field breaks the valley symmetry and introduces an energy asymmetry between the K and K′ valleys, while the</sub>

**Summary.** The paper theoretically models spin- and valley-resolved quantum transport in WSe2 through a tunable electrostatic barrier. By engineering the Fermi velocity within this barrier, the authors show that the system acts as a powerful quantum filter. This method allows for precise control over spin and valley polarization by manipulating interference conditions, establishing a key framework for 2D material electronics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete example of engineering quantum transport by manipulating internal degrees of freedom (spin/valley) via external fields or geometric parameters (velocity profile), which is highly relevant to advanced spintronics and valleytronics concepts often studied in AMO physics contexts.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates spin- and valley-resolved quantum transport through a finite barrier region in monolayer WSe2.

**Main result.** The work demonstrates that combining velocity modulation (Fermi velocity engineering) and potential barriers allows for the strong, continuous tuning of both the magnitude and orientation of spin-valley polarized currents.

**Method.** The authors solved the time-independent Schrödinger equation using an effective massive Dirac Hamiltonian, enforcing current-conserving matching conditions at interfaces to calculate transmission probabilities and conductance.

**Model / system.** The physical system is monolayer tungsten diselenide (WSe2), modeled by a low-energy effective massive Dirac Hamiltonian that incorporates intrinsic spin-orbit coupling and tunable Zeeman fields. The transport analysis focuses on the quantum tunneling through an electrostatic barrier region where both the scalar potential and Fermi velocity are modulated.

**Key observables.** Spin- and valley-dependent transmission probability ($T_{	au s z}$), conductance ($G_{	au}, G_{s\sigma}$), spin polarization ($P_s$), and valley polarization ($P_v$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Barrier velocity modulation ratio ($\xi = v_2/v_1$), barrier width ($L$), barrier height ($V_0$), incident energy ($E_{	au s z}$), and intrinsic coupling strengths ($\lambda_c, \lambda_v, M_s, M_v$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Edge effects are neglected (assuming translational invariance along the y-axis), and the simple velocity ratio $\xi = v_2/v_1$ is only recovered in the massless, symmetric limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how transmission ($T_{	au s z}$) varies with incident angle ($\phi$), barrier width ($L$), potential height ($V_0$), and critically, the velocity ratio ($\xi$). These plots show resonant tunneling features and Fabry-Pérot oscillations modulated by $\xi$.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops a theoretical framework based on solving the Dirac equation in three regions (Source-Barrier-Drain). It calculates transport quantities using matching boundary conditions, demonstrating how varying parameters like velocity ratio ($\xi$) acts as an effective control mechanism for spin-valley filtering.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We theoretically investigate spin- and valley-resolved quantum transport in monolayer tungsten diselenide (WSe$_2$) described by an effective massive Dirac Hamiltonian. Particular attention is devoted to a finite barrier region characterized by simultaneously modulated Fermi velocity and scalar potential. The barrier velocity $v_2$ is related to the external velocity $v_1$ through a velocity ratio $ξ=v_2/v_1$, motivated by an optical analogy with the Snell-Descartes law. The exact refraction condition depends on the full spin- and valley-resolved dispersion, and the simple ratio $ξ=v_2/v_1$ is recovered only in the massless, symmetric limit. The interplay of intrinsic spin-orbit coupling in the conduction and valence bands, quantified by $λ_c$ and $λ_v$, with spin- and valley-dependent Zeeman fields, $M_s$ and $M_v$, gives rise to substantial changes in the quasiparticle dispersion, leading to pronounced modifications of the transport characteristics. By solving the Dirac equation and enforcing current-conserving matching conditions at the interfaces, we compute the spin- and valley-dependent transmission probability and conductance. Our results demonstrate that the barrier velocity, scalar potential, incidence angle, incident energy, and barrier width serve as effective control parameters for transport, giving rise to strong anisotropy and resonant tunneling features. Furthermore, we show that both the magnitude and orientation of spin- and valley-polarized currents can be continuously tuned via velocity and potential modulation. These findings establish combined velocity and potential engineering as a powerful theoretical framework for controlling spin-valley physics in two-dimensional transition-metal dichalcogenides.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11608"></a>
### [Mach's principle in atomic transitions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11608v1)

**Authors:** Subhajit Barman, Bibhas Ranjan Majhi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11608v1>

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11608_figures/2606.11608_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: In the above figure, we have provided the schematic diagrams for the two different scenarios. On the left, the atom is in circular motion maintaining a radial distance a from the central axis, i.e., the Z−axis. At the same time, the mirror is a static cylinder of radius R0. On the right, the atom is static at a radial distance a from the central axis. In the second scenario, the mirror is cylindrical, with radius R0, and rotates about its central axis.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.11608_figures/2606.11608_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: In the above figure, we have plotted Rex(ω)/g2 and Rex(˜ω)/g2, that respectively represent the atomic transition probability rates corresponding to two different scenarios, with respect to the radial position of the atom a. We choose the maximum value in the numerical sum over n to be 20 and, over m, from −20 to 20, beyond which we do not observe any deviation from the obtained results. We choose other parameters suitably. From both plots, one can notice that the transition rates are qualitatively equivalent. Moreover, in the right plot, when ˜ω = m...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper theoretically examines atomic transitions near a cylindrical mirror under two different dynamic conditions: the atom moving or the mirror rotating. By calculating transition probabilities in both scenarios, the authors demonstrate a structural equivalence between the results when field frequencies are interchanged. This finding is presented as compelling evidence for a quantum mechanical manifestation of Mach's principle.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses fundamental principles like Mach's principle using quantum electrodynamics/field theory in non-inertial frames, which is highly relevant to advanced AMO physics concerning vacuum structure and boundary effects.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates structural similarities in atomic transition probabilities when comparing two scenarios: an atom moving in a static cylindrical mirror versus a static atom near a rotating mirror.

**Main result.** The calculated transition probability rates show structural equivalence upon interchanging the field mode frequencies between the two non-inertial reference frames, suggesting a quantum analogue to Mach's principle.

**Method.** Transition probabilities are calculated using perturbation theory (Dyson series) for an Unruh-DeWitt detector coupled to a scalar quantum field confined by a cylindrical boundary. The analysis involves comparing time-integrated and rate-defined quantities in both static and rotating coordinates.

**Model / system.** The system models an atom interacting with a background real scalar quantum field ($\phi$) whose modes are constrained by a perfect reflecting cylindrical mirror. The comparison is drawn between the case where the atom undergoes circular motion inside a fixed mirror, and the case where the mirror rotates while the atom remains stationary.

**Key observables.** Atomic transition probabilities ($P_{ex}$) and their corresponding rates ($	ext{Re}_{ex}$ or $R_{ex}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Field mode frequencies ($
u$), atomic energy gap ($\omega$), coupling strength ($g$), and the rotational frequency ($\Omega$) or radial position ($a$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the lowest order of coupling strength ($g \ll 1$), and the definition of transition rates is necessary due to potential divergences over infinite time.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 plots the ratio of calculated transition rates ($	ext{Rex}/g^2$) for both the moving-atom/static-mirror case and the static-atom/rotating-mirror case, demonstrating qualitative and quantitative agreement at small atomic radii ($a$).

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical model using an Unruh-DeWitt detector coupled to a field confined by a cylinder. It then calculates transition probabilities for two analogous scenarios (moving atom vs. rotating mirror) in both time-integrated and rate forms, culminating in a comparison that highlights the structural equivalence.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the atomic transition probabilities in atom-mirror set-ups that are in circular motion. In one scenario, the atom is in circular motion inside a static cylindrical mirror. In the other scenario, the cylindrical mirror rotates around its central axis while the atom remains static. We report structural similarity in the atomic transition probabilities between these two cases -- these probabilities are equivalent upon interchanging the field frequencies between the two scenarios. We interpret such an observation as a semi-classical phenomenon analogous to the classical Mach's principle.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Mass generation at a fixed point: A Functional Renormalization Group Study of the tricritical O($N$) model in $d=3$ and $N=\infty$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12269v1)

**Authors:** Shunsuke Yabunaka, bertrand Delamotte  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12269v1>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper uses Functional Renormalization Group methods to study mass generation in the tricritical O(N) model. It demonstrates that at a specific fixed point, the generated mass is nonuniversal and depends on UV initial conditions. Crucially, it reveals a discontinuity in the critical exponent as the system crosses into this singular fixed point regime.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on statistical mechanics, the use of advanced non-perturbative RG techniques (FRG) to study emergent phenomena like dynamical mass generation is highly relevant to understanding quantum field theories underlying many condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates mass generation at a fixed point (FP) in the tricritical O(N) model, specifically addressing how this occurs when standard connections between FPs and scale invariance break down.

**Main result.** The generated mass is shown to be nonuniversal, originating from the nonanalytic structure of the effective potential. Furthermore, the critical exponent $\nu$ exhibits a discontinuous jump at the singular BMB fixed point ($\nu=1/2 \to 1/3$).

**Method.** The analysis heavily relies on the Functional Renormalization Group (FRG) approach, utilizing Wilsonian flow equations and approximations like the Local Potential Approximation (LPA).

**Model / system.** The model is the tricritical O(N) sigma model in three dimensions ($d=3$) analyzed in the large-$N$ limit ($N=\infty$). The system exhibits a line of fixed points parameterized by $\lambda$, terminating at the singular BMB FP.

**Key observables.** Critical exponent $\nu$, effective potential $U_{eff}(\phi)$, and the dimensionful physical mass $M_0$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling constant parameter $\lambda$ (or $\tau$), the dimensionality $d=3$, and the large-$N$ limit ($N=\infty$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the Local Potential Approximation (LPA) for solving the exact flow equation, and in some parts, assumes certain terms become negligible in the $N \to \infty$ limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate potential parameterizations ($\bar{V}$ vs $\bar{ho}$) showing discontinuities at the BMB FP, and track the evolution of potentials and mass squared over RG time to demonstrate non-universal flow.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the FRG framework for the model, then analyzing the regular line of FPs (BMB line), constructing the singular counterpart, and finally using perturbation theory around the BMB FP to derive the mechanism for mass generation and the jump in critical exponents.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Renormalization group (RG) fixed points are commonly associated with scale invariance and a divergent correlation length. We show that this connection can fail in the tricritical $O(N)$ model in three dimensions in the limit $N\to\infty$. Revisiting the line of fixed points identified by Bardeen, Moshe, and Bander, we use the functional renormalization group to clarify the mechanism leading to mass generation at its singular endpoint (the BMB fixed point). We demonstrate that the generated mass is nonuniversal and originates from the nonanalytic structure of the effective potential. We show that the critical exponent $ν$ which takes the value $ν= 1/2$ along the regular part of the BMB line, that is, for $0 \leq λ< λ_{\rm BMB}$, jumps to $ν= 1/3$ on the singular part of this line with the BMB FP, corresponding to $λ= λ_{\rm BMB}$, being the pivotal point between these two regimes. We also show how its singular potential emerges dynamically along the renormalization flow.

</details>

### [Multipartite reference-frame-independent quantum cryptographic communication](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12284v1)

**Authors:** Donghwa Lee, Kyujin Shin, Hyang-Tag Lim, Yosep Kim, Yong-Su Kim  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12284v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum optics experiment` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5**

<sub>FIG. 3. Experimental setups of the 4-parties RFI quantum cryptographic communication using GHZ state. M: mirror, DM: dichroic mirror, H: half waveplate, Q: quarter waveplate, PBS: polarizing beamsplitter, D: detector, SNSPD: superconducting nanowire single photon detector.</sub>

<sub>FIG. 4. The experimental results of the 4-parties RFI quan- tum cryptographic communication using GHZ state. (a) cor- responds to Case a where {0, 0, 0, γD}. (b) corresponds to Case b where {γA, π</sub>

**Summary.** This paper theoretically and experimentally investigates Reference-Frame-Independent (RFI) Quantum Key Distribution using GHZ states across multiple parties. It generalizes the security analysis to N-parties, showing that while noise models affect robustness differently, the final secret key rate remains invariant under reference frame rotations. The work validates this concept with a four-party experimental demonstration.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses robustness in quantum communication protocols, which is crucial for scaling up quantum networks. The mathematical generalization of security parameters to $N$ parties provides theoretical tools for future multi-user quantum internet applications.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Multipartite quantum cryptographic communication is susceptible to errors caused by reference frame mismatches among participating parties, which complicates key distribution as the number of users increases.

**Main result.** The authors derived a generalized security parameter $C_N$ and demonstrated that while some metrics are sensitive to rotation, the asymptotic secret key rate ($R_N$) remains invariant under arbitrary reference frame rotations for N-party GHZ states.

**Method.** The study combines theoretical derivation of key rates based on mutual information with experimental validation using four-photon GHZ states subjected to controlled reference frame rotations.

**Model / system.** The system uses Greenberger-Horne-Zeilinger (GHZ) states distributed among $N$ parties. The analysis models noise using global and local depolarizing channels, while the experiment utilizes photon sources based on PPKTP crystals.

**Key observables.** Multiparty RFI parameter ($C_N$), Quantum Bit Error Rates ($Q_Z$, $Q_X$), and the asymptotic secret key rate ($R_N$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of parties ($N$), global/local depolarizing noise strengths ($\lambda_g, \lambda_l$), and reference frame rotation angles ($\gamma_i$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the ability to estimate noise parameters from measurement statistics in the X and Y bases; experimentally, it relies on generating high-fidelity GHZ states.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the setup with reference frame misalignments. Figure 2 compares key rate robustness under global vs. local noise for varying N. Figure 4 shows measured expectation values of Pauli strings and the invariance of $C_4$ and $R_4$ across different controlled rotations.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the problem of reference-frame mismatch in multipartite QKD, generalizes the security parameter to $N$ parties ($C_N$), derives the key rate formula under various noise models (global/local), and concludes with a proof-of-principle experiment demonstrating four-party RFI communication.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Reference frame mismatch among communication parties introduces errors in quantum cryptographic protocols. As the number of participants increases, aligning reference frames becomes increasingly difficult, complicating multipartite quantum cryptographic implementations. Here, we theoretically and experimentally investigate multipartite reference-frame-independent (RFI) quantum cryptographic communication using Greenberger-Horne-Zeilinger (GHZ) states. We generalize the bipartite RFI security parameter $C$ to an $N$-party parameter $C_N$ and derive the asymptotic secret key rate expressed solely in terms of experimentally accessible quantities. We analyze the key rate under global and local depolarizing noise models and find that increasing the number of parties $N$ enhances robustness against global depolarizing noise while increasing vulnerability to local channel noise. We also present a proof-of-principle experimental demonstration of four-party RFI quantum cryptographic communication using four-photon GHZ states, confirming the reference-frame invariance of both the $C_4$ parameter and the secret key rate under various reference frame rotations.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a novel, non-destructive technique using reflectometry to characterize fiber mirror substrates intended for optical cavities. By measuring back-reflection ($\eta_R$) during fabrication, researchers can accurately pre-select fibers that maintain high mode matching efficiency ($\eta_T > 95\%$). This significantly streamlines the experimental process by reducing reliance on costly post-fabrication characterization.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems because it directly addresses the engineering bottleneck—the coupling efficiency—required for building high-finesse cavities necessary for quantum memory or single-photon sources.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is achieving high collection efficiency ($\eta_T$) in fiber optical cavities by minimizing losses caused by mode mismatch between the cavity mode and the fiber core at the mirror surface.

**Main result.** A simple, non-invasive technique using *in situ* reflectometry can pre-select viable fiber mirror substrates with high coupling efficiencies ($>95\%$), greatly improving experimental yield before expensive coating steps.

**Method.** The authors developed a methodology combining cleaving and $	ext{CO}_2$ laser ablation on fiber tips, characterizing the resulting surface quality using continuous back-reflection measurements ($\eta_R$) via Superluminescent Diodes (SLEDs).

**Model / system.** The system involves an open Fabry-Perot optical cavity formed by cleaved and ablated single-mode fiber ends. The theoretical analysis models the coupling efficiency based on surface imperfections like tilt angle ($	heta$) and radius of curvature ($ho$).

**Key observables.** Collection efficiency ($\eta_T$), measured back-reflection coefficient ($\eta_R$), surface parameters (tilt $	heta$, radius $ho$), and RMS roughness.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Target collection efficiency ($\eta_T > 0.95$); Wavelength of operation (1550 nm).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations for small surface gradients, and the reflectometry measurement is assumed to provide a tight constraint on $\eta_T$, potentially bypassing the need for profilometry measurements of tilt angle.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the geometry relating coupling efficiency and reflection coefficients; one figure shows the experimental setup tracking $\eta_R$ evolution during ablation alongside AFM roughness scans; another plots measured $\eta_R$ versus theoretical parameters ($ho$, $	heta$) to constrain $\eta_T$.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical limitation (mode mismatch) and then introduces the novel experimental technique: using reflectometry *in situ* during fabrication. Theoretical derivations are presented in appendices, culminating in a Bayesian analysis framework to calibrate the measured reflection ($\alpha$) against theoretical maximums ($\eta_{R,max}$).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fiber optical cavities offer small mode volumes and correspondingly strong light-matter interactions in an open Fabry-Perot geometry. However, existing fabrication techniques do not reliably produce substrates with surface profiles amenable to high mode matching between the cavity mode and fiber core, thereby limiting the achievable collection efficiency. Here we present a technique to fabricate fiber mirror substrates while using $\textit{in situ}$ reflectometry to constrain the achievable mode matching prior to coating. By measuring the back-reflection from freshly cleaved fiber tips, we pre-select 138 fibers compatible with 96.5-99.5% mode matching, and after a single CO$_2$ laser ablation pulse, these fibers remained compatible with 95.3-99.2\%. This simple technique provides rapid feedback during each stage of substrate fabrication, greatly enhancing the yield of viable fiber mirror substrates prior to (expensive) coating runs.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11338"></a>
### [Universal critical behavior in ideal Bose-Einstein condensation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11338v1)

**Authors:** Arturo Camacho-Guardian, Leon Kleebank, Frank Vewinger, Martin Weitz, Julian Schmitt, Rosario Paredes, Victor Romero-Rochín  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11338v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11338_figures/2606.11338_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Classification of universal BEC critical behavior as a function of the low-energy scaling behavior of the density of states ρ(ϵ) ∼ϵσ, quantified by σ. Black dots indicate example systems. For σ ≤0 (e.g., Bose gas in 1D or 2D box traps), BEC does not occur at finite temperature (yellow box). For 0 &lt; σ &lt; 1 (Class I, e.g., 3D box), the thermody- namic susceptibilities diverge (blue). The marginal case σ = 1 [Class II, e.g., 2D harmonic oscillator (HO) trap] features log- arithmic corrections to the divergent scaling (gray). For σ &gt; 1 (Class III, e.g. realized in a 3D harmonic trap), only the cor- relation length diverges with the exponent νT = 1/2, while the isothermal compressibility...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper develops a unified theory for critical phenomena in ideal Bose-Einstein condensation. It shows that the nature of the phase transition—and its associated critical exponents—is entirely determined by the low-energy scaling exponent ($\sigma$) of the density of states, which is set by the confining potential's geometry. This classification framework applies broadly to various quantum bosonic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides fundamental scaling laws that unify the understanding of phase transitions across different physical realizations (atoms, photons) by mapping them onto a single parameter ($\sigma$) derived from geometry.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To provide a unified framework for classifying the critical behavior of ideal Bose-Einstein condensation (BEC) based on its thermodynamic singularities.

**Main result.** The critical behavior is classified into three regimes ($\sigma < 1, \sigma = 1, \sigma > 1$) determined solely by the low-energy scaling exponent ($\sigma$) of the single-particle density of states.

**Method.** Analysis involves deriving thermodynamic properties using the grand potential and analyzing asymptotic forms of correlation functions near the critical point.

**Model / system.** The system is an ideal Bose gas, applicable to various condensates (atomic, photonic, polaritonic) confined by potentials $V(\mathbf{r})$. The low-energy physics is governed by the density of states $ho(\epsilon) \sim \epsilon^\sigma$.

**Key observables.** Critical exponents ($\gamma_T, 
u_T, \eta_T$), isothermal compressibility ($\kappa_T$), and correlation length ($\xi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The DOS scaling exponent $\sigma$, which depends on dimensionality ($d$) and confinement potential details (e.g., box vs. harmonic trap).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the Local Density Approximation (LDA) for inhomogeneous systems, and semiclassical methods are used to determine the low-energy DOS.

**Figures summary.** Figures summarize the classification into three universality classes based on $\sigma$, showing specific scaling laws for different trap geometries like box traps ($\sigma = d/2 - 1$) and Harmonic Oscillator (HO) traps ($\sigma = d-1$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the general framework using the DOS exponent $\sigma$ to deriving explicit critical exponents for various thermodynamic quantities, culminating in a comprehensive classification table.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ideal Bose-Einstein condensation (BEC) remains a paradigmatic example of a continuous phase transition and a cornerstone for understanding quantum degenerate bosonic matter. We demonstrate that critical behavior of the ideal Bose gas near the BEC phase transition falls into three distinct classes, determined exclusively by the low-energy scaling of the density of states. Depending on its scaling exponent, which is controlled by dimensionality and confinement, the transition displays either the usual algebraic divergences of thermodynamic susceptibilities, divergent behavior with marginal logarithmic corrections, or a more subtle form of criticality, where only the correlation length diverges. Our work provides a unified framework for criticality in noninteracting bosonic systems. This classification applies broadly to atomic, photonic, polaritonic, and magnonic condensates, where dimensionality, confinement, and spectral engineering can strongly reshape the density of states.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.11343"></a>
### [Fisher geometry reshapes the effect of incompatibility in multiparameter quantum estimation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11343v1)

**Authors:** Jiayu He, Matteo G. A. Paris  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11343v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11343_figures/2606.11343_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Numerical diagnosis of the incompatibility contribution in the three-parameter qutrit model.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.11343_figures/2606.11343_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Variation of G along an approximately fixed-sloppiness slice in the (t, θ) control plane. The probe</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a refined understanding of precision limits in multiparameter quantum estimation by quantifying the joint impact of parameter sensitivity (sloppiness) and measurement non-commutativity (incompatibility). It introduces a key diagnostic factor, $G$, showing that how incompatibility is distributed relative to the system's natural geometry dictates the final achievable precision bound. This moves beyond simple scalar measures to provide a deeper geometric understanding of quantum sensing limitations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides advanced tools to quantify resource limitations in quantum sensing beyond simple measures like total Fisher information or total incompatibility strength. The focus on geometric alignment makes it highly relevant for designing optimal measurement strategies.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses how two fundamental obstacles—sloppiness (QFIM anisotropy) and incompatibility (non-commutativity of optimal measurements)—jointly limit precision in multiparameter quantum estimation.

**Main result.** The distribution of incompatibility relative to the Fisher eigenbasis is a crucial diagnostic, showing that concentrating incompatibility can reduce the cost if the Fisher geometry allows for reshaping. The penalty depends on this geometric alignment factor $G$, not just the total incompatibility strength.

**Method.** The authors derive explicit upper bounds and analyze the structure of the trade-off bound by introducing dimensionless quantities like $G(\mathbf{F})$ that measure the alignment between incompatibility and QFIM eigenvalues.

**Model / system.** The analysis is performed on general quantum statistical models, using both pure-state (QGT) and mixed-state formulations involving the Symmetric Logarithmic Derivative (SLD). A concrete test case uses a qutrit SU(2) encoding for three parameters $(B, 	heta, \phi)$.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information Matrix ($Q_{\mu
u}$), Uhlmann Curvature ($U_{\mu
u}$), Sloppiness measure ($s = 1/\det Q$), and the dimensionless matching factor $G(\mathbf{F})$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of parameters ($n=2, 3$, general $n \ge 4$) and the relative strength/placement of incompatibility components.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on deriving explicit upper bounds for the Holevo bound and assumes certain structural properties (e.g., diagonalizability) when simplifying the cost function in low dimensions.

**Figures summary.** Figures numerically confirm that the penalty term $C_T - C_S$ depends critically on the matching factor $G$, demonstrating that increasing total incompatibility ($\sqrt{c}$) does not guarantee a higher cost if $G$ is small.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the joint impact of sloppiness and incompatibility, deriving bounds using geometric tensors (QGT/SLD), introducing the diagnostic factor $G(\mathbf{F})$, proving structural results for low dimensions ($n=2, 3$), and finally testing these findings numerically on a qutrit model.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multiparameter quantum estimation faces two fundamental obstacles: sloppiness, i.e., anisotropy of the quantum Fisher information matrix (QFIM) that renders some parameter directions insensitive, and incompatibility, the non-commutativity of optimal measurements for different parameters. The trade-off bound $C_T$ captures their joint impact on precision, but it has remained unclear how the distribution of incompatibility across parameter planes affects its overall cost. Here we separate the total amount of incompatibility from its location. We introduce a dimensionless quantity $G_n^{(F)}$ that measures the alignment between the incompatibility distribution and the eigenvalues of the QFIM, and show how the Frobenius scale of the incompatibility contribution factorizes. We obtain a bound and prove the incompatibility cost lies between this bound and a rank-dependent multiple thereof. We also prove that at fixed sloppiness, or equivalently fixed Fisher volume, concentrating incompatibility into a single parameter plane reduces the optimized trade-off cost because the Fisher geometry can then be reshaped to allocate more Fisher area to that plane. A qutrit $SU(2)$ encoding numerically confirms that states with larger incompatibility strength can nevertheless incur a smaller cost if the matching factor $G$ is sufficiently small. Our results establish that the distribution of incompatibility relative to the Fisher eigenbasis is a central diagnostic for multiparameter estimation, beyond the total incompatibility strength.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.11557"></a>
### [Emergent mirror symmetry in the optimization of the central-spin quantum battery](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11557v1)

**Authors:** Hui-Yu Yang, Kun Zhang, Xiao-Hui Wang, Hai-Long Shi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11557v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<sub>Figure 4 conﬁrms this prediction by comparing the symmetry-selected protocol with the fully polarized bench- mark. **Summary.** This paper investigates the optimization of energy storage in a central-spin quantum battery model. By analyzing two structural indicators related to charging dynamics, the authors find that maximum efficiency is achieved when the system's effective Hamiltonian becomes exactly mirror symmetric. This establishes emergent symmetry as a key organizing principle for maximizing performance in interacting quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work connects abstract mathematical concepts (emergent symmetry) directly to measurable physical quantities (charging power/energy), providing a powerful theoretical tool for designing and understanding optimal operation regimes in complex quantum many-body systems relevant to AMO physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to optimize the charging efficiency of a quantum battery by identifying structural indicators that govern energy storage dynamics.

**Main result.** Joint optimization of two structural indicators ($\Omega(n)$ and $\Lambda(n)$) occurs at an initial charger excitation number ($n^*$) where the effective charging Hamiltonian exhibits exact mirror symmetry. This emergent symmetry is shown to be a useful principle for maximizing charging performance.

**Method.** The authors derived upper bounds on average charging power and stored energy using structural indicators related to hopping profiles, followed by optimization techniques (AM-GM inequality) to find the optimal initial state.

**Model / system.** The system is a central-spin quantum battery model consisting of $N_b$ central spins coupled to $N_c$ bath spins. The dynamics are analyzed within an invariant subspace defined by the charger's Dicke state sector, governed by a specific interaction Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Average charging power ($ar{P}(	au)$), stored energy ($E(	au)$), and two structural indicators: $\Omega(n)$ (upper bound on power) and $\Lambda(n)$ (stored energy buildup characterization).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The optimal excitation number $n^*$, which is found to be near $(N_b + N_c)/2$. The analysis also considers the large $N_c$ limit for scaling laws.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on bounding techniques (Hölder, AM-GM) and approximates the optimal Dicke state dynamics using product initial states like spin coherent states.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate that both stored energy and average power are maximized at $n^*$, confirming the physical relevance of the symmetry selection. Figure 4 specifically shows the scaling of the optimal charging time ($	au \propto 1/N_c$).

**Paper structure.** The paper first defines the quantum battery model and its associated structural indicators. It then mathematically derives upper bounds for performance metrics using these indicators. The core is the optimization step, showing that maximizing both indicators simultaneously selects $n^*$, which in turn enforces mirror symmetry on the effective Hamiltonian.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum batteries provide a useful setting for exploring nonequilibrium many-body effects in energy storage. Here we investigate the optimization of a quantum battery based on the central-spin model. We identify two complementary structural indicators associated with the effective charging dynamics: one yields an upper bound on the average charging power, while the other characterizes the buildup of stored energy. We show that these two indicators are jointly optimized at a distinguished initial charger excitation number, which selects a particular Dicke sector of the model. At this common optimal point, the effective charging Hamiltonian becomes exactly mirror symmetric, suggesting mirror symmetry as a useful structural indicator for optimizing quantum batteries. We further show that the corresponding optimal dynamics can be closely approximated by product initial states, in particular by spin coherent states whose excitation-number distribution is centered at the symmetry-selected point. Our results establish a direct connection between charging performance, optimal-state structure, and emergent symmetry in the central-spin quantum battery, and suggest symmetry as a useful organizing principle for efficient charging in interacting many-body quantum systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11438"></a>
### [Isotropic random walks and Brownian diffusion on complex projective space](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11438v1)

**Authors:** Gyula I. Tóth  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11438v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.11438_figures/2606.11438_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

**Summary.** The paper introduces an isotropic random walk model on the complex projective space to study quantum state evolution geometrically. By analyzing this process, it derives explicit formulas for fidelity decay and calculates the mean time required to reach a target state. This framework offers a powerful, solvable reference model for understanding decoherence dynamics in high-dimensional quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides powerful geometric tools—solvable stochastic processes on curved manifolds—to analyze fundamental concepts in quantum information like scrambling and decoherence without needing specific Hamiltonian details. The dimension-dependent divergence of $m^*$ is particularly insightful for understanding state preparation difficulty.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop an analytically tractable stochastic-geometric framework for understanding quantum state space exploration, particularly in the context of decoherence.

**Main result.** The isotropic random walk on complex projective space provides a solvable model that reproduces Haar-random fidelity statistics and yields analytical expressions for first-passage times. The mean time to reach high-fidelity states diverges strongly with dimension due to geometric concentration effects.

**Method.** The analysis combines harmonic analysis on compact rank-one symmetric spaces with the study of random walks, which are shown to converge to Brownian diffusion governed by the Fubini–Study Laplace–Beltrami operator in the short/long time limits.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled as an isotropic random walk on the complex projective space ($\mathbb{CP}^{n-1}$), which represents the pure quantum state space. This approach serves as a geometric reference model for open quantum evolution, bypassing the need to specify a microscopic Lindblad generator.

**Key observables.** Transition kernels, fidelity statistics ($F$), mean first passage time ($m^*$), and expectation values of geometric observables related to the Fubini–Study metric.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension $n$ (related to the Hilbert space dimension), the number of steps $m$, and the target fidelity $F^*$ are crucial parameters governing convergence rates and divergence behavior.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes an isotropic, Markovian evolution on $\mathbb{CP}^{n-1}$ and relies on approximating the random walk by Brownian diffusion in certain limits to solve for first passage times.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the general framework using harmonic analysis on symmetric spaces, derives transition probabilities via Jacobi polynomials, analyzes the short-time convergence to heat kernel/Brownian motion, and finally solves the mean first passage time problem by reducing it to a boundary value problem involving the killed Laplacian.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that isotropic random walks on the complex projective space provide a canonical and analytically tractable stochastic-geometric framework for the exploration of quantum-state space. The approach combines harmonic analysis on compact rank-one symmetric spaces with stochastic pure-state evolution and yields explicit analytical expressions for transition kernels, fidelity statistics, and geometric observables associated with the Fubini--Study metric. In particular, the framework provides a solvable reference model for isotropic depolarization and Haar equilibration, reproducing Haar-random fidelity statistics and the invariant measure on projective Hilbert space without specifying a microscopic Lindblad generator. In the short-time regime, the stochastic evolution converges to Brownian diffusion generated by the Fubini--Study Laplace--Beltrami operator, while the long-time limit exhibits concentration-of-measure behaviour characteristic of high-dimensional random quantum states. We further derive analytical and asymptotic results for the first-passage-time problem, including closed-form expressions in the Brownian limit for the mean first passage time and the long-time tail of the first-passage-time distribution. For high-fidelity target states, the mean first passage time exhibits a strong dimension-dependent divergence originating from the concentration properties of the Fubini--Study geometry.

</details>

### [Mean-field limits for stochastic particle systems on dense graphs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11369v1)

**Authors:** Angeliki Koutsimpela, Elena Magnanini  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11369v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11369_figures/2606.11369_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: From left to right: the step graphons W10 and W25, followed by the limiting continuous graphon W. The parameters used for the cut-off χ are a = 0.35 and b = 0.65. Additionally we used α = 3, β = 2.5, and γ = 0.3. As L →∞, the step kernels converge to the limiting graphon, preserving the underlying community structure.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.11369_figures/2606.11369_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

The graphon assigns a larger interaction intensity to ordered pairs (u, v) with u &lt; v than to pairs with u &gt; v.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.11369_figures/2606.11369_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Core–periphery interaction kernels with parameters a = 0.2, b = 0.45, and θ = 2. From left to right: the step graphons W10 and W25, followed by the limiting continuous graphon W. The region near u = 0 represents the core and has stronger interactions, while the region near u = 1 represents the periphery.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper rigorously analyzes the mean-field limit of stochastic particle systems defined on large, dense graphs. It proves that the system's evolution converges to a deterministic nonlinear master equation whose structure is dictated by the limiting graphon. This framework allows for modeling complex, spatially heterogeneous dynamics in the thermodynamic limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides rigorous mathematical tools for analyzing complex, non-equilibrium dynamics in many-body systems where interactions are spatially structured but approach a continuum limit, which is relevant for modeling interacting quantum matter or open quantum systems with structured environments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study focuses on deriving the mean-field limit for stochastic interacting particle systems whose interactions are defined by dense graphs converging to a graphon.

**Main result.** The empirical measure process converges weakly to a deterministic nonlinear master equation governed by the limiting graphon, capturing spatially non-homogeneous dynamics.

**Method.** The analysis employs techniques from probability theory, including establishing tightness via Prohorov's theorem and deriving convergence results through moment bounds and weak convergence arguments in function spaces like D([0, T], P(...)).

**Model / system.** The system consists of stochastic particles undergoing monomer-exchange on a graph $G_L$ whose structure converges to a graphon $W$. The dynamics are tracked via the empirical measure $\pi_L(t)$ in the thermodynamic limit.

**Key observables.** Empirical measure process $\pi_L(t)$, its weak limit $\pi(t)$, and derived density functions $p_t(u, k)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The system size limit ($L 	o \infty$), the particle density ($ho$), and the interaction structure defined by the graphon $W$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Convergence of weighted graphs to a graphon (Assumption 1), boundedness of rates, and initial moment bounds are required for convergence theorems.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates community formation using step graphons converging to a continuous graphon; Figure 2 shows directed interaction kernels where $W(u, v) 
eq W(v, u)$.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from establishing moment bounds (Prop 2.1), proving tightness (Prop 2.2), and finally deriving the Law of Large Numbers (Theorem 2.3) leading to the nonlinear master equation for the limit process.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study stochastic interacting particle systems whose interaction structure is described by dense weighted directed graphs converging to a graphon. In the thermodynamic limit, we prove a law of large numbers for the empirical measure process and derive a deterministic nonlinear master equation describing the macroscopic evolution. The limiting equation retains the heterogeneous interaction structure of the microscopic system through the limiting graphon, allowing for spatially non-homogeneous behaviors such as localized or community-type interactions.

</details>

### [Scaling-optimal purification of noisy qubit unitary channels](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12394v1)

**Authors:** Ryotaro Niwa, Satoshi Yoshida, Koki Ono, Takeru Utsumi, Zhaoyi Li, Yuxiang Yang, Ryuji Takagi, Mio Murao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12394v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.12394_figures/2606.12394_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

For odd integer n = 2k −1, we define λ0 := (k, k − 1), and |Φ+ mλ0 ⟩:= 1 √mλ0 Pmλ0 i=1 |i⟩⊗|i⟩. The unitary USch</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.12394_figures/2606.12394_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. The red dots show the optimal fidelity ef Seq 3,2 (p). Our numerics suggest that the optimal fidelity is achieved by a parallel strategy: ef Seq 3,2 (p) = ef Par 3,2 (p). The black dashed line shows the non-purified trivial fidelity.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper tackles the challenging problem of purifying unknown unitary channels corrupted by noise. It rigorously compares sequential and parallel error correction strategies using SDPs and QECC bounds. The key finding is that while finite uses show protocol differences, asymptotically, both methods achieve the same optimal scaling for noise suppression.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides deep insights into the fundamental limitations of quantum channel estimation and purification protocols, particularly clarifying when resource constraints (like sequential vs. parallel use) matter versus when scaling laws dominate in the low-noise limit.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is purifying noisy qubit unitary channels, meaning constructing a superchannel that recovers an unknown original unitary $U$ despite repeated applications of the channel corrupted by depolarizing noise.

**Main result.** While sequential strategies show a finite-use advantage over parallel ones, the analysis proves that in the low-noise limit ($p \ll 1$), both protocols achieve asymptotically optimal noise suppression scaling ($\Theta(1/n)$).

**Method.** The problem is formulated and analyzed using Semidefinite Programming (SDP) techniques, comparing lower bounds derived from concrete entanglement-assisted Quantum Error-Correcting Codes (QECCs) against upper bounds derived from quantum metrological arguments.

**Model / system.** The system involves qubit unitary channels subjected to depolarizing noise ($N_{U,p} = D_p \circ U$). The analysis is performed for $d=2$ qubits and generalizes the concept of channel purification beyond simple state purification.

**Key observables.** Channel fidelity ($f_{	ext{Choi}}$) quantifying how close the output superchannel approximates the original unknown unitary, and the performance operator $\Omega_{n,p}$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of uses ($n$), depolarizing noise strength ($p$), and system dimension ($d=2$). The analysis focuses heavily on the low-noise limit ($p \ll 1$) and the asymptotic scaling $n 	o \infty$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumptions involve analyzing the process in the low-noise regime, using specific algebraic tools like Schur-Weyl duality for constraints, and comparing two distinct operational strategies: sequential versus parallel channel uses.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the performance comparison between sequential and parallel protocols; Table I provides numerical optimal fidelities for $d=2$ at small $n$, showing initial divergences that converge asymptotically.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the problem via SDP formulation, then providing numerical evidence distinguishing finite-$n$ strategies, followed by deriving rigorous lower and upper bounds using QECCs and QFI arguments to establish asymptotic optimality.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider the problem of purifying noisy qubit unitary channels. Given the ability to apply an unknown qubit unitary channel followed by depolarizing noise, we aim to construct a superchannel that purifies the noisy unitary back to the original unknown unitary. We first provide numerical evidence that sequential strategies can strictly outperform parallel strategies when the number of channel uses is finite, highlighting the fundamental distinction from state purification. We then provide a concrete $\mathrm{U}(2)$-covariant parallel protocol based on a novel entanglement-assisted quantum error-correcting code that suppresses the first-order noise strength as $O(1/n)$ with $n$ channel uses and show this scaling is asymptotically optimal in the low-noise regime, even when sequential strategies are allowed.

</details>

### [A stochastic model for elastoplastic contact of rough surfaces incorporating scale-dependent hardness](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11765v1)

**Authors:** Yang Xu, Hengxu Song, Jianqiao Hu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11765v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<sub>Figure 4: Convergence test of linear elastic contact solutions solved by Chapman-Kolmogorov (C-K) equation (solid lines) and Persson’s theory (dashed lines): (a) relative contact area A∗, (b) P0(p/ p</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.11765_figures/2606.11765_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Convergence test of elastoplastic contact solutions with constant hardness solved by Chapman-Kolmogorov (C-K) equation (solid lines) and Persson’s theory (dashed lines): (a) relative elastic contact area A∗ el, (b) relative plastic contact area A∗ pl, (c) P0(p/H0, ζ). The magnification axis is discretized by various nζ. The mean pressure ¯p = 1.5 GPa, H0 = 3 GPa, C0 = 1 × 10−8 mm4, ql = 2π mm−1, H = 0.7, E = 200 GPa, ν = 0.33, ∆p0 = H0/99.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper pioneers a stochastic model for elastoplastic contact on rough surfaces by incorporating the crucial effect of scale-dependent hardness. It uses compounded Chapman-Kolmogorov equations to track how probability distributions governing contact pressure and area evolve across different observation scales. The resulting framework provides advanced diagnostics to map out the transition between linear elastic, elastoplastic, and fully plastic regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on solid mechanics, the use of stochastic process theory, probability density function evolution across scales (Chapman-Kolmogorov), and multi-scale modeling techniques are highly relevant to non-equilibrium statistical physics problems in AMO or condensed matter systems where scale separation is key.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses stress concentrations caused by inherent surface roughness leading to plastic deformation at contact interfaces, complicated by size effects and self-affine topography.

**Main result.** The authors developed a stochastic framework using compounded Chapman-Kolmogorov equations to model the evolution of probability density functions describing elastic, plastic, and non-contact areas across geometrical scales.

**Method.** The methodology involves formulating and solving three coupled integral equations derived from the compounded Chapman-Kolmogorov equation, which tracks probability flow as the observation scale changes.

**Model / system.** The system is elastoplastic contact between rough surfaces. The model incorporates a novel element: scale-dependent hardness, $H(\zeta)$, which modifies the standard contact mechanics framework by introducing size effects into the yield criterion.

**Key observables.** Probability density functions (PDFs) for elastic contact pressure, relative plastic contact area ($A^*_{pl}$), and relative non-contact area ($A^*_{nc}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Scale-dependent hardness $H(\zeta)$, magnification $\zeta$, topographic yield parameter ($\omega$), Hurst exponent ($H$), and hardness exponent ($n$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model builds upon stochastic process theory, though it notes that previous assumptions like 'no re-entry' are inaccurate in practice. Numerical solutions rely on discretizing the scale (magnification) axis.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate probability flow for both constant and scale-dependent hardness, showing reopened paths due to size effects. Contour plots map contact status regimes based on misfit parameters ($M_{le}, M_{fp}$), comparing results against prior literature.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical problem (rough surface elastoplasticity) to developing the core mathematical tool (compounded Chapman-Kolmogorov equation). It then details the derivation of integral equations, presents numerical solutions for various hardness regimes, and concludes by defining diagnostic parameters to map out contact status zones.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The stress concentrations caused by inherent roughness of natural and manufactured surfaces often induce plastic deformation at contact interfaces, a challenge compounded by competing influences of the size effect of plastic deformation and self-affine rough surface topography. To address this, we developed a novel methodology based on stochastic theory using compounded Chapman-Kolmogorov equations, for the first time, to solve elastoplastic contact problems involving scale-dependent hardness. Our approach formulates three integral equations describing the evolution of probability density functions of elastic contact pressure, relative plastic contact area, and relative non-contact area across geometrical scales. We thoroughly investigate the effects of scale-dependent hardness on contact pressure distribution, relative elastic and plastic contact areas, and the area-to-load relationship. By adjusting various mechanical and material properties, our model predicts a smooth transition from linear elasticity to elastic-plastic behavior and finally to full plasticity. A key advancement is the derivation of a new topographic yield parameter incorporating a wider range of material and geometrical properties, aiding identification of contact status. Numerical solutions enable highly precise determination of elastic and plastic limits via curve-fitting, and we also provide a new diagram for rapid identification of contact status. This study pioneers a stochastic process framework for applying the compounded Chapman-Kolmogorov equation to rough surface contact analysis, and the integral equations characterizing how interfacial properties evolve with scale could offer valuable insights for other multidisciplinary fields where multiscale roughness is critical, such as earthquakes, electrical contact, and contact electrification.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.12049"></a>
### [Searching for cosmic vortices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12049v1)

**Authors:** Marek Nikołajuk, Tomasz Karpiuk, Mirosław Brewczyk  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12049v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.12049_figures/2606.12049_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Bosonic density (left column) and quantum phase (right column) at the z = 0 plane for different time instants. The density and time are in units of 1/a3 B and mBa2 B/ℏ, re- spectively. The positions of the positive and negative quan- tized vortices are marked by red/yellow plus signs and blue minus signs, respectively. Initially, there are no vortices asso- ciated with the white dwarf (top frame), although many are visible in the background noise. Later on, the white dwarf drags some vortices into an accretion disc that is forming (middle frame). <details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

**Summary.** This theoretical study models the tidal disruption of a helium white dwarf by a black hole, treating the matter as a Bose-Fermi droplet governed by quantum hydrodynamics. The key findings are that quantized vortices generate distinct electromagnetic flickering patterns in the resulting accretion disc, and these same vortices cause the escaping white dwarf to emit detectable gravitational waves.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work applies quantum hydrodynamic techniques, typically used for ultracold atomic systems (Bose-Fermi droplets), to extreme astrophysical environments, linking fundamental quantum phenomena (vortices) to observable signals like GWs and EM radiation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study searches for cosmic vortices generated during the tidal disruption of a cold helium white dwarf passing near a black hole, and investigates the resulting electromagnetic radiation and gravitational wave emission.

**Main result.** Vortices generate characteristic flickering electromagnetic radiation signals in the accretion disc, and their subsequent propagation along the escaping white dwarf's surface causes measurable gravitational wave emission.

**Method.** The authors use quantum hydrodynamic equations to model the binary system evolution, simulating both electromagnetic power spectra and gravitational wave strain.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a cold helium white dwarf modeled as a Bose-Fermi droplet interacting with a black hole. The dynamics are governed by coupled quantum hydrodynamic equations for bosonic and fermionic components using pseudo-Newtonian potentials.

**Key observables.** Electromagnetic radiation flickering patterns (Power Spectral Density, $P_{	ext{dip}}$), the ratio of bosonic to fermionic dipole power ($P_B^{	ext{dip}}/P_F^{	ext{dip}}$), and gravitational wave strain ($h_+, h_	imes$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Timescales of a few seconds for flickering; GW frequencies around 1 Hz; BH modeled as non-rotating (Schwarzschild metric).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The fermionic density follows the bosonic one ($ar{n}_F = ar{n}_B$), and the black hole is assumed to be non-rotating.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the process from initial symmetry breaking through vortex dragging into the accretion disc, showing correlations between radiation spikes and vortex presence, and demonstrating the transition of the Power Spectral Density (PSD) from $1/f$ to $1/f^2$.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a logical progression: first modeling the initial tidal disruption and vortex generation in the accretion disc (EM signals); second, analyzing the electromagnetic radiation signatures linked to these vortices; and finally, examining the gravitational wave emission from the departing, vortex-laden white dwarf.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Our study focuses on the strong tidal disruption of a cold helium white dwarf passing a black hole. We model the white dwarf as a Bose-Fermi droplet and use quantum hydrodynamic equations to simulate the binary system's evolution. As the white dwarf passes through periastron, it loses a significant amount of mass. This mass falls onto the black hole and forms an accretion disc. Quantized vortices appear in the accretion disc, manifesting as strong electromagnetic radiation signals that exhibit characteristic flickering patterns changing on a timescale of a few seconds. Meanwhile, the white dwarf moves away from the black hole. As the white dwarf moves through space, vortices run along its surface. This elongates its geometry, causing it to rotate and emit gravitational waves.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11527"></a>
### [Invariants of Sequential Circuits and Generalized Non-Abelian Statistics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11527v1)

**Authors:** Shintaro Sato, Yoshimasa Hidaka, Ryohei Kobayashi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11527v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11527_figures/2606.11527_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. A sequential circuit V (Σ, a; D, D′) is supported at a region Σ. The corresponding symmetry generator is labeled by a (generally an object of a symmetry category), and trans- forms a defect network.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.11527_figures/2606.11527_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. We break a sequential circuit V (Σ, a; D, D′) into small fractions {Lr′}, so that each fraction is contained in a disk Dr of radius r (red disk). A process of moving a defect breaks into D →D1 →...Dn →D′ via intermediate configurations. Each edge e of the fine network supports an MPS state |ψ⟩µν e , which constitutes a 1d state localized along the defect. Each local phase factor θ(Lr′, a; Dj|Lr′ , Dj+1|Lr′ ) cancels out due to the locality constraints on integer coefficients ϵ.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

</details>
