<a id="paper-2606.12310"></a>
### [Partitioned Iterative Quantum Scheduling of Satellites for Urgent Disaster Response: Case study of Wildfire](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12310v1)

**Authors:** Lucas T. Braydwood, Taejin Park, Hirofumi Hashimoto, Zoe Gonzalez Izquierdo, Andrew Michaelis, Eleanor Rieffel, Shon Grabbe  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12310v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.12310_figures/2606.12310_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: (a) Sample GOES-16 FDC imagery from Aug. 18, 2020 at 00:01 UTC. Red denotes an active fire area, green regions with no fire, yellow clouds, and blue water. (b) Corre- sponding WUI image from the U.S. Forest Service [29]. Yellow and orange indicate wildland urban interface and intermix, re- spectively. No housing indicated in dark green and very low housing density shown in medium green. Low and very low housing density in light green, medium and high housing den- sity in maroon, and water indicated in blue.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.12310_figures/2606.12310_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Lines are orbital tracks in one-second increments for CARTOSAT-2e (blue line), KAZEOSAT-1 (yellow line), and HYSIS (green line) on July 25, 2023. Orbital tracks have been synchronized to pass over the CA coast in unison. Red filled polygons are the intersection of GOES FireTracks data from July 19 – Sept 28, 2020 with the WUI low/med/high data. Rectangles are sample, notional field of views (FOVs).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.12310_figures/2606.12310_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Simulation results for three different satellites, using the classical algorithms MIN and partitioned MIN as well as two quantum algorithms, both partitioned. The y axis represents the achieved reward of the algorithm divided by the number of requests, with a value of 1 corresponding to satisfying every request from an optimal angle. The data is averaged over all instances for the indicated satellites with higher request simulations coming from longer time bins.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper tackles the computationally hard problem of scheduling satellites for wildfire monitoring by formulating it as a Maximum Independent Set problem. It proposes using advanced quantum computing paradigms, including iterative and distributed quantum algorithms, to solve this resource allocation challenge. While current simulations show classical methods leading, the methodology advances the theoretical path toward solving utility-scale problems on future quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** The focus on mapping complex combinatorial optimization problems onto structured quantum Hamiltonians ($\hat{C}$) and developing strategies (partitioning, iteration) to handle system sizes beyond current hardware capabilities is highly relevant to the theory of quantum algorithms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing efficient quantum algorithms to solve the NP-hard problem of scheduling satellite observations for near real-time wildfire tracking.

**Main result.** The work validates the utility of iterative and distributed quantum techniques for this resource allocation problem, despite current hardware limitations preventing a demonstrated quantum advantage over classical solvers.

**Method.** The scheduling problem is mapped to finding the Maximum Independent Set (MIS) on a graph, which is then formulated as a QUBO/Ising model suitable for QAOA or iterative quantum algorithms.

**Model / system.** The system involves coordinating large constellations of satellites observing wildfire targets derived from GOES-16 multi-spectral data. The computational model uses MIS formulation and explores partitioned/distributed quantum processing units (QPUs).

**Key observables.** Achieved Reward/Number of Requests; Expectation values ($\langle \sigma^z_i angle$); Fire size, Fire Radiative Power (FRP).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lagrange multiplier ($\lambda=2$); Partition size ($m$, limited to $\sim 18$ qubits in simulations); Number of satellites/requests.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The current quantum subprocesses are too small to show significant quantum advantage; the scheduling problem is modeled by mapping constraints onto an MIS graph structure.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate sample GOES-16 fire detection overlaid with WUI data, and compare simulation results of classical greedy vs. partitioned MIN/MINQ/MMQ across varying request numbers.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the real-world scheduling challenge (wildfire tracking) to formulating it as an MIS problem, then detailing quantum approaches like QAOA and iterative algorithms (MINQ/MMQ), culminating in a methodology for partitioning large problems into manageable sub-problems for distributed computation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The standard in Earth-observation tasks today is having near real-time access to surface images in response to changing conditions. For instance, as urban environments interface more with wildlands and wildfires become less predictable, their tracking with satellite resources becomes essential. This requires the coordination of increasingly large constellations of satellites, giving rise to challenging computational problems. With wildfire detection and tracking as a backdrop, we investigate the power of special purpose and novel computing paradigms to tackle the ensuing satellite scheduling problems, making a compelling case for quantum algorithms. We bring quantum scheduling algorithms closer to implementation by examining both the emerging iterative quantum algorithm framework, which comes with analytic guarantees compared to some classical algorithms, and distributed quantum computing methods whose relevance is on the rise as utility-scale problems begin to get solved with quantum computers. Drawing strength from several computing fronts, we develop a distributed/parallelization scheme in conjunction with the quantum algorithm design and apply these techniques to real-world datasets for wildfire detection. While our quantum subprocesses are currently too small to see significant quantum advantage, our results validate the utility of these techniques, and continue forging the path toward distributed quantum computing.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11759"></a>
### [Random Grover Search](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11759v1)

**Authors:** Dekuan Dong, Jiaxin Ma, Yingzhou Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11759v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11759_figures/2606.11759_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Partition of the search space into the regions Ij induced by A0, A1, A2.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.11759_figures/2606.11759_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: Projected trajectories onto the (ba3(t), ba0(t))-plane under the averaged evolu- tion matrix M for different values of N, with fixed r = 1, |A0| = 200, and |A1| = 100. The dashed curves represent quarter circles of radius 1, while the solid curves denote the projected trajectories.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.11759_figures/2606.11759_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Comparison between the deterministic periodic sequence and the correspond- ing randomized strategy under four parameter settings. In each panel, the solid curve represents the deterministic sequence, while the dashed curve shows the empirical mean over 50 independent randomized trials. The vertical dotted line marks the stopping time T, and the dash-dotted vertical line marks the fist peak time t∗ det of the deterministic curve. The corresponding values of t∗ det are 2.6 × 106, 1.8 × 106, 1.3 × 106, and 0.7 × 106 for the four panels, respectively.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.11759_figures/2606.11759_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Success-probability evolution for different values of δ, with fixed N = 240, |A1| = 100, and r = 1. The solid curves show the empirical averages over 50 indepen- dent trials, while the dashed curve corresponds to repeatedly applying only G0. The vertical dotted line marks the stopping time T.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.11759_figures/2606.11759_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5: Numerical results for N = 240, |A0| = 100, |A1| = 200, |A2| = 50, r = 1, and δ ∈{0.005, 0.15, 0.4}. (a) Empirical mean success probability for different values of δ. (b) Corresponding mean usage ratios of G0, G1, and G2.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a randomized Grover search algorithm designed to solve problems defined by the intersection of multiple constraints without needing a single, costly global oracle. By randomly applying individual constraint-specific Grover operators, the authors prove that the algorithm maintains the optimal quadratic speedup complexity. This is significant because it offers a practical pathway for implementing quantum searches on complex, modularly constrained systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful algorithmic tool for quantum computation that bypasses major hardware/implementation bottlenecks associated with constructing complex global oracles by leveraging random sampling over simpler components.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research addresses the challenge of quantum search algorithms for finding solutions that belong to the intersection of multiple constraint sets, where constructing a single global oracle is computationally expensive.

**Main result.** A randomized Grover search algorithm was developed that achieves the same asymptotic query complexity as standard Grover search ($\Theta(\sqrt{N/r})$) without requiring a costly global oracle.

**Method.** The method involves stochastically applying randomly selected individual Grover operators corresponding to each constraint set at every iteration, and analyzing the resulting expected quantum evolution.

**Model / system.** The model is based on Grover's algorithm applied to search spaces defined by multiple intersecting constraints. The state space is analyzed using a basis derived from membership in these individual constraint sets.

**Key observables.** Success probability ($P_{	ext{succ}}(t)$), which measures the likelihood of measuring an element within the target intersection set, and the required number of iterations ($T$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** N (total search space size), r (size of the intersection), k (number of constraint sets/operators), and the sampling distribution probabilities $\{p_i\}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximating the expected Grover evolution using auxiliary operators, and certain conditions like $N > (m+r)^2/r$ are required for specific theorems.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the projected trajectories of state coefficients in the two-operator case, comparing randomized versus deterministic strategies, showing that random sampling consistently maintains a high success probability near unity.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the problem (intersection search), deriving theoretical bounds for the randomized approach using expectation values, and then rigorously comparing this stochastic method against deterministic sequences to prove its superior asymptotic performance.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Grover's algorithm achieves a quadratic speedup for unstructured search given a global oracle for the target set. In many applications, however, the target set is specified as the intersection of multiple constraint sets. Constructing a global oracle for the intersection can be costly, whereas the individual constraint oracles are often much simpler to implement.   We study a randomized Grover search algorithm that directly uses these constraint oracles. At each iteration, one of the corresponding Grover operators is selected at random. For the two-operator case with uniform sampling, we prove that the success probability approaches one after \[ Θ\left(\frac\pi4\sqrt{\frac{N}{r}}\right) \] iterations, where $r$ is the size of the intersection. Thus, the algorithm achieves the same asymptotic query complexity as standard Grover search but without requiring a global oracle. We then generalize the analysis to arbitrary sampling distributions and an arbitrary number of Grover operators through an auxiliary operator that approximates the expected Grover evolution, while retaining the same asymptotic complexity. We further show that highly biased sampling distributions can still achieve near-unit success probability, enabling cheaper Grover operators to be used more frequently. Finally, we prove asymptotic optimality and support the theoretical results with numerical simulations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.11383"></a>
### [Rolling Stock Planning Using the Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11383v1)

**Authors:** Jiří Guth Jarkovský, Patricia Bickert, Elisabeth Wybo, Martin Leib  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11383v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11383_figures/2606.11383_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Results showing the performance of various methods to solve the subgraph MWIS, with subgraph size kept fixed at 20 (and the greedy algorithm III-B1 as a benchmark). The left panel shows the data points from each individual full algorithm run, while the right panel shows the average performance of each method with standard error of the mean (SEM). IQM Emerald refers to results obtained from QAOA runs on IQM Emerald quantum computer. For more details about the solvers, see IV-B.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.11383_figures/2606.11383_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Results showing the performance (measured by the number of empty kilometers) of the algorithm using the classical exact MWIS solver, as a function of the subgraph size. Linear fit of the data shows a clear trend of improving solution quality with increasing subgraph size.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work applies quantum computing techniques, specifically QAOA, to solve the NP-hard rolling stock planning problem. By reformulating it as a Maximum Weight Independent Set problem, the authors propose a hybrid algorithm that uses divide-and-conquer strategies to tackle large instances. The results suggest that integrating near-term quantum solvers can effectively improve solutions over purely classical or approximate methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, real-world optimization application for near-term quantum algorithms like QAOA, demonstrating how quantum computation can be integrated into classical combinatorial problem-solving frameworks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper tackles rolling stock planning, a complex optimization problem in railway management that is mathematically formulated as finding a Maximum-Weight Independent Set (MWIS) on a large graph.

**Main result.** A hybrid quantum-classical framework was proposed, showing that increasing the size of subgraphs solved by QAOA generally improves solution quality compared to purely approximate methods.

**Method.** The core method is a divide-and-conquer approach where the MWIS problem on large subgraphs is addressed using the Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm (QAOA), orchestrated by classical heuristics.

**Model / system.** The system models a railway network with 190 trips over two days. The optimization graph nodes represent feasible train cycles, and edges connect conflicting cycles, leading to an MWIS formulation.

**Key observables.** Solution quality (minimizing empty kilometers) and resource consumption (number of trains required/fleet size).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of trips ($N=190$), maximum cycle length ($	ext{lmax}=4000 	ext{ km}$), QAOA depth parameter ($p$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes unlimited parking capacity and relies on a hybrid approach to manage the computational complexity, as exhaustive enumeration of cycles is prohibitive.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares solver performance for small subgraphs; Figure 2 shows that larger subgraphs lead to better solutions (less empty kilometers).

**Paper structure.** The paper first formulates the problem as MWIS. It then proposes a hybrid divide-and-conquer algorithm, detailing subgraph selection heuristics and applying QAOA on these smaller instances before iteratively updating the global graph.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Rolling stock planning is a complex optimization problem in railway management that involves assigning physical trains to scheduled trips while minimizing operational costs. In this work, we address a specific instance of this problem featuring 190 trips over two days, subject to constraints such as mandatory maintenance stops. We reformulate the problem as a Maximum-Weight Independent Set (MWIS) problem on a graph where nodes represent feasible train cycles. To handle the computational complexity of the large search space, we propose a hybrid divide-and-conquer algorithm. This approach iteratively selects subgraphs and solves the MWIS problem using various solvers, including exact classical methods and the Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm (QAOA). We evaluate the algorithm's performance by comparing these methods and analyzing the scaling with respect to subgraph size, with QAOA assessed through both classical simulation and execution on a quantum device (IQM Emerald). Our results indicate that increasing the subgraph size generally improves solution quality, demonstrating that the hybrid framework can effectively bridge the gap between polynomial-time approximate solvers and exponential-time exact methods.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.12383"></a>
### [The Simplified Stabilizer ZX-Calculus is Minimal](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12383v1)

**Authors:** Harry K. Stoltz  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12383v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.12383_figures/2606.12383_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.12383_figures/2606.12383_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.12383_figures/2606.12383_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.12383_figures/2606.12383_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.12383_figures/2606.12383_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proves that the rule set defining the Simplified Stabilizer ZX-Calculus is mathematically minimal. By constructing countermodels using complex matrices and dual number algebras, the authors demonstrate that two specific rules—the red/green coincidence and the bialgebra law—are individually necessary components of the calculus's structure. This establishes a complete and non-redundant set of rewrite rules for this quantum formalism.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides fundamental structural results about the underlying mathematical formalism of quantum computation, which can guide the development of more efficient and minimal computational models for quantum algorithms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To rigorously prove that every rewrite rule within the Simplified Stabilizer ZX-Calculus ($	ext{ZX}_{	ext{simp}}$) is individually necessary relative to the connectivity meta-rule.

**Main result.** The authors establish that both the red/green compact-structure coincidence rule ($(	ext{S3}'	ext{R})$) and the bialgebra law rule ($(	ext{B2}')$) are necessary, proving that the entire set of rules in $	ext{ZX}_{	ext{simp}}$ is minimal.

**Method.** The necessity proofs employ countermodel-style arguments, utilizing algebraic structures such as ordinary complex matrices ($\mathbb{C}$) and categories over dual numbers ($\mathbb{R}_5$) to derive contradictions if any rule were assumed redundant.

**Model / system.** The theory concerns the ZX calculus, a graphical language used for reasoning about quantum computation, specifically focusing on its stabilizer fragment. The analysis is performed using algebraic interpretations of the rules within specific mathematical models ($\mathbb{C}$ or $	ext{R}_5$).

**Key observables.** Consistency checks derived from comparing coefficients (e.g., in $\mathbb{C}$ for $(	ext{S3}'	ext{R})$ or in $\mathbb{R}_5$ for $(	ext{B2}')$) between the left and right sides of the rewrite rules.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis involves specific algebraic structures like complex numbers ($\mathbb{C}$) and the ring of dual numbers over $\mathbb{F}_5$, $R_5 = \mathbb{F}_5[\delta]/(\delta^2)$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The necessity proofs rely on constructing suitable interpretations (functors) that capture the constraints of the calculus within the chosen algebraic settings.

**Figures summary.** Not specified, but the notes reference Table 1 listing the rules in $	ext{ZX}_{	ext{simp}}$ and details about spider interpretations ($	ext{Z}'^k_{n,m}$).

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically addresses the necessity of individual rules. It first proves $(	ext{S3}'	ext{R})$ is necessary using complex matrices, followed by a detailed construction in the $	ext{C}_5$ model over $\mathbb{R}_5$ to prove that $(B2')$ is necessary.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The stabilizer fragment of the ZX calculus is amongst the most important fragments of the theory. The closely related Clifford+T fragment is approximately universal (arXiv:1705.11151). Additionally, the stabilizer calculus can be described by a small collection of rewrites, most of which have been shown to be necessary (arXiv:1709.08903). However, two rules, describing the red/green compact-structure coincidence and the important bialgebra law, had not been shown to be necessary. We present a countermodel-style argument showing that both of these rules are individually necessary relative to the connectivity meta-rule of Backens--Perdrix--Wang (arXiv:1709.08903), and hence establish that the rule set presented in arXiv:1709.08903 has no redundant rewrite rule.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11904"></a>
### [Intrinsic Nonreciprocity in Electron-Phonon Interaction Driven Thermoelectric Diodes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11904v1)

**Authors:** Hao-Kun Ke, Lie-Run Tian, Jun-Feng Liu, Pei-Hao Fu, Jun Wang, H. Xu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11904v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This work theoretically examines how intrinsic asymmetry in electron-phonon interactions can create highly efficient thermoelectric diodes. The key finding is that this nonreciprocity generates novel current pathways and suppresses resistive losses, potentially leading to energy conversion devices superior to those relying on topological or superconducting effects.

**Why it may be interesting.** It deals with non-equilibrium energy transport driven by coupling to a bosonic bath (phonons) in a mesoscopic structure, which touches upon open quantum systems dynamics and thermalization processes.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating intrinsic nonreciprocity in electron-phonon (e-ph) interaction driven thermoelectric diodes, challenging assumptions about the origin of nonreciprocal transport.

**Main result.** The intrinsic nonreciprocity leads to two novel phenomena: a new thermoelectric effect dependent on the temperature difference between leads and the central region, and the suppression of electronic backscattering that breaks Ohm's law.

**Method.** Theoretical calculation using Green's functions (retarded) incorporating e-ph interaction self-energy ($\Sigma_{ph}$) within a 1D lattice model.

**Model / system.** A nanowire modeled as a 1D tight-binding lattice, featuring three regions: a tunneling junction ($L_1$), an inelastic e-ph interaction region ($L_{ph}$), and a dephasing resistor ($L_p$).

**Key observables.** Charge current ($J$), nonreciprocal efficiency ($\eta_N$), downstream resistance ($ho$), and thermoelectric current.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature difference ($\Delta T$ or $\delta T$), phonon frequency ($\omega$), hopping energy ($t$), and resonance energy ($E_0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The nonreciprocity arises from the inherent asymmetry between phonon emission and absorption probabilities, independent of reversing external temperature biases.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate device schematics, current/efficiency vs. $\Delta T$ or $\delta T$, and resistance/current dependence on the length of the dephasing region ($L_p$).

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the model, derives the intrinsic nonreciprocity mechanism from phonon statistics, analyzes two distinct transport regimes (novel $\Delta T$ bias vs. conventional $\delta T$ bias), and quantifies resulting phenomena like resistance suppression.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study an electron-phonon interaction driven thermoelectric diode. The nonreciprocity in this diode arises from the asymmetry between the probabilities of phonon emission and absorption in the electron-phonon interaction, as well as the structural reflection asymmetry. We reveal the intrinsic nature of this nonreciprocity, as the forward and backward electron transport remains asymmetric even when the applied temperature difference is not reversed. This intrinsic nonreciprocity gives rise to two novel transport phenomena. One is a novel thermoelectric effect which is driven by the temperature difference between the leads and the central device region, rather than the conventional temperature difference between the two leads. The second, and more significant, phenomenon is the suppression of electronic backscattering in the load resistor. This suppression decreases the resistance of the load resistor, which leads to the breakdown of Ohm's addition law. Under suitable conditions, the presence of electron-phonon interaction can yield a larger thermoelectric current compared to the case without it. This intrinsic nonreciprocity opens up a new pathway for low-power electronics besides topology and superconductivity, and for nonreciprocal thermoelectric devices.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Deterministic Single-Photon Emitter Arrays in Hexagonal Boron Nitride by Carbon-Assisted Focused Ion Beam Engineering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12304v1)

**Summary.** The authors report a breakthrough lithography-free technique using focused ion beam milling and carbon deposition to deterministically create arrays of single-photon emitters in hBN. By achieving high site yield and confirming single-photon purity via $g^{(2)}$ measurements, this work establishes a scalable platform crucial for integrated quantum optical circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses a key hardware bottleneck for integrated quantum photonics by providing a scalable, direct-write pathway to high-quality SPE sources in 2D materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge is creating scalable, deterministic single-photon emitters (SPEs) at room temperature within van der Waals materials for on-chip quantum photonic circuits.

**Main result.** A novel, lithography-free three-step process was developed to deterministically generate SPE arrays in hBN with high site-correlated yields ($\sim 89\%$) and confirmed single-photon purity ($g^{(2)}(0) = 0.15 \pm 0.09$).

**Method.** The method involves a sequential fabrication process: Gallium focused ion beam milling, followed by conformal carbon deposition, and final thermal annealing.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) hosting defect-related SPEs. The emission dynamics are analyzed using a three-level emitter model to fit the second-order autocorrelation function $g^{(2)}(	au)$.

**Key observables.** Second-order autocorrelation function ($g^{(2)}(0)$), Zero-Phonon-Line (ZPL) wavelength ($\sim 581 	ext{ nm}$), and site-correlated emitter yield.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Annealing temperature ($850^\circ	ext{C}$), milling depth ($\sim 40 	ext{ nm}$), and the single-photon purity threshold ($g^{(2)}(0) < 0.5$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The precise microscopic origin of the emitting centers is not fully resolved, and defect migration during annealing is assumed to follow classical diffusion theory.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the three-step fabrication process (milling $ightarrow$ carbon deposition $ightarrow$ annealing), PL maps showing emission spots, and $g^{(2)}(	au)$ measurements confirming single-photon antibunching for representative emitters.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the necessity of deterministic SPEs, presents the novel three-step fabrication protocol, characterizes the resulting arrays using PL mapping and quantum correlation measurements ($g^{(2)}$), and validates the method's reproducibility.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The realization of on-chip photonic circuits requires scalable and deterministic single-photon emitters (SPEs) at room temperature, which remain a challenge in van der Waals materials. In this work, we report a novel three-step fabrication process for the generation of spatially controlled SPE arrays in hexagonal boron nitride (hBN). The process comprises site-selective gallium (Ga) focused ion beam milling, nanoscale conformal carbon deposition over the patterned regions, and subsequent thermal annealing. The synergistic combination of these steps resulted in a site-correlated emitter yield of ($\sim 89\%$) across 100 fabrication sites. Second-order autocorrelation measurements revealed pronounced three-level emitter dynamics where the best emitters exhibited high purity ($g^{(2)}(0)=0.15 \pm 0.09$).To the best of our knowledge, this is the first lithography-free, direct-write approach combining Ga-ion milling, selective carbon engineering, and thermal annealing to deterministically generate \hBN{} \SPE{}s. The reproducibility of the method is validated across multiple independently fabricated samples. These results establish a scalable, lithography-free pathway toward on-demand SPE arrays relevant to integrated quantum photonics.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper reports on the discovery and characterization of superconductivity near quarter- and half-filling in twisted trilayer WSe2. By combining transport measurements with continuum theoretical models, the authors map out a complex phase diagram featuring Mott insulators and superconducting domes. This establishes the system as a crucial platform for investigating strongly correlated triangular-lattice Hubbard physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides an experimental realization platform for studying fundamental strongly correlated physics (Hubbard model) in a solid-state moiré system, offering insights into unconventional superconductivity mechanisms driven by electronic correlations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates superconductivity and various correlated insulating phases occurring near quarter- and half-filling in a strongly correlated triangular Hubbard band system.

**Main result.** Superconductivity is observed over extended regions of the phase diagram, alongside robust Mott insulators at half-filling and other correlated insulating states at quarter-filling.

**Method.** The research combines electrical transport measurements (resistance vs. filling/field) with theoretical continuum model calculations to map out the complex phase diagram.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is alternating twisted trilayer WSe2, which realizes a system analogous to the triangular-lattice Hubbard model. The analysis uses continuum $k\cdot p$ frameworks and calculates susceptibilities for weak-coupling instability analysis.

**Key observables.** Longitudinal resistance (R), superconducting transition regions ($	ext{SC}1, 	ext{SC}2$), Mott insulating states ($	ext{Mott}1, 	ext{Mott}2$), and characteristic temperature dependencies ($T_{	ext{coh}}, T'$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Filling factor ($
u$) near $1/4$ and $1/2$, displacement field ($D$), magnetic field ($B$), and the effective on-site interaction ($U$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on continuum descriptions, assuming strong spin-orbit coupling leads to effective spin-valley locking, and uses simplified treatments for parameters like the effective g-factor.

**Figures summary.** Figures detail phase diagrams mapping superconducting domes adjacent to insulating states, showing field dependence of superconductivity, and comparing transport measurements against calculated electronic structure features (DOS, Berry curvature).

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically maps out a rich phase diagram by performing transport measurements across varying doping ($
u$) and electric fields ($D$), correlating the observed superconducting and insulating regimes with theoretical predictions derived from continuum models of the triangular Hubbard model.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The triangular-lattice Hubbard model is one of the paradigmatic settings for studying correlated electronic systems with geometric frustration that can give rise to a variety of phases. Recently, twisted bilayer TMD systems have emerged as attractive experimental platforms for realising such a model in the moderate-correlation regime. Alternating twisted trilayer TMD systems are expected to realise stronger correlations than bilayer systems owing to their mirror symmetry. Here we report the discovery of superconductivity near quarter- and half-filling, together with various correlated phases, in alternating twisted trilayer WSe2 (TTWSe2). At half filling, a robust correlated insulating state persists over a broad range of displacement fields, signalling the strongly correlated regime. Upon doping, superconductivity emerges over an extended region of the phase diagram. Near quarter filling, another superconducting state appears flanked by correlated metals. Our results establish the TTWSe2 system as a compelling platform for studying strongly correlated triangular-lattice Hubbard physics.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a breakthrough fabrication technique for Josephson junctions by utilizing substrate profiling instead of traditional organic resists. This method yields high-quality, oxide-free junctions suitable for advanced superconducting quantum circuits. The results demonstrate improved junction performance and scalability across various complex geometries.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses hardware limitations in superconducting qubits by improving junction quality and scalability through novel material engineering techniques, which is central to quantum computing architectures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is developing fabrication techniques for Josephson junctions (JJs) that avoid intentionally introduced oxides or organic residues, which are major sources of decoherence in superconducting quantum circuits.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated substrate profiling methods to create high-quality, oxide-free JJs using various geometries (SEI, MT, cT), enabling operation at higher speeds and temperatures.

**Method.** The core method involves etching patterned trenches into the substrate material itself to define the junction barrier and electrodes, circumventing traditional resist-based lithography steps.

**Model / system.** The platform is superconducting quantum circuits utilizing Josephson junctions (e.g., Nb/AlOx/Nb). The geometry is defined by creating specific step profiles on the substrate rather than relying on deposited insulating layers or organic resists.

**Key observables.** I-V characterization curves showing pronounced hysteresis, extracted gap voltage ($2.78 	ext{ mV}$), critical current density ($J_c$), and microwave resonator quality factor ($Q > 3 	imes 10^5$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Operating temperatures (e.g., $4.2 	ext{ K}$), junction sizes, and the ratio of edge leakage to normal resistance ($R_{sg}/R_n$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often requires correcting simple approximations for small junctions using complex fitting procedures involving effective temperature ($\Theta_{	ext{eff}}$) or deposition parameters ($p \cdot t$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate cross-sections and I-V curves for various junction types (SEI, cSEI, MT), alongside plots showing $J_c$ dependence on processing time/pressure and resonator performance.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by introducing the limitations of current JJ fabrication, detailing several novel substrate profiling geometries (SEI, MT, etc.), presenting characterization results via I-V curves and microwave measurements, and finally analyzing the derived junction parameters ($J_c$, leakage ratios) across different structures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We have developed a fabrication technique for Josephson junctions that employs a three-dimensional patterned, low-loss substrate instead of commonly used organic resists. The technique enables the fabrication of high-quality trilayer junctions from a wide range of geometries and materials, including high-melting-point superconductors such as tantalum or niobium. The junction electrodes are free from intentionally introduced oxides and organic materials, which are known sources of decoherence. We fabricate and characterize underdamped Nb/AlOx/Nb junctions of different sizes in several geometries. Such junctions enable manufacturing of quantum circuits operating at higher speeds and elevated temperatures.

</details>

<img src="2606.11912_figures/2606.11912_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Universal stability of synergistic switching operations. The macroscopic switching profile drops precipitously to an exact OFF-state at the critical compensation α/(J sin β) = 1. This behavior is highly robust against diverse perturbations: (a) varying FM-channel interfacial coupling tc, demonstrating immunity to contact scattering; (b) increasing non-magnetic Anderson disorder W (100-sample average), highlighting the insulation of spin coherence by the generalized SU(2) symmetry; (c) normalized channel length Lx/lα PSH, showing the deep OFF-state requires only commensurability with the PSH period; and (d) transverse width Ly, confirming full geometric scalability. **Summary.** The paper addresses the challenge of maintaining spin coherence in advanced magnetic materials by proposing an active compensation scheme. By tuning gate-induced Rashba SOC to exactly balance the material's intrinsic $p$-wave exchange coupling, the authors restore a generalized SU(2) symmetry. This engineered protection yields robust, electrically tunable Persistent Spin Helix states suitable for high-contrast spintronic switching.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete mechanism for engineering topological protection (via symmetry) into solid-state spintronics devices, offering a pathway to robust quantum control elements that are tunable electrically.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge is realizing functional spin-logic devices using unconventional magnets while maintaining long-range spin coherence against scattering and dephasing.

**Main result.** By engineering a generalized SU(2) symmetry through the active compensation of intrinsic exchange coupling with gate-tunable Rashba SOC, the authors demonstrate robust, electrically tunable spintronic switching.

**Method.** The work uses continuum Hamiltonian modeling combined with Non-Equilibrium Green's Function (NEGF) transport simulations to analyze spin dynamics and conductance.

**Model / system.** The system is a 3D p-wave magnet modeled as a spin-FET, described by a Hamiltonian combining $p$-wave exchange ($H_M$) and gate-induced Rashba SOC ($H_{SOC}$).

**Key observables.** High-visibility Datta-Das conductance oscillations (transport) and the spatially evolving local spin expectation value $\langleoldsymbol{\sigma}(\mathbf{r})angle$ (spin texture).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The critical resonance condition is $\alpha = J \sin(eta - 	heta)$, which tunes the system into a symmetry-protected Persistent Spin Helix (PSH) regime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the spin quantization axis can be constrained to the 2D plane for simplicity in modeling the compensation mechanism.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison of spin texture when the system is at resonance versus detuned, and map out the parameter space $(\alpha/J, eta)$ showing the boundary condition required for zero conductance (OFF-state).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying the coherence challenge in 3D magnets to proposing active symmetry restoration via SOC compensation. It analyzes the resulting PSH state and culminates by demonstrating its use in a high-contrast, disorder-resilient spin-FET switch.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Unconventional magnets combine the vanishing stray fields of antiferromagnets with the strong spin-splitting of ferromagnets, offering a unique material platform for spintronics. However, a critical challenge in realizing functional spin-logic devices lies in preserving long-range spin coherence against momentum-degrading scattering and gate-induced dephasing. Here, we demonstrate that the intrinsic momentum-dependent exchange field of a three-dimensional $p$-wave magnet can be precisely tuned against gate-induced Rashba spin-orbit coupling to establish a \textit{generalized} $\mathrm{SU}(2)$ spin-rotation symmetry. This emergent conservation law generates a symmetry-protected Persistent Spin Helix (PSH), effectively integrating the high energy scales of 3D bulk magnetic exchange with the macroscopic coherence of symmetry protection. By modeling a synergistic $p$-wave magnetic spin field-effect transistor (spin-FET), we reveal high-visibility Datta-Das conductance oscillations controlled purely by electrical gating. Crucially, our quantum transport simulations confirm that this symmetry-engineered transport regime exhibits exceptional resilience against strong non-magnetic Anderson disorder and geometric variations. These results establish a synergistic paradigm for non-magnetized spintronics, demonstrating how the active integration of spin-orbit coupling and unconventional magnetism can yield disorder-resilient spintronic logic.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper quantitatively investigates supratransmission in a 2D NLS model confined to an annular region with a central hole. By analyzing the system's spectral properties and performing time-domain simulations, the authors show that energy can leak through forbidden bands or exhibit nonlinear transitions even when linear propagation is allowed. The key finding is that nonzero angular momentum ($m$) drives symmetry breaking, resulting in the emission of discrete localized structures called 'pearls'.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to theoretical physicists working on open quantum systems or quantum optics because it models energy transport phenomena in structured media where linear wave propagation is forbidden, requiring nonlinear mechanisms for energy leakage. The concept of 'pearls' suggests discrete excitation dynamics within a continuous field.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates energy supratransmission—the transport of energy through a frequency band where linear waves are evanescent—in a two-dimensional nonlinear Schrödinger equation (NLS) defined on an annular geometry with a central hole.

**Main result.** Supratransmission is demonstrated both in the forbidden gap regime and, analogously, in the allowed spectral band via nonlinear effects. The presence of azimuthal charge ($m 
eq 0$) triggers symmetry breaking, leading to the emission of localized excitations called 'pearls'.

**Method.** The research employs a combination of analytical techniques (variational approximation) for standing wave analysis and advanced numerical methods (pseudo-arclength continuation, Crank-Nicolson scheme with TBC) for time evolution simulations.

**Model / system.** The system is governed by the 2D NLS equation on an annulus ($r \in [r_{	ext{min}}, r_{	ext{max}}]$). Nonlinearities studied include cubic–quintic and saturable forms, and the driving mechanism involves harmonic forcing at the inner boundary.

**Key observables.** Critical driving amplitude for supratransmission ($F_{0C}$), spectral stability ($	ext{Re}(\lambda)=0$), localized pulse emission ('pearls'), and spatiotemporal evolution of wave profiles.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inner radius ($r_{	ext{min}}$), azimuthal index ($m$), nonlinear coefficients ($s$), and driving frequency ($\Omega$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations such as the variational method for threshold determination, and numerical simulations require absorbing boundary conditions (TBC) at outer boundaries.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate bifurcation diagrams showing stable/unstable branches versus drive amplitude, time evolution snapshots showing ring formation and outward propagation of localized 'pearls', and isosurface plots visualizing radial and angular excursions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the linear band structure to analyzing standing wave stability via eigenvalue problems. It then details numerical simulations in both the forbidden gap (supratransmission) and the allowed band, culminating in a variational analysis linking critical drive amplitude to geometric parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate energy supratransmission in a boundary-driven two-dimensional nonlinear Schrödinger equation with a central hole. Harmonic forcing with azimuthal modulation generates standing-wave states whose existence and stability depend on the driving amplitude, the inner radius, and the imposed azimuthal charge. Bifurcation analysis shows that small inner radii produce strongly confined states with higher destabilization thresholds, whereas larger radii yield broader profiles and smoother transitions between stable and unstable branches. The cubic--quintic and saturable models exhibit similar qualitative behaviour but differ quantitatively in their critical amplitudes and parameter dependence. A variational approximation captures the dependence of the critical drive on the azimuthal charge and nonlinear parameters, and clarifies how the nonlinear response shapes the stationary states near the turning point. Time-dependent simulations show that supratransmission occurs through the emission of localized pulses, with nonzero azimuthal charge triggering symmetry breaking and producing two-dimensional localized excitations (pearls). Isosurface plots provide a complementary view of the resulting radial and angular excursions. These results establish a quantitative framework for supratransmission in two-dimensional geometries and are relevant to driven nonlinear systems in optics, Bose--Einstein condensates, and structured media.

</details>

### [Lectures on Semiclassical Methods for Composite Operators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11317v1)

**Authors:** Francesco Sannino  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11317v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

Right: The full special conformal transformation with parameter b = (0.35, 0.15). The reference circle is mapped to another circle, illustrating that conformal transformations preserve angles and map generalized circles (lines or circles) to generalized circles, while not preserving lengths or centres. Infinitesimally, this inversion–translation–inversion composition generates the vector field ϵµ = bµx2 −2(b · x)xµ, associated...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.11317_figures/2606.11317_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2.2: The conformal multiplet. Starting from a primary state |O⟩(bottom, blue) satisfying Kµ|O⟩= 0, repeated action of the momentum operator Pµ (red arrows, upward) generates descendants at dimensions ∆, ∆+ 1, ∆+ 2, . . . The number of independent states at level n equals the number of symmetric tensors of rank n, namely  d+n−1 n  . The special- conformal generator Kµ (blue arrow, downward) lowers the dimension; its vanishing on |O⟩ defines the primary as the lowest-weight state of the representation. In the state–operator correspondence each node is a local operator; the levels are the scaling dimensions measured on the cylinder.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<sub>Figure 2.3: The two-point function fixes the scaling dimension. Conformal invariance uniquely determines ⟨O(x) O(0)⟩= CO |x|−2∆; the only free parameter is the dimension ∆. Left: Linear scale for ∆= 0.5, 1, 2, 3.5; higher dimensions decay faster. Right: Log–log scale, where the relation becomes a straight line with slope −2∆. **Summary.** This lecture series details a powerful, systematic method for calculating the scaling dimensions of composite operators in Conformal Field Theories. It bridges abstract CFT concepts with concrete semiclassical mechanics by mapping the problem onto finding the energy levels of a state on a cylinder geometry. The resulting framework is crucial for understanding universal properties near critical points.

**Why it may be interesting.** The deep connection between CFT/RG flow and semiclassical quantization techniques is highly relevant. The use of spectral methods (like those derived from Floquet theory) to calculate quantum corrections around classical solutions mirrors approaches used in analyzing stability in many-body systems or quasi-periodic potentials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to provide a coherent introduction to Conformal Field Theory (CFT) by developing a systematic semiclassical framework for calculating the scaling dimensions of composite operators, particularly $\phi^n$. This addresses gaps in literature regarding how these complex operators probe collective sectors.

**Main result.** The notes establish a rigorous connection between the state-operator correspondence on the cylinder and the energy spectrum of the theory, allowing for the calculation of anomalous dimensions via semiclassical quantization techniques. The framework is successfully applied to $O(N) \phi^4$ theory in $d=4-\epsilon$.

**Method.** The core methodology involves mapping the quantum problem onto a classical dynamics problem on the cylinder ($\mathbb{R} 	imes S^{d-1}$), utilizing semiclassical approximations like WKB, Bohr-Sommerfeld quantization, and advanced tools such as Floquet theory and the Gutzwiller trace formula.

**Model / system.** The general context is Quantum Field Theory in critical regimes described by CFT. Specific models include free scalar theories (used for initial development) and the $O(N) \phi^4$ model at its Wilson-Fisher fixed point, which exhibits universal critical behavior.

**Key observables.** Scaling dimensions ($\Delta_n$) of composite operators $\phi^n$, energy levels on the cylinder, and one-loop corrections to these dimensions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The large occupation number ($n$ or $N$), the spacetime dimension $d=4-\epsilon$, and the coupling constants defining the fixed point.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The semiclassical approach assumes that for heavy operators (large $n$), the system's dynamics are dominated by a classical periodic solution, allowing quantum corrections to be treated perturbatively via fluctuation determinants.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the logical descent from a critical system to the scaling dimension ($\Delta_n = \Delta_{cl} + \Delta_{1-loop}/n + \dots$) and summarize the five parts of the lecture notes, detailing the mathematical machinery used at each stage.

**Paper structure.** The structure is highly pedagogical: Part I reviews CFT basics (conformal group, state-operator map); Part II builds the semiclassical framework using free theory; Part III develops general tools (Floquet theory, Gutzwiller formula) for periodic saddles; Part IV applies this machinery to $O(N) \phi^4$ theory; and subsequent sections provide appendices on related mathematical structures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

These lecture notes are intended as a coherent introduction to conformal field theory in general, and composite operators in particular, through a semiclassical framework for computing scaling dimensions, with emphasis on operators of the form $φ^n$. In doing so, they aim to fill a gap in the literature and to help decode some of the relevant concepts. The physical idea is that at large $n$ an (heavy) operator creates a highly occupied state. Through the state-operator correspondence, this state lives on the cylinder $\mathbb{R}\times S^{d-1}$, and its scaling dimension is the corresponding energy of the theory on the cylinder. The notes are organized as a self-contained route from conformal symmetry to semiclassical dynamics. Part I reviews the conformal group, primary operators, radial quantization, the state-operator correspondence, and operator mixing. Part II builds the semiclassical framework, first in the free scalar theory, where the dimension of $φ^n$ is recovered in three independent ways, and then through the double-scaling limit, the action variable, and Bohr-Sommerfeld quantization. Part III develops the general machinery of periodic saddles, Floquet theory, fluctuation determinants, the Gel'fand-Yaglom method, and the Gutzwiller trace formula. Part IV applies the framework to the $O(N)$ $φ^4$ theory in $d=4-ε$ at the Wilson-Fisher fixed point, deriving the classical elliptic solution, the Lamé fluctuation spectrum, the zero modes, and the one-loop contribution to the large-$n$ scaling dimensions. Beyond the explicit computation, the notes emphasize the role of composite operators as probes of collective sectors of quantum field theory, with extensions to gauge theories, conformal windows, and asymptotically safe field theories.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.11481"></a>
### [Synthetic Altermagnetism Beyond the Crystal Limit](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11481v1)

**Authors:** Rodolfo A. Gallardo, Andrea M. León, Jürgen Lindner, Jhon W. González  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11481v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper theoretically demonstrates how synthetic altermagnetism can be realized in multilayered magnetic films without requiring intrinsically altermagnetic crystals. By engineering alternating anisotropic exchange couplings, the authors show that magnon spectra exhibit characteristic splitting and nodal structures. This work establishes parity and surface termination as key design principles for controlling these collective quantum magnetic responses.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, material-science inspired model (multilayers) to study exotic quantum magnetic phenomena (altermagnetism), which is relevant for understanding collective excitations in engineered quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To demonstrate a synthetic realization of altermagnetic magnonics beyond the constraints imposed by crystalline symmetries.

**Main result.** The authors show that engineered multilayer structures can reproduce characteristic altermagnetic magnon spectra, and this phenomenon is controllable via stack parity and surface termination effects.

**Method.** Theoretical modeling involving solving the linearized Landau-Lifshitz equation within a dipole-exchange framework for coupled ferromagnetic multilayers.

**Model / system.** The system is an antiferromagnetically coupled multilayer of ferromagnetic films with alternating in-plane exchange anisotropies, forming a continuum platform. The dynamics are governed by the LLG equation applied to spin-wave excitations across multiple layers.

**Key observables.** Momentum-dependent branch splitting ($\Delta k$), anisotropic isofrequency contours, and parity-dependent reconstruction of surface vs. bulk magnon modes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Stacking layer number ($N$), spacer thickness, and the relative strength of dipole interactions versus exchange coupling.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the continuum approximation for anisotropic exchange and treats long-range dipolar interactions via magnetostatic formalism.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate spin-wave dispersion (a) showing momentum-dependent splitting, isofrequency contours (b) exhibiting anisotropy, and layer-resolved dynamics (c, d), with further figures detailing parity dependence in multilayers.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theoretical framework by defining the effective Hamiltonian/equations of motion for coupled layers, solving the eigenvalue problem for spin waves, analyzing the minimal bilayer case, and finally extending the analysis to finite multilayers to reveal parity-dependent reconstruction effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnetic magnons in crystalline materials exhibit momentum-dependent splitting whose nodal structure and chiral character are governed by the point-group symmetry of the magnetic sublattice rotation. Here, we demonstrate the first synthetic realization of altermagnetic magnonics in a continuum platform composed of antiferromagnetically coupled ferromagnetic films with alternating in-plane exchange anisotropies, showing that the key signatures of altermagnetic magnonics emerge beyond the crystalline setting. Solving the linearized Landau-Lifshitz equation within a dipole-exchange framework, we show that this architecture reproduces the characteristic momentum-dependent splitting, nodal directions, and anisotropic isofrequency contours of A-type altermagnets. Long-range dipolar interactions qualitatively reconstruct this exchange-driven spectrum by lifting the nominal nodal degeneracy, hybridizing opposite-chirality modes, and producing a finite, thickness-dependent wave-vector splitting along directions that are nodal in the exchange-only limit. Extending the bilayer to finite multilayers reveals that synthetic altermagnetism undergoes a parity-dependent reconstruction that separates surface and bulk altermagnetic excitations. These results establish altermagnetic magnon phenomenology as an engineerable collective response of dipole-exchange multilayers beyond microscopic crystal symmetries.

</details>

<sub>FIG. 3. Observation of the C = 4 topological charge. The central panel shows the momentum-space pseudospin texture and the experimental scanning scheme (inset). The corre- sponding real-space field patterns ( 1 ○– 5 ○) repeat exactly af- ter a π/2 rotation, directly demonstrating C = 4.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a general theory to calculate topological charges based on Berry phases in quasicrystals, extending beyond conventional periodic lattices. By applying this framework to photonic quasicrystals, the authors predict that these higher-order topological charges manifest as a distinct, measurable periodicity ($C$ times) in the real-space electromagnetic field pattern when probing the system's momentum space.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges concepts from topology (Berry phase) and condensed matter physics into the realm of quasiperiodic materials, providing concrete, measurable predictions for photonic systems that are highly relevant to quantum optics and metamaterials research.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To extend Berry-phase-based topological charges, traditionally studied in periodic crystals, to quasicrystals and demonstrate their physical observability.

**Main result.** A universal framework is established for calculating higher topological charges ($C=4$) in 2D quasicrystals using group representation theory. These charges manifest as a quantifiable winding number of the electromagnetic field pattern in photonic realizations.

**Method.** The authors derive effective low-energy Hamiltonians by analyzing band degeneracies at high-symmetry points ($\Gamma$ point) using symmetry constraints and Berry phase calculations.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on 2D quasicrystals, exemplified by the $C_{8v}$ symmetry. The theory is applied to both electronic systems (via quasiperiodic potentials) and photonic systems (by analyzing Maxwell's equations with modulated permittivity).

**Key observables.** Topological charges ($C=2, 4, -2$), which are diagnosed by the winding number of the pseudospin texture in momentum space. Experimentally, this corresponds to the real-space electromagnetic field pattern repeating $C$ times upon encircling the charge point.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The rotational symmetry $n$ dictates the maximum possible topological charge ($C \le n/2$). The modulation strength ($\alpha$) is a key parameter for photonic realization.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on constructing an effective Hamiltonian by truncating the infinite-dimensional band structure problem, assuming that large-scale averaging allows for a momentum-space description around $\Gamma$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the physical setup of the quasicrystal potential, the resulting band structures showing degeneracies at $\Gamma$, and crucially, diagrams visualizing the pseudospin texture winding number ($C=4$) alongside the corresponding real-space field pattern repetition.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the theoretical framework by deriving topological charges from group theory for 2D quasicrystals. It then applies this to photonic systems, showing how the derived mathematical structure predicts observable features in light propagation, culminating in a proposal for experimental detection via momentum-space scanning.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Topological charges based on Berry phase play the fundamental role in the topological physics. However, such topological charges remain unexplored in quasicrystals, impeding the systematic understanding of topological states in such quasiperiodic systems. In this work, by deriving all the allowed topological charges according to group representation theory and the corresponding low-energy effective Hamiltonians, we establish a universal framework for Berry-phase-based topological charges in two-dimensional quasicrystals. Taking the $C_{8v}$ quasicrystal as an example, we demonstrate and characterize a higher topological charge of $C=4$, which is inaccessible in conventional periodic systems. Applying our framework to photonic quasicrystals, we uncover that the circling of photon momentum around the charge gives a $C$ times winding of the electromagnetic field distribution pattern. Such observable feature provides a direct experimental method to probe the topological charges. Our work paves the way for exploring topological charges in quasiperiodic matter, and fundamentally bridges periodic and quasiperiodic topological band theories.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Reflective Metastructure Q-plate for Ultrashort Laser Pulses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12041v1)

**Summary.** The paper presents an experimental realization of a reflective Q-plate using a plasmonic metasurface made of gold nanorods. This device efficiently converts the spin angular momentum (SAM) of incident light into orbital angular momentum (OAM) upon reflection. Its significance lies in its ability to maintain high reflectivity and preserve the integrity of ultrashort laser pulses across a broad spectral range, making it ideal for advanced ultrafast optical applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems because it demonstrates a robust, solid-state platform for manipulating the angular momentum of light—a key resource in quantum information processing—using plasmonics rather than bulk optical elements.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The goal is to develop a highly reflective Q-plate capable of converting orbital angular momentum (OAM) from nanostructures into ultrashort laser pulses without incurring temporal broadening.

**Main result.** The proposed plasmonic metasurface successfully achieves high reflectivity and broadband OAM generation for femtosecond pulses, overcoming dispersion issues associated with traditional transmissive Q-plates.

**Method.** The authors fabricated a reflective metasurface using ordered arrays of gold nanorods on a dielectric spacer above a back reflector to mimic the phase transformation properties of conventional Q-plates upon reflection.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a plasmonic metasurface constructed from anisotropic gold nanorods deposited on an $	ext{SiO}_2/	ext{Au}$-reflector stack. This structure leverages the geometric Pancharatnam-Berry phase acquired during reflection to couple input polarization state (SAM) into output OAM.

**Key observables.** High reflectivity ($>60\%$), generation of helical phase fronts ($\ell=\pm 1$) from circularly polarized light, and preservation of ultrashort pulse duration upon reflection.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Operating wavelengths range from $550 	ext{ nm}$ to $1000 	ext{ nm}$; incidence angles tested include $\alpha = 45^\circ$ and $\alpha = 5^\circ$; input pulses are in the femtosecond regime ($<30 	ext{ fs}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the metasurface can effectively mimic the required birefringence landscape of conventional Q-plates while operating robustly under reflection geometry.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the device schematic, SEM images confirming nanorod dimensions and orientation, optical setups for characterization (reflectivity/beam profiles), and wavelength-dependent measurements showing OAM generation ($\ell=\pm 1$ donut modes) and polarization conversion.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the need for reflective Q-plates, details the plasmonic metasurface design using gold nanorods, presents experimental characterization across various wavelengths and incidence angles, confirms broadband operation, and discusses pulse integrity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The orbital angular momentum of light is an intriguing property for developing light driven applications. It emerged as an independent degree of freedom by which to manipulate light and, consequently, the interaction of light with matter. Several methods exist for the generation of light carrying orbital angular momentum, mostly employing transmitting or reflecting optical components, which radially modulate the phase profile of the light. As one of such components, transmissive q-plates established themselves as standard elements due to their usability over a broad wavelength range. Here, we present our approach to build a highly reflective q-plate based on a plasmonic metasurface capable of converting orbital angular momentum from the nanostructure to ultrashort laser pulses without temporal broadening. We highlight its working principle over a wide range of wavelengths for reflection under normal and gracing incidence.

</details>

### [Chiral anomaly and planar Hall conductance in pseudospin-$1$ Fermions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11364v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical study analyzes how chiral anomalies manifest as measurable transport signals (PHC and LMC) in advanced systems like pseudospin-1 Weyl semimetals. By incorporating complex effects such as momentum-dependent scattering and dispersion tilting, the authors predict characteristic angular anisotropies and sign reversals in these magnetotransport coefficients. The findings offer concrete, experimentally testable signatures for identifying multifold fermion materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed, quantitative framework for interpreting complex magnetotransport measurements in topological materials by explicitly modeling higher-order pseudospin degrees of freedom and their coupling to external fields.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates magnetotransport signatures, specifically Planar Hall Conductance (PHC) and Longitudinal Magnetoconductance (LMC), arising from the chiral anomaly in systems with higher-pseudospin quasiparticle excitations.

**Main result.** The PHC exhibits characteristic angular dependencies ($\sin 2\gamma$ without tilt, $\sin \gamma$ or $\cos \gamma$ with tilt) and shows a sign reversal when scattering strength increases. Tilt introduces strong anisotropy to the planar Hall signal.

**Method.** Semiclassical Boltzmann transport theory is employed within the relaxation-time approximation, incorporating momentum-dependent scattering and orbital magnetic moment corrections.

**Model / system.** The model uses pseudospin-1 Weyl semimetals characterized by a Lagrangian density describing multifold chiral quasiparticles. The analysis accounts for both generic tilt of the dispersion and misalignment between E and B fields to break azimuthal symmetry.

**Key observables.** Planar Hall Conductance (PHC), Longitudinal Magnetoconductance (LMC), angular dependence ($\sin 2\gamma$, $\sin \gamma$, $\cos \gamma$), and nonmonotonic dependence on tilt magnitude.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Scattering strength ($\alpha$), magnetic field angle ($\gamma$), and the tilt parameters ($t_x, t_z$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on semiclassical dynamics and assumes that the Lorentz-force contribution can be neglected when $\omega_c 	au \ll 1$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate LMC and PHC dependence on scattering strength ($\alpha$) and field angle ($\gamma$), demonstrating sign reversals. Further figures map out the anisotropy of PHC as a function of tilt components ($t_x, t_z$) and angle ($\gamma$).

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from general theoretical frameworks (Hamiltonian/Lagrangian) to specific transport calculations using the Boltzmann equation. It systematically analyzes the effects of symmetry breaking mechanisms—namely, scattering strength variation and explicit dispersion tilting—on the resulting magnetoconductivity components.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Positive longitudinal magnetoconductance (LMC) and planar Hall conductance (PHC) are hallmark transport signatures of the chiral anomaly in Weyl semimetals. Recent theoretical developments have extended Weyl fermions to multifold Fermionic systems with higher-pseudospin quasiparticle excitations, motivating the study of their magnetotransport properties. Here, we employ semiclassical Boltzmann transport theory within the relaxation-time approximation to investigate magnetotransport in pseudospin-1 Weyl semimetals, incorporating momentum-dependent scattering, orbital magnetic moment corrections, and charge-conservation constraints. To obtain a finite PHC, we break azimuthal symmetry through either a generic tilt of the quasiparticle dispersion or a finite misalignment between the electric and magnetic fields. In the untilted case, the PHC is positive and scales quadratically with magnetic field strength. Increasing the scattering strength induces a sign reversal of the PHC, producing a transition from positive to negative values. The PHC further exhibits the characteristic angular dependence $\sin 2γ$, where $γ$ is the angle between the magnetic field and the $x$-axis. Tilt qualitatively alters this behavior: tilt along the $x$- and $z$-directions changes the angular response to $\sinγ$ and $\cosγ$, respectively, generating strong anisotropy in the planar Hall signal. Moreover, the PHC shows a nonmonotonic dependence on tilt magnitude, revealing the interplay between tilt-induced symmetry breaking and chiral-anomaly-driven transport. Our results provide experimentally accessible signatures of multifold fermions and a framework for interpreting magnetotransport measurements in candidate materials of space groups 199, 214, and 220.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study explores charge transport in graphene encapsulated by 1T-TaS2 monolayers, showing that the stacking geometry is crucial. The AA stacking induces a strong radial Rashba field leading to enhanced spin effects, whereas the AA' stacking stabilizes out-of-plane spins and maximizes the orbital Hall effect. This highlights how structural control can engineer distinct topological electronic responses.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a clear example of how interfacial symmetry breaking (stacking order) can act as a quantum switch, tuning the dominant topological transport mechanism between spin-based and orbital-based responses.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how the stacking order of graphene encapsulated by 1T-TaS2 monolayers dictates fundamentally distinct charge-to-spin and charge-to-orbital transport regimes.

**Main result.** Asymmetric (AA) stacking yields a strong, radial Rashba field leading to an unconventional Rashba-Edelstein effect, while symmetric (AA') stacking establishes stable out-of-plane spin textures and highly efficient orbital Hall effects.

**Method.** The research employs first-principles DFT calculations, tight-binding modeling, and the Kubo formalism to calculate linear response transport coefficients.

**Model / system.** The system is graphene encapsulated between two 1T-TaS2 monolayers in their charge density wave phase. The electronic structure and transport are modeled using effective Hamiltonians incorporating Rashba coupling and SOC terms.

**Key observables.** Orbital Hall conductivity ($\sigma_{	ext{OHE}}$), Spin Hall conductivity ($\sigma_{	ext{SHE}}$), Rashba-Edelstein effect magnitude, and persistent spin textures ($s_z$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Stacking order (AA vs. AA'), Fermi energy relative to the Dirac point ($E_F - E_0$), and proximity-induced Rashba phase ($\pi/2$ for AA).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a vacuum gap of $\approx 15$ Å, and calculations are performed within linear response theory.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare band structures and spin expectation values; Figure 4 specifically plots $\sigma_{	ext{OHE}}$ and $\sigma_{	ext{SHE}}$ versus $E_F - E_0$, showing plateaus in OHE inside spectral gaps for both stacking types.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the electronic structure, spin textures, and transport coefficients ($\sigma_{	ext{OHE}}, \sigma_{	ext{SHE}}$) by comparing results derived from two distinct structural configurations: AA (asymmetric) and AA' (symmetric).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the electronic structure, spin textures, and charge to spin/orbital transport in graphene encapsulated by 1T-TaS$_{2}$ monolayers in the charge density wave phase. Using first-principles calculations, tight-binding modeling, and the Kubo formalism, we show that the encapsulation stacking dictates fundamentally distinct transport regimes. In the asymmetrical (AA) stacking, proximity fields from both interfaces constructively interfere, yielding a cumulative Rashba phase of nearly $π/2$. This pure radial Rashba spin pattern leads to the unconventional Rashba-Edelstein effect, which robustly dominates over the conventional response by a factor of 35 across a wide energy range. Conversely, the symmetrical (AA') stacking preserves a horizontal mirror symmetry, establishing a stable, purely out-of-plane persistent spin texture. Furthermore, the computed orbital Hall effect is exceptionally efficient, surpassing the spin Hall effect by three orders of magnitude. Within the proximity-induced spectral gaps, the orbital Hall conductivity exhibits a finite plateau, whereas the spin Hall conductivity vanishes. Our findings establish graphene encapsulated heterostructures as a promising system for realizing distinct charge to spin and charge to orbital interconversion regimes determined by the choice of stacking order.

</details>

**Authors:** Isam A. Balghari, M. Faryad, M. Sabieh Anwar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11685v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11685_figures/2606.11685_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Complete workflow of the DSpinGNN framework for mesoscale dynamic magnetic map-</sub>

</details>

<img src="2606.11685_figures/2606.11685_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
