<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.11685_figures/2606.11685_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Predicted exchange coupling landscape during strain wave propagation and reflection in</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.11685_figures/2606.11685_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Quantitative mesoscale observables from the 3,200-atom DSpinGNN simulation. (a) AFM</sub>

**Summary.** This work introduces DSpinGNN, an advanced machine learning model that predicts strain-dependent magnetic exchange couplings in monolayer CrI$_3$. By combining equivariant graph networks with physics constraints derived from the Goodenough-Kanamori rules, it accurately models how mechanical strain generates spatially heterogeneous magnetic textures. The resulting framework provides testable mesoscopic predictions for cryogenic magnetic measurements.

**Why it may be interesting.** The integration of equivariant ML directly into predicting magnetic interactions under mechanical stress provides a powerful, transferable tool for simulating complex quantum materials behavior at mesoscopic scales, bridging solid-state theory and AI.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is accurately calculating the instantaneous, position-dependent isotropic magnetic exchange coupling ($J_{ij}$) across a crystal lattice undergoing dynamic structural deformation.

**Main result.** DSpinGNN successfully predicts these couplings on unseen data and simulates strain wave propagation, yielding testable mesoscopic predictions like domain wall width ($\xi=1.7 	ext{ nm}$) and oscillation period ($	au=0.27 	ext{ ps}$).

**Method.** A bifurcated machine-learning architecture combines an E(3)-equivariant GNN for structural dynamics with a physics-informed $\Delta$-MLP that maps local bond geometry to $J_{ij}$, incorporating the Goodenough-Kanamori superexchange relationship.

**Model / system.** The system is monolayer CrI$_3$, modeled using an effective classical Heisenberg Hamiltonian ($H_{	ext{spin}}$). The framework operates under several approximations, including the collinear Ising limit and adiabatic decoupling between structure and magnetism.

**Key observables.** Energy MAE (1.1 meV/atom), Force MAE (6.5 meV/Å), Exchange Coupling MAE (0.18 meV), Domain wall width ($\xi$), Oscillation period ($	au$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature ($T=5 	ext{ K}$), Strain magnitude (up to $\pm 9.1\%$ compression causing FM-to-AFM transition).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes an adiabatic decoupling between structural dynamics and magnetic coupling, and it is restricted to the collinear isotropic limit by omitting SOC effects.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the workflow from DFT data generation to ML prediction; key results show $J_{ij}$ landscapes during strain wave reflection, confirming the formation of transient AFM-sign zones. Quantitative analysis provides fitted values for $\xi$ and $	au$.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the computational difficulty (instantaneous $J_{ij}$). It then details the DSpinGNN architecture—the E-GNN structural branch and the physics-informed $\Delta$-MLP exchange predictor. The methodology is validated on a test set, followed by mesoscale simulations of strain wave dynamics to extract quantitative physical observables.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Resolving the instantaneous, position-dependent isotropic magnetic exchange coupling $J_{ij}$ across a dynamically deforming crystal lattice requires a computational approach that simultaneously handles structural forces and magnetic interactions at length scales inaccessible to first-principles methods. Here we introduce DSpinGNN, a bifurcated machine-learning architecture comprising an $E(3)$-equivariant graph neural network (E-GNN) for classical Langevin structural dynamics and a physics-informed $Δ$-MLP that maps instantaneous local Cr-I-Cr bond geometry to isotropic exchange couplings, with the Goodenough-Kanamori superexchange relationship embedded as an analytical inductive bias. Trained on 345 DFT+U configurations of monolayer CrI$_3$ and evaluated on a strictly withheld 61-configuration test set, DSpinGNN simultaneously achieves an energy MAE of $1.1$ meV/atom, a force MAE of $6.5$ meV/Å, and an exchange coupling MAE of $0.18$ meV ($R^2 = 0.91$). Deployed at 400$\times$ scale in a 3,200-atom supercell under a collinear Ising-constrained adiabatic approximation at $5$ K, the model maps the local exchange response to a propagating biaxial strain wave. Wave reflection at periodic boundaries generates transient constructive interference regions where local compressive strain exceeds the DFT-established FM-to-AFM threshold, producing spatially heterogeneous exchange coupling textures that damp as the wave dissipates. Quantitative analysis yields a domain wall width of $ξ= 1.7 \pm 0.3$~nm and a constructive-interference oscillation period of $τ= 0.27$~ps -- mesoscopic observables inaccessible to direct DFT and constituting testable predictions for cryogenic magnetic force microscopy. DSpinGNN provides a reproducible, transferable framework for mesoscale exchange mapping in strain-driven 2D magnetic materials.

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.11365_figures/2606.11365_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes the Color-Rule-Function (CRF) method to encode data into combinatorial memory paths within a structured mesh. It demonstrates that this approach offers a superior scaling ($\mathcal{O}(N^4)$) compared to conventional memories, potentially enabling ultra-high-density storage for read-only applications. The core challenge remains optimizing the rule set and understanding the minimum achievable Hamming distance.

**Why it may be interesting.** While not directly quantum, the concept of encoding information into complex structural constraints (paths/rules) shares conceptual parallels with topological quantum error correction or state preparation in highly constrained physical systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a systematic encoding framework for representing information in combinatorial memory that surpasses the scaling limits of conventional storage technologies.

**Main result.** The Color-Rule-Function (CRF) approach achieves a design space capacity scaling of $\mathcal{O}(N^4)$, potentially leading to data storage densities exceeding $10^{19}$ bits/cm$^2$ in customized hardware realizations.

**Method.** Information is encoded by selecting paths through a structured mesh, assigning values (colors) to cells, defining rules based on path colors, and constructing a final state via a Boolean function.

**Model / system.** The system models combinatorial memory using an $N \times N$ mesh structure. The hardware realization involves three modules: a Mesh Module (frequency generators), a Rule Module (frequency decoders/filters), and a Function Module (Boolean logic gates, potentially magnonic).

**Key observables.** Path states (0 or 1), design space capacity ($	ext{BCRF}$), data storage density (bits/cm$^2$), and the minimum Hamming distance.

**Important parameters / regimes.** $N$ (Mesh dimension), number of colors, number of rules, and the complexity scaling exponents ($N^4$ vs $N^2$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The advantage relies heavily on using customized module designs rather than universal ones; finding the optimal CRF encoding is computationally hard.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the general architecture (Mesh $ightarrow$ Rule $ightarrow$ Function), show colored meshes for encoded data examples, and compare scaling trends between different memory types.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the CRF framework via four steps of encoding. It analyzes the theoretical design space scaling ($\mathcal{O}(N^4)$) compared to conventional methods. The discussion then focuses on hardware realization using magnonics and comparing storage density estimates for universal versus customized module designs.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Combinatorial memory is a class of memory in which information is encoded in the set of paths through a structured mesh. In this work, we introduce a systematic encoding framework, referred to as the Color-Rule-Function (CRF) approach, for representing information in combinatorial memory. The method consists of four key steps: selecting a sequence of paths in the mesh, assigning values (e.g., colors) to each cell, defining a set of rules based on the values encountered along each path, and constructing a Boolean function that determines the state of each path. . The coding procedure is illustrated by several examples. The design space scales of the CRF scale fundamentally faster compared to conventional memory. This apparent advantage arises from the use of rule-based and functional representations but is accompanied by increased hardware complexity. A possible hardware realization of the CRF framework is discussed. Importantly, the hardware overhead can be substantially reduced through the use of customized modules. The examples of the customized design are described in the text. The combination of CRF coding with customized module design may lead to a practical advantage in data storage density. According to the estimates, the data storage density may exceed Exabit per centimeter squared. A key problem that requires further investigation is related to the minimum Hamming distance between an arbitrary target bit sequence and the closest sequence realizable within the CRF framework under fixed hardware constraints.

</details>

**Summary.** This research explores how N-heterocyclic carbene monolayers can be used to decouple Graphene Nanoribbons from metal substrates. By comparing methyl and isopropyl derivatives, the authors show that the molecule's physical size and adsorption geometry critically determine whether intercalation occurs, leading to electronically decoupled segments favorable for device fabrication.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed case study in controlling interfacial electronic coupling using molecular engineering, which is highly relevant for designing functional quantum materials interfaces.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates methods to electronically decouple Graphene Nanoribbons (GNRs) from their metallic growth substrates, which is crucial for device integration.

**Main result.** The adsorption geometry of N-heterocyclic carbenes (NHCs) dictates the intercalation outcome; methyl-substituted NHCs ($	ext{NHCMe}$) can partially intercalate and electronically decouple the GNRs, while bulkier isopropyl groups ($	ext{NHCiPr}$) prevent this.

**Method.** The research combines low-temperature STM/STS measurements to probe electronic structure with DFT calculations and Raman spectroscopy to analyze interfacial coupling mechanisms.

**Model / system.** The system involves 7-atom-wide armchair graphene nanoribbons (7-AGNRs) placed on Au(111), which are subsequently treated with self-assembled monolayers (SAMs) formed by $	ext{NHCMe}$ or $	ext{NHCiPr}$.

**Key observables.** Electronic band gap size and symmetry (STS); Raman mode shifts ($	ext{G}$ and RBLM); Intercalation yield percentage; DFT calculated relative binding energies ($\Delta E$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Molecular adsorption geometry, NHC ligand bulkiness, temperature (especially low T for STM), and the energy cost of lifting the GNR from the metal surface.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The interpretation relies on distinguishing between true intercalation, simple top-adsorption, and embedded states; thermal effects in Raman spectroscopy must be carefully separated from chemical changes.

**Figures summary.** Figures show STM images detailing SAM structures ($	ext{NHCMe}$ dimers vs. $	ext{NHCiPr}$ upright monomers); STS spectra compare gaps on bare Au(111) versus intercalated GNRs; DFT results map out energy barriers for intercalation.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically characterizes the NHC SAM formation (using STM/NMR), then uses low-temperature STM/STS to distinguish between various adsorption states, followed by DFT and Raman analysis to provide theoretical and spectroscopic evidence supporting the geometric control over electronic decoupling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Graphene nanoribbons (GNRs) synthesized on metal substrates experience electronic coupling and screening from the underlying surface, which, although often weak, can modify their observed properties and complicate their transfer to device-compatible substrates. Intercalation of GNRs by self-assembled monolayers (SAMs) offers a possible route to reduce this interaction. Here, we investigate the intercalation of 7-atom-wide armchair graphene nanoribbons (7-AGNRs) on Au(111) using N-heterocyclic carbenes (NHCs). Low-temperature scanning tunneling microscopy and spectroscopy, Raman spectroscopy, and density functional theory calculations reveal that the adsorption geometry of the NHCs strongly influences the intercalation yield for GNRs. Methyl-substituted NHCs form flat-lying dimers that partially intercalate the GNRs, producing locally decoupled segments. In contrast, bulkier isopropyl-substituted NHCs form upright monomers that embed the GNRs within the monolayer, preventing intercalation. The low intercalation yield indicates that lifting the nanoribbon from the Au surface is energetically costly. These results establish molecular adsorption geometry and packing as key parameters controlling intercalation at GNR-metal interfaces, with implications for the rational design of decoupling layers for GNR-based device integration pathways.

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 3. Local Chern number contributions in small regions around the K and K′ valleys for three representative points in the phase diagram of FIG. (2) at fixed ϕt = 1.55. (a) C = 4 phase (θ = 1.00). The plaquette contribution is sharply focused on a closed loop encircling each valley, with CK = CK′ = 2, giving a total Chern number C = 4. (b) C = 0 phase (θ = 1.35). The contribution remains localized around the valley contours, but positive and negative parts around each valley cancel, yielding CK = CK′ = 0 and C = 0. (c) C = −4 phase (θ = 1.58). The Berry-curvature weight remains localized near the two valleys but its sign is reversed relative to (a), giving CK = CK′ = −2 and C = −4.</sub>

<sub>FIG. 5. WFC flow, computed via the Wilson loop method, for the singlet gap function ∆s A0(π/4, π/3). **Summary.** The paper theoretically explores unconventional superconductivity in a twisted graphene/NbSe2 heterostructure. By analyzing the superconducting gap functions using symmetry constraints, the authors predict the existence of chiral topological superconducting phases characterized by non-zero Chern numbers. This suggests the material is a promising platform for realizing and detecting exotic quantum states.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work connects concepts from superconductivity, topology, and 2D materials physics. The mechanism of proximity-induced pairing leading to quantized topological states is highly relevant for designing novel quantum electronic devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates proximity-induced unconventional superconductivity and the emergence of chiral topological phases within a twisted graphene/NbSe2 van der Waals heterostructure.

**Main result.** The system exhibits a rich phase diagram characterized by non-zero Chern numbers (e.g., C=4), indicating the stabilization of chiral topological superconducting states.

**Method.** Theoretical calculations utilize the Bogoliubov-de Gennes formalism, employing group theory to construct symmetry-allowed gap functions and calculating topological invariants via Wilson loop methods.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a twisted graphene/NbSe2 van der Waals heterostructure at a specific twist angle (23.4 degrees). The model incorporates orbital hopping, intrinsic SOC, and Rashba SOC effects into the effective Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Chern numbers ($C \in \{-4, -2, 2, 4\}$), $\mathbb{Z}_2$ invariants, superconducting gap functions ($\Delta(\mathbf{k})$), and spin expectation values.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle ($	heta = 23.4^\circ$), mixing parameters ($	heta, \phi_t$), and the proximity-induced gap intensity ($\Delta_0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The superconducting order parameter of NbSe2 is debated; the analysis assumes that heterostructure formation alters competing pairing channels to stabilize chiral components.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the heterostructure geometry, the Chern number phase diagram based on mixing parameters ($	heta, \phi_t$), and local contributions to the Chern numbers ($C_K, C_{K'}$).

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by establishing the effective Hamiltonian using DFT results, constructing symmetry-adapted superconducting gap functions via group theory, and finally calculating topological invariants (Chern number/Z2) across parameter space.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study proximity-induced unconventional superconductivity in a twisted graphene/NbSe$_2$ van der Waals heterostructure using the Bogoliubov-de Gennes formalism. The normal-state parameters of proximitized graphene are extracted from ab initio calculations at a twist angle of $23.4^\circ$, which reduces the common symmetry of the heterostructure to $\mathbf{C}_3$. We construct symmetry-allowed superconducting gap functions of the graphene layer according to the irreducible representations of the $\mathbf{C}_3$ group, containing singlet and triplet pairing channels and their mixtures. Computing the topological invariants as a function of the mixing parameters, we find a rich phase diagram of chiral topological superconducting phases, characterized by nonzero Chern numbers $C\in\{-4,-2,2,4\}$. While the nature of the superconducting order parameter of NbSe$_2$ remains debated, the formation of the van der Waals heterostructure and the related symmetry reduction can alter the relative stability of competing pairing channels, potentially stabilizing a chiral component that is proximity-induced into graphene and triggers the topological phases identified here, making the twisted graphene/NbSe$_2$ heterostructure a promising platform for chiral topological superconductivity detectable via quasiparticle interference imaging and transport measurements.

</details>

### [Unusual half-metallic state in unconventional magnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11384v1)

**Summary.** This paper theoretically explores an unusual half-metallic state found in unconventional magnets with helical magnetic order. It develops tight-binding models for p-wave and f-wave states, showing that these topological features lead to unique spin polarization properties. The work predicts the formation of persistent spin currents when these materials are confined into quantum well structures.

**Why it may be interesting.** While primarily condensed matter magnetism/spintronics, the focus on topological band structures and emergent spin currents in confined geometries touches upon concepts relevant to engineered quantum systems and boundary physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates an unusual half-metallic state where the spin gap depends on the direction of the wave vector, originating from the nontrivial topology of the band structure.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that such p-wave and f-wave half-metals can exist in helimagnets and lead to phenomena like standing waves with helical spin structures and persistent spin currents in quantum wells.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis using two-dimensional tight-binding models, analyzing band structure along specific paths in the magnetic Brillouin zone, and reducing the problem to 1D motion for nanostructures.

**Model / system.** The model uses a general 2D tight-binding Hamiltonian applied to helimagnets exhibiting $120^\circ$ magnetic order. The system is analyzed in both bulk (rectangular/hexagonal lattices) and confined geometries (quantum wells).

**Key observables.** Density of States (DOS), spin polarization, band structure visualization, persistent spin current.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The existence of the half-metallic gap depends on the Fermi level placement relative to topological gaps; $120^\circ$ magnetic ordering is crucial.

**Assumptions / limitations.** For nanostructures, the analysis assumes an infinite potential wall perpendicular to the x-axis. The unusual state requires breaking parity-time symmetry (alternamagnets).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate DOS comparisons between ordinary and unusual half-metals; band structures along X'-$\Gamma$-X paths for $120^\circ$ order on rectangular/hexagonal lattices; and schematics showing the resulting persistent spin current in a quantum well.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the theoretical framework using tight-binding models for p-wave and f-wave half metals in bulk helimagnets. It then focuses on confinement effects, analyzing the resulting 1D physics in quantum wells to predict observable phenomena like persistent spin currents.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Half-metallic ferromagnets exhibit a gap in the density of states for one spin projection while remaining gapless for the opposite spin. We show that in helimagnets an unusual half-metallic state can exist, where the spin projection that experiences the gap is determined by the direction of the wave vector. This state originates from the nontrivial topology of the band structure, specifically from the dispersion forming a multi-sheeted covering over the Brillouin zone. We present two-dimensional tight-binding models for $p$-wave and $f$-wave half metals. These structures can be realized in crystalline and van der Waals systems. The complex band structure of the unusual $p$-wave half metal in nanostructures is also discussed. In a quantum well, a standing wave with a helical spin structure is formed, and a persistent spin current exists.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11434"></a>
### [Extrinsic quantum geometry in the quadrupolar bulk photovoltaic effect](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11434v1)

**Authors:** Steven Gassner, Swati Chaudhary, Martin Claassen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11434v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.11434_figures/2606.11434_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Electric quadrupole corrections to optical responses encode extrinsic quantum geometry. (a) Electrons in Bloch bands couple to a uniform electric field via an interband elec- tric dipole matrix element. Gradients in the electric field cou- ple to an interband quadrupole matrix element. (b) The in- terband electric dipole rµ mnk probes the intrinsic geometry of the Bloch states, i.e. within the resonant two-band mani- fold. (c) The origin-independent part of the interband electric quadrupole, which we denote as eQνµ mnk, probes the extrinsic geometry of the Bloch states, i.e. orthogonal to the resonant two-band manifold. Here P⊥n = 1 −|unk⟩⟨unk| is the projec- tor to the subspace...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.11434_figures/2606.11434_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Shift current at linear order in wavevector for the min- imal three-band model described in the text, in which near k = 0 the upper and lower dispersing bands have a mutual coupling ∼−igk2 and a coupling ∼−i √</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.11434_figures/2606.11434_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Oblique-incidence photocurrents probe the extrin- sic quantum geometry of the moir´e bands in tMoTe2. (a) Schematic of a photocurrent experiment in a twisted bilayer material whereby oblique incidence introduces an in-plane component to the optical wavevector. (b) The yxyy compo- nent of the real linear-in-wavevector photovoltaic conductivity (for which the magnetic dipole contribution is forbidden by symmetry) computed from continuum models of valley-and- spin-polarized tMoTe2 at three different twist angles. The considerably larger response for the twist angle where the top few bands have equal Chern number can be anticipated from the viewpoint of extrinsic quantum geometry.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work develops a framework to calculate the bulk photovoltaic effect using an expansion sensitive to higher-order electric quadrupole contributions. By analyzing these 'extrinsic' geometric terms, the authors predict that materials with complex band mixing, such as twisted bilayer semiconductors, will exhibit enhanced photocurrents measurable via photon drag effects.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit connection drawn between band-geometric quantities (like extrinsic quadrupole moments) and measurable electromagnetic multipole corrections in nonlinear optics provides a powerful new diagnostic tool for characterizing electronic structure beyond simple dipole responses.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work investigates the role of 'extrinsic quantum geometry' in the quadrupolar bulk photovoltaic effect (BPVE), extending beyond standard dipole approximations.

**Main result.** The authors predict that systems with strong multi-band mixing, such as twisted MoTe2 bilayers, will exhibit anomalously large photon drag due to this higher-order quadrupolar effect.

**Method.** The analysis uses diagrammatic perturbation theory and expands the second-order optical conductivity ($\sigma_{\mu\alphaeta}^{PV}$) to linear order in the light wavevector ($\mathbf{q}$), separating contributions into multipolar terms.

**Model / system.** The study is based on continuum models of electronic band structures, particularly applied to twisted bilayer materials like MoTe2. The theoretical framework involves Bloch Hamiltonians and calculating current operator matrix elements.

**Key observables.** Photovoltaic conductivity ($\sigma_{\mu\alphaeta}^{PV}$), which is proportional to the electric quadrupole moment ($eQ$) when expanded in $\mathbf{q}$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis focuses on the linear-in-$\mathbf{q}$ correction, comparing results across different multipolar contributions (dipole vs. quadrupole).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key assumptions include expanding responses linearly in $\mathbf{q}$ and utilizing approximations like the noninteracting electron picture within diagrammatic calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the coupling of interband dipole versus quadrupole moments to light-matter interaction, and show calculated shift currents for minimal three-band models and twisted MoTe2.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from general theoretical derivations of $\sigma_{\mu\alphaeta}^{PV}$ using diagrammatic techniques (Diagram 1 & 2), systematically derives the linear-$\mathbf{q}$ expansion, and applies this formalism to specific physical systems like twisted bilayer MoTe2.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The bulk photovoltaic effect has become a valuable probe of the quantum geometry of Bloch bands. While it is restricted to inversion-broken systems within the dipole approximation, the finite momentum of light is appreciated to give rise to this effect even in centrosymmetric crystals, an effect referred to as "photon drag." In this work, we recast the photon drag effect at leading order in the optical wavevector, highlighting a previously neglected contribution arising intuitively from the electric quadrupole correction to light-matter coupling. In the language of band geometry, we identify this interband quadrupole as a multiband metric tensor that quantifies the variation of two resonantly driven states extrinsic to the subspace they span. We predict that systems in which three or more bands strongly admix in momentum space, such as twisted MoTe$_2$ bilayers, will have anomalously large photon drag due to this quadrupolar effect. Our work provides a conceptual bridge between band-geometric organizing principles and electromagnetic multipole corrections in nonlinear optics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.11752"></a>
### [Spin-Orbit Torque and Magnetization Switching in 2D Ferromagnetic Devices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11752v1)

**Authors:** Bao-Huei Huang, Hong Guo, Yu-Hui Tang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11752v1>

**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

(d) Both the spin current injection and local spin induction contribute to a current-induced spin density 𝝈∥x when a charge current density Jc ∥y is applied, initiating CISOT acting on the FM magnetization in the fieldlike (𝑇FL) and dampinglike (𝑇DL) directions. <img src="2606.11752_figures/2606.11752_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3, the band structure exhibits inversion symmetry along 𝑘𝑥for arbitrary orientations of M. This symmetry implies that an 𝑥-directed current can generate an additional 𝑧-polarized spin accumulation. In such cases, the system would generate two distinct current-induced spin-orbit fields, one along ˆy and another along ˆz, both contributing to the CISOT depending on the current direction. In this work, we restrict our analy- sis to the simpler geometry where the current flows along 𝑦, corresponding to the conventional Rashba-Edelstein scenario. **Summary.** This work theoretically analyzes how spin-orbit torque drives magnetization switching in 2D ferromagnetic heterostructures. By decomposing the total torque into fieldlike and dampinglike components, it reveals that different physical mechanisms—local spin induction versus spin current injection—dominate switching depending on whether the material favors in-plane or perpendicular magnetism. This guides optimization for energy-efficient spintronic memory design.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed quantum mechanical decomposition of torques into local induction versus injected current provides a highly granular understanding of spin-charge conversion, which is crucial for designing next-generation spintronic devices that interact with quantum phenomena.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the mechanisms governing current-induced spin-orbit torque (SOT) for magnetization switching in 2D ferromagnetic devices.

**Main result.** The paper distinguishes between Fieldlike Torque (FLT), dominated by local spin induction (Rashba-Edelstein effect in FM layers), and Dampinglike Torque (DLT), associated with spin current injection, showing their distinct roles depending on the magnetic anisotropy regime (IMA vs. PMA).

**Method.** The research employs first-principles quantum transport calculations using DFT/NEGF methods combined with macrospin simulations solving the LLG equation.

**Model / system.** The model uses a $	ext{Cr}_3	ext{Te}_4/	ext{PtTe}_2$ van der Waals heterobilayer, analyzing spin dynamics governed by terms including spin current injection ($\Phi$) and local spin induction ($	ext{TSO}_{	ext{FM}}$).

**Key observables.** Spin current injection ($\Phi$), local spin induction ($	ext{TSO}_{	ext{FM}}$), Fieldlike Torque ($T_{	ext{FL}}$), Dampinglike Torque ($T_{	ext{DL}}$), and the critical switching current density ($J_{sw}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strong in-plane magnetic anisotropy (IMA) vs. perpendicular magnetic anisotropy (PMA); torque efficiencies ($\eta$); damping parameter ($\alpha$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Calculations are performed assuming a clean limit and often utilize approximations like the small angle approximation for magnetization dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate spin textures, band structures showing Rashba splitting due to SOC, and $M-J_c$ loops demonstrating switching behavior under different anisotropy conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the theoretical framework of CISOT components (FLT vs. DLT), derives the necessary equations based on material properties (e.g., SHE/REE effects), and applies these models to predict switching currents for specific magnetic anisotropy regimes in the vdW heterostructure.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Current-induced spin-orbit torque has emerged as a powerful technique for manipulating magnetization switching of ferromagnet/nonmagnet (FM/NM) based memory cell. By investigating nonequilibrium spin torque effect in a van der Waals heterobilayer, trigonal $\text{Cr}_{3}\text{Te}_{4}/\text{PtTe}_{2}$, the first-principles quantum transport calculations are applied to determine both local spin induction, resulting from Rashba-Edelstein effect in the FM layer, and spin current injection, flowing from the NM to the FM layer. Our work reveals that local spin induction significantly generates the fieldlike torque, which primarily governs the switching current in systems with strong in-plane magnetic anisotropy. Our work emphasizes the importance of optimizing spin Hall effect in the NM layer for perpendicular magnetic anisotropy (PMA)-based magnetization switching and maximizing the Rashba effect in the FM layer for in-plane magnetic anisotropy (IMA)-based switching.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Structural Changes and Transport Properties of $\mathrm{YBa_2Cu_3O_7}$ Locally Modified by a He$^+$ Focused Ion Beam](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12145v1)

**Authors:** Ross Carter, Robin Hutt, Paul Zimmermann, Ainur Abukaev, Jan Ullmann, Simon Koch, Christoph Schmid, Manfred Burghammer, Reinhold Kleiner, Dieter Koelle, Edward Goldobin, Ivan A. Zaluzhnyy

**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work uses a He+ Focused Ion Beam to locally damage YBa2Cu3O7 superconducting films. By correlating changes in lattice structure (measured by XRD) with electrical transport measurements, the authors demonstrate that irradiation induces structural disorder and drives the material toward an insulating state. This technique enables the fabrication of tunable superconducting nano-resistors.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on cuprate superconductivity, the controlled introduction of disorder and strain via ion beams provides an experimental platform to study how local structural perturbations affect high-$T_c$ pairing mechanisms, relevant to strongly correlated electron physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To investigate how localized structural modifications, induced by focused ion beam irradiation, affect the superconducting properties of YBa2Cu3O7 thin films.

**Main result.** Irradiation causes lattice expansion and a decrease in critical temperature ($T_c$), eventually leading to an insulating state. The technique allows for creating tunable superconducting nano-devices with size-dependent transport properties.

**Method.** The study combines nanofocused X-ray diffraction (XRD) to measure structural changes (lattice parameters $a$ and $c$) with low-temperature electrical resistivity measurements on patterned microbridges.

**Model / system.** The physical system is epitaxial YBa2Cu3O7 thin films grown on LSAT substrates. The modification is achieved by bombarding the surface with 30 keV He+ ions, creating controlled irradiated stripes of varying dose and size.

**Key observables.** Critical temperature ($T_c$), lattice parameters ($\delta a$, $\delta c$), sheet resistance ($R_\square$), and film height change ($\Delta z$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Irradiation Dose ($D$) in the range $10–100 	ext{ ions}/	ext{nm}^2$; irradiated area size ($L$); temperature ($T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis models structural changes and transport behavior based on localized damage accumulation from ion bombardment, linking lattice strain to electronic degradation.

**Figures summary.** Figures show XRD intensity profiles across pristine, transition, and fully irradiated regions; plots illustrate the dose dependence of lattice parameters ($\delta a$, $\delta c$) and sheet resistance ($R_\square$) as a function of stripe size $L$.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically presents structural characterization (XRD/AFM) showing lattice expansion and bending, followed by electrical transport measurements demonstrating the superconductor-insulator transition correlated with irradiation dose.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Irradiation of a material with ions can cause various defects that can lead to structural phase transitions and the modification of the material's properties. Here we study the irradiation of the epitaxyally grown thin films of the high-temperature superconductor $\mathrm{YBa_2Cu_3O_7}$ with $30\,\mathrm{keV}$ He$^{+}$ ions which leads to the expansion of the crystal lattice, decrease of the critical temperature $T_c$ and eventually transition to an insulator. Fabrication of such insulating regions with a focused He-Ion beam with a spot size of $\sim 10\,\mathrm{nm}$ is a powerful technique for fabrication of superconducting nano-devices. Using low-temperature resistivity measurements, diffraction with a nanofocused X-ray beam and atomic force microscopy, we investigated how the structure and the electric transport properties of $\mathrm{YBa_2Cu_3O_7}$ depend on the irradiation dose in a range $10$--$100\,\mathrm{ions/nm^2}$ and on the lateral size of the irradiated area in a range $30$--$5000\,\mathrm{nm}$.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11721"></a>
### [Ambient and Pressure Dependent Superconductivity with Hydrogen Storage Potential in Quaternary Hydride LiMgZr2H12: A Comprehensive First-principles Insights](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11721v1)

**Authors:** Jubair Hossan Abir, Tauhidur Rahman, Salauddin Muhammad Anis, Saleh Hasan Naqib, Raihana Shams Islam  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11721v1>

<sub>Figure 5. (a) Elastic constants (b) Calculated elastic moduli (c) Calculated Poisson’s ratio and Pugh’s ratio (d) Calculated machinability index under 0, 2, 4, 6, 8, 10 GPa.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study investigates $	ext{LiMgZr}_2	ext{H}_{12}$ as a promising ambient-pressure superconductor and hydrogen storage material. Using first-principles DFT, the authors confirm its structural stability, calculate a measurable $T_c$ of $72.8 	ext{ K}$, and demonstrate that mechanical properties remain favorable under pressure. The work establishes a new computational pathway for designing high-performance energy materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the core physics is solid-state superconductivity, the detailed calculation of lattice dynamics, phonon softening/hardening under pressure, and the coupling between electronic structure and mechanical stability provides rich data relevant to understanding quantum materials behavior far from equilibrium.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to identify superconducting materials that maintain high critical temperatures ($T_c$) while remaining stable under ambient pressure, overcoming the limitations of current superhydride candidates.

**Main result.** The quaternary hydride $	ext{LiMgZr}_2	ext{H}_{12}$ was shown to be thermodynamically and mechanically stable at ambient conditions, exhibiting a $T_c$ of $72.8 	ext{ K}$ (increasing to $77.3 	ext{ K}$ under pressure) and possessing potential for hydrogen storage.

**Method.** The study relies entirely on first-principles calculations using Density Functional Theory (DFT) within the VASP package, analyzing electronic structure, elastic constants, phonon dynamics, and electron-phonon coupling ($\lambda$).

**Model / system.** The primary system is the quaternary hydride $	ext{LiMgZr}_2	ext{H}_{12}$, which is modeled as a stable crystal structure with an orthorhombic symmetry. The analysis systematically varies external pressure (0 to 10 GPa) and temperature.

**Key observables.** Critical Temperature ($T_c$), Electron-Phonon Coupling Constant ($\lambda$), Hydrogen Storage Capacity, Elastic Moduli (Bulk, Shear, Young's), Debye Temperature ($	heta_D$), and Optical Conductivity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ambient pressure (0 GPa) vs. high pressure (10 GPa); $	ext{Li}$ doping effect; $T_c$ enhancement via phonon hardening.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculations assume the validity of DFT approximations (GGA-PBE) and that the material's properties can be extrapolated across the studied pressure range. The superconducting analysis uses the Allen-Dynes-modified McMillan formula.

**Figures summary.** Figures summarize the evolution of $	heta_D$ and $T_m$ with pressure, show energy-dependent optical properties (reflectivity, conductivity) for different polarizations, and plot calculated mechanical parameters like hardness vs. pressure.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a systematic computational investigation: first establishing structural stability and basic electronic bonding; then characterizing mechanical robustness (elasticity, machinability); followed by thermal/optical analysis ($	heta_D$, $T_m$, $\kappa_{Ph}$); and finally, calculating superconducting properties ($T_c$ via EPC).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Molecular hydrides have attracted relatively less attention in the search for high Tc superconductors because their hydrogen quasi-molecular units tend to be electronically inactive for superconductivity. In contrast, hydrogen rich compounds under high pressure have been widely considered strong candidates for achieving room-temperature superconductivity. However, their dependence on extreme pressure conditions significantly constrains their practical applicability. This work investigates hydrogen-rich superconducting materials that may be stable under ambient pressure conditions. Motivated by recent studies on the MgZrH2n family, a LiMgZr2H12 structure with Pmmm symmetry was designed. The mechanical, thermodynamic, and dynamical stability of the compound, together with its electronic and optical properties, were systematically investigated using first-principles calculations. Li doping in LiMgZr2H12 significantly increases the hydrogen derived contribution near the Fermi level (EF) and strengthens the electron-phonon coupling constant (λ) compared with MgZrH6. LiMgZr2H12 exhibits a critical temperature of 72.76 K at ambient pressure, which is further enhanced by applying pressure. At 10 GPa the critical temperature increases to 77.3 K. Elastic property analysis shows that the material remains mechanically stable over the pressure range studied (0-10 GPa). It also behaves like a ductile material suitable for current carrying applications. The material has a high machinability index, which is much higher than that of stainless steels. In addition, LiMgZr2H12 exhibits a gravimetric hydrogen storage capacity of 5.36 wt%, indicating its potential as a promising candidate for hybrid hydrogen storage technologies. This work offers a new direction for designing high-Tc hydrides at ambient conditions.

</details>
